THE RED PAGE.
James Brunton Stephens.
11.
FOR twenty-one years, among the crowds
from the southern suburb that trooped
daily across Victoria bridge, Brisbane,
James Brunton Stephens was a familiar
figure. He used to wear a loose, old-fashioned,
dark walking coat, and an old-fashioned hard
black hat. Of medium height, and rather thin,
he walked with a stick, and he wore dark glasses
on account of the weakness of his eyes. He
carried his head with dignity. His chin and
throat were shaven ; and in later days his hair
and beard were sparse and silvery white. If
faces are any indication of the mind, then
Stephens’s face indicated the cultured mind
behind it. It was the face of a poet.
In spite of official obligations and family dis-
tractions, Stephens managed to do a great deal of
literary work. For the last thirty years of his
life, and until his official work became engross-
ing, he wrote for the newspapers topical notes,
short stories, and verses light and serious. Most
of these writings, possibly, possessed only a
passing interest, but the saving grace of style is
with them all. His literary craftsmanship
was also to be traced in the more important
State documents that of late years appeared
above the signatures of Queensland Premiers ;
and politicians heard, at the opening of Parlia-
ments, Stephens’s language issuing from the
mouth of the Governor.
“ The longer I live,” he has written, “and the
more poetry I read, the more does my apprecia-
tion of absolute accuracy of expression increase.”
Like his ancestor, Buchanan, Stephens was a
stickler for the precise word—the exact phrase.
Often he would lie awake at night fighting over
a troublesome line of poetry, unable to sleep until
he had mastered it.
The- burden of Stephens’s official work lay
heavily upon his shoulders towards the end.
Federation and the South African war, into which
Queensland had been hustled, meant for him, as
the mouthpiece of the Premier, much additional
work. There were days when, to use his own
expression, “his brains were like batter-pud-
ding.” He had not the faculty of being able
to write to order. He was particularly sensitive
to noise, and could only concentrate his thoughts
in isolation. He envied the writer who could
fling himself into his work, oblivious of his sur-
roundings.
Stephens’s official work undoubtedly interfered
with the maturing of those sub-conscious pro-
cesses that with him preceded literary production.
He held that to do good work one needed leisure
for rumination. Impressions or ideas, stored up
in his brain, grouped or classified themselves sub-
consciously, and, gradually taking form, quickened
for literary birth in due season. Meditation
aided the process ; distraction and pre-occupation
delayed it. The artist who could devote himself
entirely to an art was to be envied, he thought.
He did not believe in artistic coddling, however—
in sitting down and waiting for moods. Without
doubt, there were times when a man could write
better than at others. To some extent moods could
be cultivated, but the man who set himself to
find moods—to drag them out of their lairs, so to
speak—had a considerable advantage over the
man who preferred to sit down and wait for his
moods to manifest themselves.
Stephens wrote but one leading article for a
newspaper. His sipgle novel is practically out of
print. A comic opera entitled Fayette, libretto by
Stephens and music by G. B. Allen (who had
conducted Gilbert and Sullivan’s first opera), was
taken to London by an influential friend, but
failed to find acceptance ; though selections ren-
dered publicly in Brisbane had much praise from
local musical lovers and critics. Two other comic
operas, taken at different times to London, met
no better reception.
On the whole Stephens made very little money
out of his writings. He did not lack business
instinct, for he was a careful man in money
matters; but he hated advertisement, and he had
always a curious lack of faith in the value of his
literary productions. When Henry Lawson once
complained in print that he had not made A7OO
out of all his writings, Stephens laconically
remarked, “ If I had made anything like that out
of mine, I’d have been satisfied.”
His head was always full of literary projects.
In one of his Tamrookum letters (1870) to Earle,
written in the light bantering vein that charac-
terises most of his letters to close friends, he
says:—
Will you get me a book on the Sandwich Islands ? I
want the S.I. for the base of the thing. I’ll tell you all
about it when I get the thing clearer in my mind. It’s
the scenery chiefly I want, especially, the. volcano of
Kilauea. The basis of the pome will be historical, but the
most of it will be reflections, like. The title is clear
enough, so I won’t withhold it—
KAPIOLANI.
Ain’t that a bee-youtiful name ? None of yer Englified
pronunciation for it tho’. None of yer Oxford Kappy-o-lay-
nigh, but a fine broad Scotch Ka,pio-lah-nee. Four
syllables, mind, Kap-yo-lah-nee.
Q. Who was Kapiolani ?
A. A queen of the Sandwidge Islands.
Q. What can you tell me of Kapiolani?
A. She embraced Christianity and overthrew the idola-
trous worship of Pete.
Q. How did she go for to do it ?
A. She announced to her subjects that she would go up
to the top of the volcano of Kilauea, the supposed
stronghold of Pet 4, and throw her slipper down the
crater, defying the goddess to avenge the insult.
Q. And did she do it ?
A, Guess she did.
Q. And did Pet 6 avenge the insult ?
A Not yet.
Q. How do you know ?
A. You Te another.
It is to be written in a totally new style of versification
as thus
Kapiolani swore she’d dip her
Slipper
In the crater,
And her subjects swore to await her
At the foot of it,
Not a doot of it,
One with her stocking and another with her gaiter —
And the whole transaction took place about 20° north
of the Equator.
It is called the crescendo-diminuendo metre. lam
sure Jo [Clarke] will admire it—it is so pittoresque even
in the outline.
But, seriously, I want a book about the Sandwich
Islands—not too dear, you know. The subject is, I
venture to say, utterly virgin and untouched, and there
is a whole poem in the very idea.
It was Stephens’s custom to read all the avail-
able literature dealing vyitli the subject upon
which he intended to write. He was particularly
careful of his natural history and geography, and
generally went to great trouble in verifying his
references. He was an excellent letter-writer,
and delighted in a congenial correspondence.
In his letters to his few intimate friends the
nature of the man is disclosed, sometimes
exuberant with rollicking humour, full of the
zest of life; sometimes sad and melancholy when
the pendulum had swung to the other extreme.
On Saturday evenings, when the worries of
office were not heavy, the friend who called at
Stephens’s home in Water-street, South Brisbane,
saw him at his best. These friends were few, because
he was not an easy man to get in touch with. He
strained at uncongenial companionship; in con*
genial company he unfolded himself slowly, and
then only when sure of his ground. Here is a
characteristic invitation to a Saturday evening
symposium:—
Dear . It is an age since we have seen you. Why
is that thus and not otherwise? Can you come to us to-
morrow evening ?
Menu for the evening: Chatter and Bulimba, with
entr6e of Capstan medium. Write and say whether
Yours faithfully,
J. B. Stephens.
He loved “a yarn,” as he called it—an uncon-
ventional chat about men, and books, and things.
In the roof of his house at Wator-street he had an
attic built to which he retired from the bosom of
his family to smoke, and write, and read, and
meditate. There the privileged visitor saw him
as he really was—simple, natural, modest, and un-
restrained. His voice was musical, clear, courtly,
and cultured. There was no trace of egotism
about him. He did not monopolise the conversa-
tion, and spoke about himself and his writings
seldom, with great diffidence. He read widely in
poetry, fiction, history, science, and metaphysics.
Italian and French literature he absorbed in the
original. Shakespeare, Dante, Tennyson, Ros-
setti and Swinburne ranked highest in his estima-
tion of the poets. He described Swinburne as
“poison ” to young poets, who found it difficult to
resist imitating his mannerisms. Those who
know Swinburne’s “Hesperia” will recognise
that “Convict Once” is written in a similar
metre. Of Rossetti he wrote to a young writer :
‘ ‘ Now, there is a man who can meet all the
modern symbolists or other ists on their own
ground and lick them. Which of them can bring
such treasures from the Infinite as he ? But take
his “ House of Life.” The sublimation of thought
in it is not more remarkable than the incontest-
able accuracy of expression, the definition, the
preciseness of each wonderful phrase. Others
grope in the Infinite. Rossetti knows his way in/
it.”
Of Australian poetry he had a due appreciation,
but its “horseyness” and its pessimism did not
appeal to him. “It lacks a fundamental basis of
brain,” was his verdict. He hated the Bush.
“ Soul cannot march to the bleating of sheep and
the lowing of cattle,” he wrote in “Convict
Once.” The element of local colour in any verse
mattered little to him ; the essential thing was the
broader emotion common to humanity.
<3*
Stephens made a point of keeping abreast of the
times in his reading, and in developing what he
called “the modern side of his mind.” He read
the American novelists with zest, and had a keen
appreciation of the American short story.
“There is something fresh about it to me,” he
would say. But realism in literature was his
Mte noire. “The coarseness ”of Zola offended his
literary susceptibilities. The portrayal of scenes
and actions such as are depicted in Zola’s writings,
he averred, is in itself a stimulant to crime. And
he wrote —
lam at war with pessimism as a literary cult. To me
no grind is so weak and monotonous as the weary, mono-
tonous grind of pessimism. Things may be rotten, but
the poetry of rottenness is the rottenest of them all. The
cry to genius is to create—make something, not weep
and wail. Pessimism (I mean literary pessimism), in my
opinion, is weakness of mind—decay of originality, and
most of its literary devices merely disguise the want of
positive ideas. ..lam constitutionally pessimistic. I have
been there, and am still apt to go back. But that would
not justify me in making a cult of pessimism, any more
than my being a leper would justify me in disseminating
a nn«cr"y of leprosy.
And—
uost of the rising poets seem to me to be occupied in
describing their point of view.. . The devil take their point
of view ! What I want to know is what they see from it.
In politics, Stephens was a conservative, but
his conservatism was tempered by a sympathetic
nature. He was appalled at the amount of
misery and suffering that existed in the world ;
he was astonished at the patience of the oppressed
masses; but he was not to be convinced that it
was possible for State effort to remedy their
grievance. “I have experienced penury myself,”
he has said; “I have known the time when I
sat in the gardens near the Brisbane Observa-
tory and wondered where I was going to get
my next meal from. lam not Jacking in sym-
pathy for the poor and the outcast, but I do not
hold with the wrong ideas of the Labour party.”
The subject of Socialism was a tabooed one at
Water-street. “No doubt it is the greatest
question of the day,” he has written, “but I
confess I am getting a little tired of book
Socialism as material for conversation. If you
can tell me the actual steps—not the mere prin-
ciples of the bill—that are going to be taken in
consequence of it all, and what are likely to be
the proximate results to ourselves and our chil-
dren—no sweet-by-and-by business—l shall be
much obliged, but in the meantime I am going to
take a rest from first principles.”
Stephens prided himself upon keeping his mind
open for the reception of new truths, and upon
his toleration. And on all other subjects
than Socialism he was tolerant. An intellectual
aristocrat, he had a groundless dread of the
possibility of a hobnailed invasion. Democracy,
in a vague sort ot way, meant a levelling down
to the status of the man hanging about the
street corner waiting for someone to come
along and buy his labour. It meant that the
vestal in the sacred temple of Art would be a
fellow with bowyangs ! . , ,
In the orthodox sense, Stephens was without
religion. Christ was to him the moral ideal. But
Christ’s divinity was quite another matter.
Stephens’s mental attitude towards that great
personification of all the virtues was the mental
attitude of Renan. His nature was too sympa-
thetic to remain bound by the hard, cold, cruel
Calvinism with which his young mind had been
saturated. His mind was too broad to be confined
by any particular sect or creed. “But if ever I
should turn to any particular religion, lie has
said, “I think it would be Roman Catholicism.
The’self-sacrifice of its devotees impressed one to
whom true religion was manifest in deeds, not
words. And the antiquity of Roman Catholic tradi-
tions appealed to him. , . ..
“Have you read Lafcadio’s Hearns Kokorol
he asked a friend a week before his death. “ Ihere
is a chapter headed " The Idea of Pre-existence
in it. I was just working towards that.” In the
particular ohapter mentioned the Oriental idea of
the soul is outlined. It is not “the single,
tenuous, tremulous, transparent, inner man of
the Western peoples, not the ghostly supernatural
visitant or guest that links man to the Great Un-
known. To the Buddhistic mind the soul is not
individual, but multiple. “It is the inherited
memory of the absolutely inconceivable multitude
of experiences received by all the brains of which
it is the descendant.’’ The individual soul is
likened to a composite photograph. “The
struggle between impulses good and evil is but a
conflict between the various ghostly wills that
make up the Ego. And a person’s spiritual hope is
to disengage his better self or selves from his
worso selves ; Nirvana, or the Supreme bliss,
being attainable only through the survival of the
best within him.” “Through all my ups and
downs,” Stephens has said, “I have never lost
that inherent something that set me striving to
live up to the best that was in me.” He was con-
vinced that true happiness consisted in right-doing,
and that “that which is right is that which is best
not only for the individual, but for all.” At
times a peculiar dread of the possibility of a
future life would possess him, but normally he
was without prejudice in respect to any after
state.
J*
Stephens had an ideal nature in many respects.
In the combination of ancestors that epitomised
in him the female element predominated. He
had none of the distinctive virile qualities of
his sex. Books and his pen were his hobbies.
He was a student from his boyhood upwards.
His office, his family, his study, the School of
Arts, an occasional literary gathering, and some-
times a visit to the theatre, made up the routine
of his days during the last two decades. He
shrank from prominence, and he had a holy
horror, of being lionised. At literary gatherings,
people over whom he stood head and
intellectually, he would retire into out-
of-the-way corners. An inordinately modest man,
he could never bring himself to the assurance that
his writings deserved the success they had
achieved. At the height of his literary fame,
when a common correspondence clerkship in the
Colonial Secretary’s Department was offered to
him, he fretted in fear that he might,not be com-
petent to do the work.
He was, indeed, exceedingly sensitive. Any
extraordinary event, such as a new appointment
or a birth in his family, unbalanced him, often
for days together. When a person was to be
hung at the gaol, half-a-niile away from his house,
he could not sleep for several nights previous to
the execution. In fancy he would hear the
hammering of the mien erecting the gallows. He
was intensely sympathetic. He could not refuse
a request for charity. As he saw the outcast and
the mendicant, so he pictured himself as becoming.
Did one of his daughters have occasion to
go to the dentist’s, he went to his office and was
miserable all day.
An absolutely conscientious man in all his
undertakings! As a Bush tutor, be preferred
to resign his position rather than brook parental
interference with what he conceived to be his duty
towards his charges. As perhaps the most promin-
ent literary personality in Australia, numbers of
young writers sent him MSS, and besought his
advice. Though he hated to act as a literary
judge, often under the necessity of passing
adverse judgment, still he felt it incumbent upon
him to undertake such work, and he carried
it through conscientiously, frequently at great
personal inconvenience. “He was an excellent
friend,” says one who had known him intimately
for thirty years, with _ moistened eyes. “He
never let anyone in in his life, he never borrowed
money. When he hadn’t it he went without it.
His attachments were eternal. He was as tender-
hearted as a woman. He had the soul of an
angel, had Jimmy Stephens.”

MARGINALIA
M a mie a un petit rien,
Si joli, si doux, si fin ;
Et ce cher petit rien
Pour moi c’est l’Eden.
Pavfois me mets en Ute
Que seul lui appartient:
Ont le rn£me toutes fillettes,
Un joli, joli petit rien..
L’une le livre d tons,
L’autre le garde d soi ;
Pourtant mon bijou
Ne le donne qu’d moi.
Lorsqu’avec extase
Elle me fixe tendrement,
Vile je Venlace,
Sa main cache le talisman.
Et ce cher petit rien
Auquel personne ne touche,
Vous le concevez bien,

EMILE STEADS.
O women! what did you do with the sweet
depths of your eyes—with the intoxication of vour
dreaming- lips or with their swelling, voluptuous
ardour—with your dimples—with your little curis—with
the baudelairian swing of your hips—with your wilful
f orce — w ith your audacious charm—when you became
painters and sculptors?— Adolphe Dervaux, commisera-
ting the (Paris) exhibition of the Union of Woman
Painters and Sculptors.
As Gelett Burgess writes—
Not the quarry, hut the chase,
Not the laurel, but the race,
Not the hazard, but the play,
Make me, Lord, enjoy alway.
MEMORANDA
Wagner’s literary and musical copyrights are
worth to his heirs about .£30,000 a year, without counting
Bayreuth. Last year Lohengrin was given 997 times in
the German countries, 420 times in Latin countries, and
318 times in Britain and the U.S.A. Tannhauser, in all
countries, was performed upwards of 500 times ; and the
author’s royalties came to about £l5 per representation.
Barron Field’s First Fruits of Australian
Poetry brought £8 at a London sale in June. For the
price of late fruits from rich fields, see advertisements.
Eloquent statistics ! In Paris Salons this year
there were catalogued by the Independents, 2462 works
of art from 350 painters, sculptors, etc. ; by the Artistes
franqaia, 2755 works from 900 authors; and by the
Soci6t6 nationale, 4757 works from 3000 authors making
a total of 4250 artists and 9974 works of art! So
that the adventurous Australian who gets One In may
seem scarcely to earn his cable.
“ Mark Twain” has announced his intention to
leave America and make his home if) Italy.
The International Encyclopcedia of Journalism.,
“an authoritative history and comprehensive hand-
book,” is being compiled in London.
Jumbuck : You propose restricting competition
in the Herald chess problem tourneys to Australian com-
posers only. I am an Australian composer whose
problems have appeared in every illustrated Australian
weekly, and elsewhere. So far from deprecating the
world's competition, I wish for it. The £5 ss. is nothing
in my eyes : the glory is everything. It would be an
honour to come out first against the world’s best com-
posers ; whereas a first against local composers, however
good the winning problem, would have little merit in the
eyes of the world at large.
But one could do chess a real service by securing
anonymity. Suppose the judge never sees the names of
the composers, do you think he does not know ? Do you
think “Hop’s” cartoons could remain anonymous,
wherever appearing ? A chess-man who had seen even one
of my problems would know my compositions by the very
colour of the inks employed ; by the diagramming ; by
the handwriting in the solution sent with the problem.
It seems to me evident that Glynn, though a conscien-
tious and honourable judge, was “ gallied ” into award-
ing a place to a problem by Max Feigl—a problem
utterly unworthy of that fine composer. Could the
judge, recognising Feigl’s Work, dare to pass it over ?
especially in face of Lond. Times critique on the last
prize-winners. .
So with the problems by Charlick, and “ Sans Peine.
Let the competitive problems with their solutions be
copied or printed off in the Herald office all in one style,
before forwarding to the judge. This suggestion in
kindness to the judge himself, and in fairness to Aus-
tralian composers.
Some of Whistler’s “ Propositions
A picture is finished when all trace of the means used
to bring about the end has disappeared. .
To say of a picture, as is often said in its praise, that it
shows “great and earnest labour,” is to say that it is in*
complete and unfit for view.
Industry in Art is a necessity, not a virtue ; and any
evidence of the same in the production is a blemish, not
a quality : a proof, not of achievement, but of absolutely
insufficient work, for work alone will efface the footsteps
of work. , ~ ' , ~
The work of the Master reeks not of the sweat of the
brow, suggests no effort, and is finished from its be-
ginning. , .
The “ completed task ” of perseverance only has never
been begun, and will remain unfinished to eternity—a
monument of goodwill and foolishness.
“ There is one that laboureth, and taketh pains, and
maketh haste—and is so much the more behind.”
The masterpiece should appear as the flower to the
painter, perfect in its bud as in its bloom, with no reason
to explain its presence, no mission to fulfil—a joy to the
artist, a delusion to the philanthropist, a puzzle to the
botanist, an accident of sentiment and alliteration to the
literary man.
Another Whistler story. To a painter who
complained that there was “a conspiracy of silence
against him, and asked for advice, Whistler gently re-
sponded, “Join it! 1
Another. He was painting the portrait of an
American lady, but could not please himself (her
pleasure was of no importance). After twenty sittings
he gave a “Chut!” of disgust, and destroyed the
canvas before her eyes. Much chagrined, the
asked what was to recompense her for the time she had
wasted. “ Madam !—all these days you the
society of ” —bowing gracefully—“ Whistler !"
A Sydney firm of colourmen addresses its
advertisement to “ Artists and Painters.” The distinc-
tion is appreciable—and necessary.
Eng. Law Times urges that ifi'-is only by an act
of grace that Australia is allowed to make up her own
mind on the preferential tariff question and others. It
we look to the letter of the British Constitution, as dis-
tinguished from the spirit by which its working is
prompted, nothing is clearer than that the legislative
authority possessed by Colonial Legislatures is puisne to
the authority of the Imperial Parliament. By the
Colonial Laws Act, 1865, laws cannot be made by Colonial
legislatures inconsistent with any Act of Parliament
intended by the Imperial Parliament to apply to these
Colonies. It would accordingly be within the com-
petence of the Imperial Parliament to adjust by its own
legislation the commercial relations between Great
Britain and the Colonies.” This is a point which Gov.
Flick-’em-on-the-Rawson and other Of Chamberlain s
Empire-touts might make when there’s question of
getting John See or Deakin to bob up unexpectedly with
a perfectly original and totally unsuggested cable.
George Taylor, a local illustrator whose drawings
have appeared in Punch, complains that some of his
acquaintances have misunderstood the charge of lack of
originality levelled against Pundh. Acquaintances are
often stupid. What obviously was meant was that Punen
dealt in out-worn modes, in tame compromises between
art and public sentimeht, and that one looked m its
pao-es vainly for the new, the virile, 1 the inspired, the
Australians who have drawn for Punch were not accused
of being imitators. No one who has seen George Taylor s
drawings, for example, would ever dream of doubting his
originality. ...
The Bulletin will presently hold an exhibition
and sale of the drawings that Phil May contributed to its
columns. The collection will be not only a series of
brilliant examples of black-and-white art: it will hold a
rare Australian interest. The drawings of eminent Aus-
tralians of the eighties and nineties, with which Mays
work is filled, have unique value. It is Australian
history and biography, as well as pen-drawing in many
respects unsurpassed, that will be shown in the rooms of
Messrs. Walter Bradley and Co.

On Our Selection, written by A. H. Davis
(“ Steele Rudd ”) and published by Tub
Bulletin-Co., has earned the title of “the
jolliest book ever printed in Australia.”
There is laughter on every page; yet the
book remains grimly true to the facts. As
a picture of life on an Australian selection
it has never been surpassed. Since it was
published, little more than two years ago,
upwards of 12,000 copies have been sold at
the comparatively high price of 6s. —making
an Australian publishing record. The reason of the sale is not far to seek : the
people who have bought the book know they have got value for their money;
and they recommend it to others. The price is comparatively high because the book is expensive to
produce. It is well printed, and strongly bound in cloth in good style. There are numerous pictures
by such well-known i lustrators as Mahony, Leist, Lambert, Fischer, Fullwood, and Vincent. From
ON
m®\\
i
cover to cover the book is Australian—the product of Aus-
tralian workmen. If it were produced by cheaper labour
in Eu"land, it would cost less, and could be sold at a
cheaper rate. The publishers prefer to employ Australian
labor only. When you buy On Our Selection you pay T
a little more than you pay for an English book ; but
you get 100 times the Australian value. “ Steele Kudd’s”
book has gone all over Australia, and wherever it has
gone it has become the treasure of a household, often
the delight of a township. People never leave off
reading it until it is thumbed to rags ; and then they
buy another copv. The characters of “ Our Selection
“Old Dad,” “Joe,” “Dave,” “Sarah,” and the rest—
OUR
have become famous throughout the country ; and their imitation by weaker writers proves the force
of their grip on the public mind. Any bookseller will sell you the book ; or if you send a postal
order for 65., with Bd. postage, direct to The Bulletin office, it will be forwarded to any address
by return mail.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Land Ahead!
N.S.W.
To landsmen far from home,
Upon the mam,
The fair, green fields of foam
Are vast and vain.
They count the time with sighs,
They pine and fret,
And see with weary eyes
Suns rise and set.
Then from the topmast high,
When hope seems dead,
They hear the welcome cry.
Of “Land Ahead !”
And though it chance to be
A foreign shore,
Right good it is to see
Greenland once more. '*
So on the ocean lone
Of Life we sail,
Upon a course unknown
With faces pale.
Then from the topmast tall,
We hear a bland
And reverend Look-Out call—
“ The Promised Land ! ”
And some with sight made strong
By faith, behold
A winged and shining throng
With harps of gold.
They stand on that bright shore .
A vision fair
But, ah, for many more
It melts in air.
V. J. DALEY.
Friends.
“As you think best! ” and she turned her head,
As to hide a sudden thought from me.
“As you think best,” she softly said,
As we stood there by the silver sea.
The ships were passing like things of life
Out in the glow of the morning air,
And she whom I would have had to wife
Was like a dream in her beauty there.
I had spoken of how we had agreed
; To be mere friends —to give and take
Solace of either as we had need,
True to each, ever, for either’s sake.
I had told her how, as the months went by,
I had learned to like her more and more ;
Till Love had come with his sovereign cry,
And we couldn’t be what we had been before.
I had laid my heart bare before her, and
Had said it was better it should end ;
I couldn’t now touch her thrilling hand
With the old true touch of a simple friend.
And “As you think best! ” was all she said ;
But there was a something in her tone—
A hint in the quick-averted head . . .
And we were there on the sands alone.
N.S.W.
R. CRAWFORD.
Black Plumes.
She stood dry-eyed as they lifted him—
Dry-eyed and cold, though her heart was wrung;
And out in the hill fog, grey and dim,
The black, black plumes on the horses swung.
She strove to fight with the bearers grim ;
With breaking heart to the bier she clung—
And out in the roadway, grey and dim,
The nodding plumes on the horses swung.
Beauty of face, and of form and limb—
She moaned to God with a voiceless tongue—
As cloaked by the eddying mist-clouds dim
The horses moved and the black plumes swung.
She would give her beauty to speak with him
Whose laugh was a song to her heart low-sung.
But far o’er the hill where the road winds dim
The drivers whispered; the black plumes swung.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
The Early Spring.
There is a oharm in the early Spring,
In the smell o’ the mould when the wind comes
cold,
In the flashing dip of the swallow’s wing,
In the cowslip’s cloth of gold
Spread on the marsh’s rugged edge,
By shallow creek and frost-rimmed sedge.
A charm the mellower season lacks,
When the free creek leaps down rocky steeps,
And Echo answers the woodman’s axe,
When over the spot where t ie buttercup sleeps
The dumb earth feels a strange new thrill,
And the feet of the Spring are on plain and hill.
Ah, to spy when the tender shoots show pink,
A shy bird breast on a quiet nest,
To mark by the glassy river’s brink
Willows in silver and greenery dressed—
Hearts that are Winter-worn wake and sing,
There is a charm in the early Spring,
Gut on the hill when the day is new
Then one may capture the spirit of rapture
Born of the dawn, baptised of the dew.
High on the hill, in the breezes pure,
One may stand, for the moment, a god,
Fully aware of it, and unawed.
N.S.W.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN.

BY ID_A_Y
WE PUBLISH EVIDENCE LIKE THIS*
The Case of Mr. A. LYES.
(for thb “standard.”)
The requirements of the Australian popu-
lace can only be arrived at by reference to
the statistics which are compiled at various
periods, and as evidence is necessary for the
purpose of gauging popular opinion, it may
here be interesting to listen to Mr. Albert
Lyes, of No. 7 Frazer-street, Leichhardt.
.** When living in the North Coast dis-
trict, said Mr. Lyes, “ I experienced a
vast amount of trouble with my liver and
kidneys, the consequence being that I was
put to a lot of inconvenience owing to the
acute pains across the small of my back.
As I had heavy work to do at the time, my
afflictions came as a great hardship, more so
if there was any lifting to be done, when my
back pains were intensified to a degree that
I could scarcely endure.”
Reporter: “ Were you much affected in
any other part ? ”
ii L y es : “ It would be quicker for me
to tell you where I was not affected, for I
had aches and pains all over me. There
was a cold, painful sensation in the spine
which felt as if somebody had shot a ball of
ice along my back, and that horrible shoot-
mg pain extended right to my eyes, which
also gave me the impression of being
swollen. I was sluggish, drowsy, and had
no energy whatever, the only thing I felt
fit for being to lie down—it did not matter
where. It is a serious matter for a married
man to get into that condition, but I held
out p long as I could, and then I had
to give in, for all the grit was taken out
u j'u' ® e * n B without any appetite, which
had been on the wane for a long time, the
chances of stopping a further leakage of my
vitality were very remote, and as I could not
sleep at all well at nights, there was no
doubt that I was in a very grave state. With
my tongue covered with a nasty substance
and a vile taste in my mouth, there is
nothing remarkable in the fact that I could
not enjoy any food, and in the natural
course of events I got a long way thinner
than I ought to have done. I was tor-
mented with very severe headaches which
made me feel so gloomy and wretched that
there seemed to be nothing in the world
worth living for, and with my weakness
daily increasing, my limbs ached so persis-
tently that I could not even take a walk in
comfort. Palpitation of the heart and dizzi-
ness always follow ed any exertion, my
heart being particularly bad. It used to
make a thumping sound like the noUe from
a horse’s hoof heard in the distance, and
many, an anxious hour have I passed in
thinking what might be wrong with that
organ. Like most people who suffer like
this for the first time, I consulted a
doctor, who said that my liver and kidneys
were diseased. He wrote a prescription,
which I had made up, and said I would
have to keep as quiet as possible, and not
exert myself any more than was absolutely
necessary."
Reporter: “ How did you feel after
taking his medicine ? ”
Mr. Lyes : “ Just about the same as
before, and when I saw it was only so much
waste to take any more I dropped it off.
The oppression in my chest and the flatu-
lence in my stomach were still there, and
the urine remained of a cloudy and scanty
nature, so what wa3 the use of throwing
any more money away I thought? Besides,
my nervous system was completely out of
order, as any kind of noise had a startling
effect which kept my nerves at extreme
tension, as if they had been suddenly un-
hinged, and then, again, I felt agitated and
irritable at the slightest provocation. The
long-continuance of these sufferings had
made me very morose and low-spirited, for
there were others to think of besides my-
self; but, thank goodness! that trying
ordeal is all over now, and it is not likely
to be repeated, because I discovered a
remedy which oared my ailments then, and
I am sure it could do so again if ever X
needed it.”
Reporter: '* May I learn what the remedy
was ? ”
Mr. Lyes: “ I have no desire to keep the
matter secret, because I might do other
poor sufferers a good turn by letting you
know. It was Clements Tonic—a medicine
that I had been reading about—that made
a new man of me, and I always regard it as
my only friend amongst all medicines. I
had only taken a couple of bottles of
Clements Tonic when I felt ever so much
better. In fact, the change was so great
that I could scarcely believe it myself,
especially as I had lost confidence in
physics generally. But Clements Tonic
asserted itself as something uncommonly
good by rapidly giving me a desire for food,
and in the matter of making me sleep wef
1^ thought it a most marvellous medicine.
The health-giving properties of Clements
lonic were not confined to those improve-
ments, as it amended the defects in my
internal organs which had gone against the
digestion of my food, and by those means
the pains in my chest and shoulders were
relieved. The fermentation of my food
was done away with in like manner, so that
I was getting the full benefit of my meals,
and how well I knew it ! for the return of
my strength was quite noticeable. I no
longer desired to lie about in a state of
careless languor, for Clements Tonic gave
me the energy and ambition to do some
work, which was becoming easier to per-
form each day, because the pains in my
back were less troublesome as time moved
on. My heart gave me no occasion to
worry myself, as it was beating with a
regular pulsation, and with the departure
of my headaches my sight became so clear
that I could sit down and read for hours
without the least inconvenience. I was
greatly pleased with Clements Tonic for
the effect it had on my nervous system,
for it invigorated my nerves so thoroughly
that they became perfectly steady, while
such a thing as becoming excited or annoyed
over trifling incidents did not happen. A
good indication to go by as to the efficacy of
Clements lonic was the entire removal of
my deranged kidney symptoms, and it was
then that I was altogether free from pain.”
Reporter : “ Did you stop taking your
medicine immediately that happened ? ”
Mr. Lyes: “Not exactly, for I wanted
to be quite sure that all disease was effaced,
so I took another couple of bottles of
Clements. Tonic, and when they were
finished it was not necessary to take any
more, as I was as strong and thoroughly
restored as I could wish to be. It is several
years ago since Clements Tonic put an end
to sny sufferings, yet my work has never
since interfered with my health—a fact
which shows that Clements Tonic made me
as sound as a bell; and if these statements
are likely to be perused by others, you may
publish them as you like.”

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 3, 1903.
The High Commissionership.
As the Australian Parliament approaches one of
the last of its tasks in equipping the people with
the implements of nation-making, it is brought
face to face again with the motley opposition of
anti-Federalism. Those people, whose grotesque
ideas of “ loyalty ” to Britain involve them in a
consistent disloyalty to Australia; the members
of the Society mob, who were awakened by Lord
Hopetoun’s ignominious departure to the fact
that Federation was not a matter of social pomp
and Court ceremony, and now mourn with
alleged Senator Reid (whose firm’s invoice was
lately a matter of much interest) that it has
“fostered no great establishments” for the dis-
play of vulgar wealth ; and the State politicians
with their little parochial grievances—are prepar-
ing to unitedly join issue now against the appoint-
ment of an Australian High Commissioner.
Some of them can see no reason at all for the
representation of Australian interests in London ;
what it suits Britain to offer us, or to take from
us, should be agreed to without argument.
Britain knows what is best for us; blessed be
Britain, and away with the disloyal idea of
a High Commissioner who might seek to thwart
Joseph Chamberlain. Others understand no
reason other than a social one for the existence of
a High Commissioner in London, and urge the
appointment of some titled outsider, who can give
an occasional Australian plutocrat travelling to
England the joy of sitting down to dinner by the
side of a peer. Yet others are particularly
anxious that the State Agents-General shall con-
tinue with undiminished prestige to represent
Australia at the great bauble warehouse. With
an Australian High Commissioner, the State
Agents-General must be practically effaced and
lose all influence in helping the State politician to
his E.C.M.G., so these people suggest that the
duties of,a Commissioner can be very well carried
out by one oh other of the State agents in his
spare time. With different ideas as to what steps
should be taken to represent, or misrepresent, Aus-
tralia, all these sections agree that to send an able
public man to London to watch over matters of
foreign policy affecting Australian interests, to
take charge of negotiations with regard to Bills
reserved for the Royal assent, to guard the finan-
cial interests of the Commonwealth, and to.speak
generally on its behalf as an ambassador, is pre-
posterous. The alternatives range from having
no representation at all, down to putting our
affairs in the charge of the nominee of Jenkins,
or Philp, or See, or of some lordling who would
condescend to be Australia’s ambassador after a
perfunctory fashion in return for the official
prominence the post would give him. But it is
agreed quite unanimously that the Commonwealth
must not presume to fancy itself as dignified an
institution as, say, the State of Victoria that, in
short, any old thing is good enough for Australia.
» * * *
Of the various pleas put forward why the
Commonwealth should not act the decent, digni-
fied and truly economical part in this matter, the
most contemptible is that it would be easy to get
gome lord or other to act in an “honorary capa-
city ” and “ lend the influence of his name ” to
Australia. Meatiness is thus combined with
snobbishness and a degraded national feeling.
The Sydney Morning Herald gives more or less
open countenance to the proposal, in spite of (or
perhaps by reason of) the fact that it would
be a humiliating confession of our national
incapacity to produce a worthy representa-
tive of our own, and of our national reverence for
a lord, as a lord, which marks about the lowest
stage of the human intellect. Australia is a debt-
ridden country, but it has not yet reached the
stage at which it is necessary to plead for eleemo-
synary service. Australia is not, just now, a land
justified in flaunting a haughty spirit in public,
but it has not, surely, descended to such a depth
that it can be glorified and exalted by the
patronage of an English lord—a person of the
class to which belong Yarmouth and Anglesea
and Russell and our one-time visitor, the peer
who used to get free board in a suburban pub.
on condition that he looked after the dog.
Almost as .contemptible is the suggestion from
another quarter that, when'creating the office,
the salary should be made a practically nominal
one, as it would be easy enough to secure at any
price, or at no price, some socially-ambitious
rich man such as Mr. John Forrest, Mr.
Bonython or Mr. M‘Eacharn, only too glad to
misrepresent Australia in London for something
near to nothing a year whilst hunting lions and
a title. This sort of man, of course, couldn’t be
expected to hold up Australia’s end in a difference
with Britain, as that would blight his hope of a
title, and the search for a title would be his real
mission in London. Australia, in settling the
terms of this appointment, should be as
generous as possible, with a view to attracting
the best man available and to giving him as
good a chance of useful work as possible, for by
the High Commissioner Australia will, to a very
large extent, be judged abroad. He will be the
only one of the great officials of the Common-
wealth who will come constantly into close con-
tact with foreigners. As our representative
sample, it will be but business-like to have him
of good quality. He should be of the type to
whom no Britishah can say “Haw!” with any
justification. There are reasons for this, both of
sentiment and of solid business. Those of senti-
ment are, of course, unexplainable; people who
don’t feel them can never be taught them. Those
of business ought to be apparent to everybody.
* * * *
The High Commissioner must be a man who has
both the ability and the personal prestige to make
out a good case for Australia, to show up Aus-
tralia as an attractive and civilised patch of
earth. We do not want an influx of colored aliens
or of contract-bound wage slaves to this continent,
but we do want immigrants of the right sort —the
sort that America attracts so constantly. By-and-
bye, when tariff conditions here are better, the
openings for such immigrants—men with some
brains and some means—will be many. It will be
the High Commissioner’s duty to help to attract
them. At present Australian legislation
especially its radical legislation —is absurdly
misrepresented in London, and, on the text of the
Six Blathers and many similar fictions, sermons
are being constantly preached as to the ruin to
which “ labor legislation ” is driving this country.
A High Commissioner could throw a little of the
light of truth on such Rougemontese. Further,
the State-frights, black labor and Tory sections
in the Commonwealth are ever inciting the
British authority to interfere with Federal law-
making ; to have enactments reserved for the
Royal assent in the hope of their being
mangled is the constant plan of the news-
papers which play the game of these people. A
High Commissioner would devote some energy
towards frustrating those tricks. Another great
task which would fall to his hand would be the
negotiations between Cohen and Australia when
the inevitable happ ms, and the Common-
wealth takes up the burden of unifying
and (The Bulletin devoutly trusts) paying
the Australian debt. That seems likely to
happen soon. Even this year Victoria has
a £5,000,000 P.N. falling clue, which must be
renewed on some terms. Though the Federation
will take no direct responsibility in regard to that
loan, it must admit to some indirect concern, for
it cannot all >w the bailiffs to be put in on one of
its States. The presence of a Commonwealth repre-
sentative in London when that—the beginning
of the great loan trouble which is looming ahead—
comes to Cohen for decision is obviously desirable.
A High Commissioner is, in short, wanted at
once to be the champion of the Australian people,
the Australian Parliament and the Australian
Constitution at the court of the suzerain power
and the paramount Cohen, and before the world
generally. And he must be a man of Australian
training and of skilful diplomacy—not a titled
nobody representing Australia in a perfunctory
fashion for the honor of the thing, or a local
plutocrat devoting his energies to social advance-
ment, and selling the interests of Australia to
Britain in return for a British title.. To get the
proper man the Australian Parliament must pay
a reasonable price. The man or the horse or the
dog that is got for nothing, or next to nothing, is
always a bad bargain.
* »
At a hazard The Bulletin would say that the
Australian High Commissioner in London should
have a status and salary equal to that of the
British High Commissioner in Australia (who is
called Governor-General), adding promptly that,
in its opinion, the Governor-General is at present
too generously paid. To put it more
this paper wouldn’t think £5OOO a year too high
a price for a first-class man, and would vote
£3500 a year a moderate salary and satisfactory
to the national purse if satisfactory to the office-
holder. The Kyabram suggestion of £lOOO a year is
only a proposal to confine the range of selection to
wealthy persons with social ambitions, who could
misrepresent Australia as much as they pleased,
for if one wealthy snob was discharged it would
only be possible to replace him by another wealthy
snob. No magnificence should be required of the
High Commissioner, no great social entertain-
ments. But he should be put on the same plane
at least as a first-class bank manager or a cable
company’s representative, and paid so that he can
hold his office with dignity without drawing on
any private resources or mortgaging his future.
Further, he should be appointed at once. This
Parliament should not dissolve without appoint-
ing a Commissioner. If the tragical fooling
of George Keid does not permit of an Act
being passed, a make-shift provision for the office
should be made on the Estimates.
The N.S.W. Land Bill Fraud.
The N.S.W, Government, cordially aided and
abetted by the N.S.W Opposition, is busy buying
the farmers’ votes by another gross swindle upon
posterity. There have been so many gross swin-
dles upon posterity that it may be said that one
more doesn’t signify much, but that is hardly the
way any sane politician would view the matter.
Under the new Land Bill, the selectors of N.S.W.
are to be allowed to repudiate some more of their
obligations to the State by having the interest on
their unpaid balances reduced from 4 per cent.,
which is low enough, to per cent., which
is ridiculous. The reduction is not to take
place immediately, so that the present Govern-
ment need not at once face the loss of
revenue, and hump the obloquy of the deficit, and
have the disgrace of having to put on new taxes,
or cut down the wages of the public service, in
order to make up the shortage. The deduction
begins two or three years hence, and any liability
that is a year or two distant is regarded in N.S.W.
as hardly existing at all. But the glory and kudos
of taking a load off the “ down-trodden agricul-
turist ” begin immediately; the votes of the
gratified agriculturist begin immediately ; and for
a year or two the See Government will be able to
pose as the Government which took a heavy
liability oft the crushed agriculturist without any
disaster to the revenue. The great mass of the
public won’t notice the discrepancy.
* • » *
This is not all, nor nearly all, the relief that the
agriculturist gets nowadays. In addition to being
allowed to repudiate his bargain about the interest,
he is also allowed to repudiate his bargain about
the principal. When the State sold him land at
less than it was worth, or constructed railways
and other works which were not in the bond, and
which made his land worth three or four times
what he agreed to pay for it, the State has to hold
to the letter of its agreement—if it isn’t actually
confronted with a demand for a reduction. But
where there is the least shadow of a possibility
that the State made a reasonably good bargain, and
sold its land for something like the fair value there-
of, the buyer has always a chance to secure a re-
appraisement and to have the price reduced. One
result is that, in manv cases, the settler will be out
of debt to the State many years earlier than would
be the case if the original agreement remained
standing; in other words, the State’s income from
this source will leave off much sooner than would
otherwise be the case. This is another case where
the glory and the votes and the thankfulness fall
to the Governments of the present, and the loss,
and the grimy drudgery of making the loss
good, remain to posterity. _ And the pros-
pects of posterity look intensely gloomy.
At the end of 1895 there was owing to
the State over .£13,000,000 on account of
conditional purchases, and from this source
N.S.W. collected an annual revenue of about
£1,000,000 a year. But the new conditional
purchases taken up do not by any means counter-
balance the old ones paid off, and by 1900
the amount owing was reduced to £10,000,000.
The cheerful throwing away of a million or two
in the way of interest reductions and re-appraise-
ments will accelerate the extinction of this ever-
dwindling asset. The throwing-away process may
buy votes inthepresent, bu t i 11 1 as tens th e time w hen
about £1,000,000 a year will suddenly disappear
out of the revenue. Posterity will have to face
that loss ; also it will have to face a vastly
increased interest bill; an increased outlay for old
age pensions; a Civil Service Superannuation
Fund and Police Superannuation Fund that have
gone broke and become a charge on the revenue
through Governments using the funds for wrongful
purposes ; also it will have to shoulder the deficits
of to-day, and to construct out of revenue works
that are now charged to loans, for the loan
insanity can’t last for ever. The whole financial
policy of N.S.W. to-day—the policy of which the
present Land Bill is a fair sample—is a cruel,
heartless process by which the fathers of the
present swindle their own sons and their own
daughters, and lay on the narrow shoulders of the
infant a burden which they are too callous and too
cowardly to carry themselves,
* » * *
The present N.S.W. Land Bill practically puts
an end to all hops of an honest and efficient
system of resuming large estates for closer settle-
ment. The closer settlement idea is that the
State should repurchase large estates, cut them
up, and either sell or lease them in small areas at
a rate which will, at the very least, fully repay
the State’s outlay. But the Government policy
of reduction, repudiation, and re-appraisement has
in many cases reduced prices so low that the man
on the resumed estate, if he is to pay enough to
cover the outlay on the repurchase of the property,
will be paying three or four times as much as the
original selector on an equally good piece of
ground alongside. And the result will be that the
man on the resumed property will demand that
his land be re appraised also, and his price re-
duced, and the almost inevitable result will be
that the big estate which was resumed at an honest
value of £lOO,OOO, and sold or let at corresponding
rates, will be re appraised till the State only gets
£50,000 or only collects interest on £50,000; and
the balance of the transaction will be dead loss.
When it is impossible to buy votes by further re-
ducing the rent or the price to the State’s original
leaseholder or selector, the process of buying votes
by re-appraising the man on resumed land will
naturally follow. Foolish or corrupt politicians
have let it become an article of N.S.W.’s political
creed that no faith need be kept with the
State—that the man who buys land from it for
£lOO need only pay £5O if he feels that way, and
the man who rents land from the State at £5O
need only pay just about what he darned well
pleases, provided he interviews his member with
sufficient energy. It has also become an article
of faith that the “poor farmer ” should be always
an object of pity and charity, while at the same
time posing as the sturdy, self-reliant backbone of
the community—that he shouldn’t pay any local
rates, that his roads, bridges and culverts should
be provided by the general taxpayer, that his
produce should be carried at one-fourth the rate
that the manufacturer pays, and that he should
be allowed to vary his bargains with the country
in a large and reckless manner. It may be
that he needs all this assistance ; but if he does,
farming in N.S.W is, so far as concerns a very
large extent of the country, a mere parasitic indus-
try—not self-supporting, but living on the rest of
the community. And if so, the whole political
situation requires to be reconsidered.
• * • •
When the loans were obtainable the farmer
received the benefit of a vast expenditure of loan
money--there is hardly a bridge, road, or culvert,
and certainly not one yard of railway, built
otherwise than on credit —and the bill was
left to posterity. Now that loans are almost
unobtainable, he is being forgiven the debts he
owes to posterity. Posterity gets the burden to
carry in every case. Both sides of the Assembly,
in their eagerness to buy the agricultural vote,
are falling over each other in their haste to cancel
the debt which the N.S.W. settlers owe to pos-
terity. And how posterity will get along no one
cares much.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Federal Arbitration.
The Federal Arbitration Bill, Prime Minister
Barton has gone out of his way (at a bagmen’s
banquet) to assure State Premier Irvine, will not
include any provision for State or Commonwealth
Government employees. Practicaljy Barton’s
statement amounts to a pledge that, if an amend-
ment is carried to extend the benefits of the Bill
to such workers, the measure will be dropped.
What logic there .is in decreeing by law that any
disputes as to industrial conditions arising between
workers and private employers are to be settled by
judicial process and not by the brutal expedient
of strike and lock-out, whilst such disputes between
workersand public employers are to be lefttotheold
anarchic, riotous, desolating way of settlement, the
helpless, drifting Barton didn’t say. If there is
reason for interfering with “ the liberty of the
subject ” to prevent the stoppage of private indus-
tries, there is, surely, far more reason to do so to
prevent the disturbance of much larger State-
owned enterprises which, as a rule, are vitally
essential to the public well-being. If the boot-
trade is stopped by a strike there is loss, incon-
venience, some misery perhaps, but the com-
munity life is undisturbed. If the water supply
or the railway service is stopped, then industrial
war begins to wear the aspect of real war. A
Federal Arbitration Bill, which ref uses tofollow the
example of the N.S.W. Act and bring under its
scope State employees, seems to be hardly worth
worrying about. Barton’s Bill also exempts
seamen, and on this point the Labor Party is
being urged by the Seamen’s Union to insist on
amendment. The argument against including
seamen is that the Bill will then be reserved for
Royal assent and thus delayed, perhaps, a year.
That difficulty can be easily surmounted by
giving the seamen the benefit of the Act in
an accompanying Act; the Labor Party should
insist on this being done. Were it not for the
The Land of the Loan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lo, this is the Land of the Debtor, the rich fief
of a far mortgagee —
Aye, the Land that the interest-fetter binds safely
to Jews o’er the sea !
This is the fool land of the careless, of the sliders
and slouchers—alas !
That a country which ought to be peerless should
barter its soul for mean “brass ” !
For this is a country whose people beg ever, in
shrill monotone;
Aye, liened from foundation to steeple, is this
Land of the Lingering Loan !
Yea, this is the land whose resources lie fallow,
awaiting the plough,
Where the wealth-bearing trade water-courses are
damless and run anyhow. (
Yea, this is the nation perpensive, where nothing
is started or done ;
Where the over-sea dues are extensive and the
bank-balance total is none !
Aye, this, the dear Land we were born in, lifts a
weak and a beggarly moan—
O, the shame to seek alms night and morning and
make it a Land of the Loan !
Lo, the fair title-deeds of our Birth-place are
locked in the safes of the Jews—
We’ve bartered our precious sweet earth-place for
funds to continue the Booze !
We are loungers and strollers and loafers, doing
chores in a leisurely way,
For we know in advance the pawn-coffers have
the call on our national pay !
We are lazy, indolent, weary, ever lifting a mendi-
cant’s groan ;
Aye, listless and useless and bleary, we 101 l in our
Land of the Loan !
Where the factory-fires should be smoking and the
thunder of hammers be heard—
Lo, we lie on our backs idly joking, caring less
than a GoD-foddered bird.
When the ear straineth after the clangor and
music of foundry and mill,
Only echo the sighs of our languor—in the place of
work’s thunder and thrill!
Where the red blood should throb and glow tidal,
where muscles should buttress the bone,
Lo 1 a current flows dully and idle in this beggarly
Land of the Loan !
Shall we beg, shall we borrow for ever, despised
by all nations of men ?
Shall we never make manly endeavor with hammer
and heart and with pen ?
Shall we for the world be a scorning—ever
craving a charity boon ?
Shall we float the P.N. in the morning and the
Bill in the fair afternoon ?
Shall our Land be ever the Debtor, the place
where the Jew has his throne ?
Shall we carry for ever the fetter, be always the
Land of the Loan ?
There be writers, my country, that laud you and
call you by flattering names.
They are hirelings of those who defraud you with
swindling over-sea games !
There be those who paint ever with crimson the
picture of life unto thee—
Listen now to a stern and a grim son, with a curse
in the place of their glee !
Listen now to a child of your bosom, ere at last
you be fooled and undone —
O give heed to the dark fate and gruesome hang-
ing o’er you, Land of the Loan !
Let us rise ere the sun mounteth higher; let us
drop the poor crutch and the whine.
There is time yet to kindle the fire of Freedom,
O Country of Mine !
There is time, but the limit is near us—we are
poised on the edge of the ditch ;
Shall we march full of life down the eras, or
crawl, alms-importing—say which ?
Over-long have we crept in the bye-ways where
the dogs seek the crust and the bone.
Let us cry a new faith in the highways and arouse
this dead Land of Loan.

policy on any account. More dignity and less
banquet would make an improvement in Premier
Barton.

Irvine’s Resumption Scheme.
I-ER RVINE> Victoria, in his speech at

That was a good beginning, but the ending was a
grievous falling away. For Irvine straightway
proceeded to assure his audience that the State
would pay full compensation, according to present
values, to the dispossessed landlords. So the situ-
ation is this :
(1) The people gave the land a new value by the
wP. m V l at^- 0f . po l' ulation . and they pay the landlords
fo £* hat - U'ns is probably unavoidable.)

towards the railways above-mentioned, and by incurring
usre deficits in order to avoid a general land-tax. And
tney propose to pay the landlord for that. (This is pure
unadulterated swindle, fraud, steal and three-card trick.)
The true market value of land is what it will
fetch in a community that doesn’t borrow any-
thing. When land carries with it a tax liability
it fetches less than unencumbered land— i.e., land
without a tax liability. With a large tax liability
it fetches less than with a small one. When a
country raises enough taxation to wholly pay its
way then everything finds its fair market level.
But to buy land on the temporary inflated value
that it acquires through a temporary relief from
m nnn OD a eni P orar y relief secured by borrowing
L 1,000,000 a year and piling up an accumulated
shortage of £3,ooo,ooo—is pure fraud. Some day
loans will have to stop, and land will have to
carry its share of the public burdens, and until
then its real value will be a mystery. A scheme
for the extensive resumption of land should be
preceded in every case by a financial reform which
will put the Treasury on an absolutely honest
basis, without any recourse to Cohen, either local
or foreign. Anything short of that means wasting
the peoples money on the purchase of fictitious
values. Also, no scheme for the resumption and
cutting up of large estates is honest unless it pro-
vides some device that will prevent the small
being gradually bought up by capitalists
till the big estates form again.

The Maoriland Cold Tea Difficulty.
It isn’t certain even yet that Maoriland’s long-
lived Seddon Government won’t fall over the
licjuor question. Certainly it has tottered over
this matter as it never tottered before
Beddon’s own sympathies are mostly with the
liquor people, but the strong man who drove so
many others is now being driven himself, and he
doesn t go gracefully in harness, nor is he satis-
fied to look back from his menial job in the
shafts and see the cadaverous teetotaller on the
box. The snag which Seddon struck concerned
the Newtown and Bruce local option polls and
some other things. Prohibition was carried
m these districts by the necessary three-fifths
majority, but the polls were declared invalid
on technical grounds. Then, as the law
by some oversight, had made no provision
for holding a second poll in such a case
and the regular poll wouldn’t come round again
for three years, there was a deadlock. The
licensing authorities, elected by the local Cold-Tea
majority, cut the knot by refusing to renew the
licenses—they declared, in fact, that, though the
poll was invalid, it was valid enough for them.
The publicans declared this action illegal, and
appealed to the Supreme Court. Pending the
appeal, they kept open and put a head on the
beer • for the usual cash consideration. The
Supreme Court decided against the publicans,
who thereupon proposed to appeal to the Privy
Council. They also proposed to sell beer without
a license till the Privy Council came to a
decision (in a year or tivo it might be, for
that august body moves slowly), whether they
had a right to get their licenses renewed or not.
Then all parties broke like a troubled sea on Pre-
mier Seddon. The Cold Tea crowd demanded
that he should enforce the law by the aid of the
police, and accused him of having given instruc-
tions to the force not to move. The liquor people
warned him that if he closed their pubs while the
case was sub judice it would be dangerously near to
contempt of court, and that if the Privy Council
decided in their favor after their pubs had been
closed, the bill for damages would be heavy— not
to say enormous. The situation was a difficult
one for Seddon ; he had for many years led
an overwhelmingly strong party, so that he
didn’t require to steer a careful course
among excited and evenly - balanced factions,
and, as a natural consequence, his gift of diplo-
macy was rusty. If he drove the Cold Tea crowd
to join the small knot of his irreconcilable
enemies the combination might be strong
enough to run him out of office. If he drove
the liquor crowd to do likewise, he would
have to face the strongest opposition he had met
for years. His own sympathies were with the
publicans, and he tried to hold up their end of
the barrel, but the Cold Tea people threatened
war, and he dropped that end of the keg suddenly
and ungracefully. So, for the time being, things
stand something like this: The teetotallers are
to have their way, but they look coldly on
Seddon as an enemy who yields ungrace-
fully under compulsion. The publicans look
coldly on him, as a friend who abandoned
them under compulsion. His mana has suffered
considerably since he struck against something
stronger than himself —which was the cadaverous
spectre of Cold Tea. Also, it is necessary to bring
in a Licensing Bill to put the law on a better
basis and prevent any more such deadlocks as
have happened _ of late, and that Bill will turn
the Legislature into a bear-garden. The measure
that Seddon would probably like to bring in
would wreck the Government. The measure that
the extreme teetotallers want him to bring in
might wreck the Government, and the sight of it
would probably drive Seddon to murder in cold
blood the first Cold Tea person he met, for it
would be a fearsome scheme, and calculated to
make any large, full-bodied gentleman fly from
the country in disgust. Yet the present law has
reached such a state of deadlock that it is impos-
sible to leave things alone. The sleeping dog
must be roused up, and he is a dog of so many
cantankerous and diverse views that there is no
knowing what will happen when he wakes.

The Need to Get Rid of Owe’Sullivan.
ALL over New South Wales public works are
being stopped for want of loan funds because
Cohen has turned cranky ; and the only satisfac-
tion that is offered is Owe'Sullivan's assurance
that things will resume when loans again pour
m from over the sea. When that will be, neither
the Owe’Sullivan nor any other person can tell.
lhere are various considerations which tend to
make the situation uncertain. In every country
there is a certain amount of money for investment
at low rates combined with supposedly dead-sure
security the money of orphans, old women,
retired elderly grocers, reserve funds of careful
institutions, and so on. Also, there is a certain
amount for investment at higher rates and not quite
so good security—the money of the more enterpris-
mg gamblers of the community. It is from the class
wh:e 1 is willing to take low interest for good
security that Australia borrows, and quite apart
from the fact that the security doesn’t look what
it once did, various things have happened of late,
i he British Government has borrowed £200,000,000
from this class to begin with. Then water-works,
tramways, etc., which used to be in the hands of
private companies and belong to the speculative
class of investments, are steadily passing under
municipal control, and the municipalities have
borrowed immense sums to buy up these works.
lhe money is being gradually repaid, but still
the kind of money that Australia used to borrow
r 16 money of the orphan and the old woman—
is now being mopped up by British municipal loans.
Jiiven if our credit doesn’t smell any worse with
the progress of years than it does now, there is no
knowing when the loan business can recommence.
Meanwhile, the half-finished public works are
being left to moulder. There is not the slightest
ft bout the helpless, shiftless, uncompre-
bending See-Owe’Sui.livan Ministry of any
attempt to carry on necessary worksout of revenue.
I here is certainly a promise that the State expen-
diture will be cut down to bedrock, but the See-
Owe Sullivan Ministry and- all the rest of the
present mob of politicians have strange views as to
what constitutes bedrock, and the idea seems
merely to be that an attempt will be made to pay
those liabilities which can’t possibly be postponed.
Ihe-auestion to be decided, now or very soon, is
whether, when loans are temporarily unprocur-
able, all progress is to stop short; and whether,
when loans become permanently unprocurable, all
progress is to leave off for ever. The Owe’Sul-
livan s assurance that things will start again when
some more borrowing can be done, and his utter
inability to even imagine anything resuming until
some more borrowing can be done, certainly point
m that direction. The inference is that the only
way to prevent stagnation and a drift back into
barbarism is to get rid of Owe’Sullivan and
everythmg that he represents. N.S.W. cannot
afford to stagnate utterly even for the joy of having l
Owe’Sullivan.
Irvine, the Illogical.
By his recent declaration of his policy at Nhill
Irvine has shown himself to be a most absurdly
illogical Premier, and Victoria hardly knows
where to look for its Iceberg now. Throughout
that speech Irvine hopped so quicklv from Tory
to Socialist, and from Socialist to Tory, that it
made the eyeballs ache to watch him. With a
jump so startling in its quickness that it almost
took away one’s breath, he was in the thick of a
magnificent argument in favor of the compulsory
resumptions of big estates, and with an outburst
of fine scorn he overturned the Tory fetish of the
sanctity of the rights of property. “ There is ’’
he said, “no right created by the State that’is
paramount to the well-being of the State itself. It
is said that the principle of compulsory resump-
tion impairs the sanctity of title and the sacred-
ness of the rights of property. The objection
rests _ upon an entire misconception of the
meamng of the word ‘ property ’ and ‘rights.’
All rights of property rest solely upon the
fact that the enforcement of them tends
to the benefit of the whole community. The State
can never give a title which will at any time
defeat the State’s ultimate ends.” Those are state-
ments that go to the very root of Socialism. No
better answer to the objections taken by Tories to
Socialism could have been made by the most
advanced Socialist. Irvine .proved in those few
sentences that the sacred rights of property are a
pure myth. “ There ain’t no such person.” Yet
in the next breath the same Irvine was asserting
that any compulsory resumption system must be
accompanied by grants of the freehold to the
smaH farmers who take up the resumed lands.
Why had the brave Socialist of the front part
of the speech not the courage to continue in a
straight line, and say that the tenure must be lease-
hold ’. that the State being the sole owner of the
land should not continue the farce of pretending to
give a sacred and inviolable title which he frankly
confesses it has no right to give and cannot give ’
Ihe granting of the alleged freehold title to the
small farmers who are to be put on the land instead
of sheep, is the blot on Irvine’s otherwise states-
manlike proposal to cut up the big estates. Coming
to the question of industrial legislation, Irvine
oscillated and tumbled about bet ween the poles 1 of
Socialism and Toryism in an equally astounding
manner. He denounced compulsory arbitration
as an evil thing. He shuddered when he men-
tioned the word, and with trembling finger pointed
out how it put shackles on the poor worker for
whom Irvine and his Ministry have shown such
• deep concern. It nearly broke him up to
contemplate the condition into which the
workers would be thrown by compulsory arbi-
tration. That frame of mind cheered up the
many Tory friends who were listening. Then
by a quick turn, Irvine was admitting he had
become convinced that it was possible to control
wages by legislation. . The experience of the Vic-
torian Factories Act had satisfied him that it was
quite possible to prevent employers taking advant-
age of the hunger of their fellow-men to make
them work long hours for little pay. And because
he had become convinced of that fact, he proposed
to create a tribunal in every way similar to the
Compulsory Arbitration Court, which he had just
mangled and left for dead, to review and revise
the work of the Wages Boards. In other words,
Irvine, the great opponent of compulsory arbi-
tration, says he intends to add to the Wages
Board system of the Victorian law an Arbitration
Court.. In a dim sort of way Irvine begins to see
that his dictum in regard to the rights of property
applies equally to the rights of employers to
enslave the rest of the community. It is to
the interests of the State., as a whole, to have a
healthy, well-fed race of. independent men and
women, while it is to the interest of the employers
to make profits out of the employes irrespective
of the fact that their doing so might result in a
weedy, servile, hungry race of white slaves.
According to Irvine the Socialist, the interests
of the employer, when they thus come into
collision with the interests of the State, are not
worth shucks and must not be considered. If
Irvine could only shed his steel bound casing of
Toryism he would make a great Socialist.

The Gun that was Loaded.
The pained surprise of the old, hard-shell Tories
a,t the democratic goings-on of the Australian Par-
liament, though a trifle amusing, is not to be
wondered at. Some of these political relics were
amongst the staunchest supporters of Federation,
and only an odd one—such as the late Sargood—
had the prevision to forecast what “the most
liberal franchise in the world ” was likely to pro-
duce. They are now discovering, to their amaze-
ment, that the gun is loaded. This fact explains
the whole of the present bogus agitation in con-
nection with the Arbitration Bill, the “Bush
Capital,” and Federation in general; and so loud
and impudent is the fat critic of the Australian
Parliament becoming that a word of warning to
the democratic elector is necessary. The mere
fact that practically the whole of the hubbub is
being created by the various Chambers of
Commerce and Horrors and Manufactures, by
the Municipal Councils, and “ National Reform ”
Leagues, is in itself a highly suspicious cir-
cumstance. At. the. present moment half the
time of the Victorian Municipal Councils is
taken up with considering proposals from
each other for conferences to protest against
the “white’.’ clause in the ocean mail contracts,
the Arbitration Bill, and the idea of having a
Federal capital which will be wholly national
property. The other day, a West Victorian
auctioneer took the appropriate opportunity
of a “swine conference” to point out the
“dangerous tendencies” of Federal legisla-
tion. Even such a pure merino as squatter
Manifold, who happens to be this auctioneer’s
representative, is beginning to be suspected of
democratic leanings; and the Argos lately -
announced that his recent actions had not pleased
his constituents, and that he would probably be
opposed at the approaching elections. These are
all signs of the times, and they point to the fact that
the Tory and his wife, together with his cousins
and his maiden aunt, will attend the polls in
force next December. If the democratic party is
to hold its own, it must continue perfecting its
organisation, and must see to it that its collective
mother, and sister, and mother-in-law—very few
democrats have maiden aunts—record their votes.
Australia wants next Australian Parliament to be
vastly more Federal and more progressive than
this one. It wants it to be solid on high Protec-
tion, a heavy Federal graduated land-tax, the
taking over of the State debts and State railways,
the repayment of the public debt, Federal old age
pensions, and the extension of the Federal
authority in every direction. And it wants no
State-rights cranks in it, and no importers.
Under these conditions the Democracy needs .to
be very wide-awake indeed next December.

A Federal Land Tax.
King O’Malley proposes, if lie gets the chance, to
challenge the House of Representatives to express
itself on the large question of a Federal Land
Tax. Probably he won’t get a chance; but he
has, anyhow, given notice of motion for the
earliest available day, which is somewhere in the
remote tail-end of October, and The Bulletin
wishes him luck. His idea (which was The
Bulletin’s idea before it was his, though the
King is welcome to it) is that Australia is
suffering from’ two diseases in particular—the
foreign-debt disease and the big landlord disease.
The absentee landlord disease is merely a
complication of the other two. A land tax
can be made to cope with all of them ; and,
on the ground that the larger the estate the
more malignant the disease, O’Malley proposes a
graduated treatment. His own idea is to start at
£2OOO, exempting all estates below that limit,
charging Ad. per £1 when the value is between
£2OOO and £20,000, and rising to 6d. in the case
of estates valued at £400,000. The suggested
exemption is, possibly, fixed too high; and the
tax should almost certainly begin at Id. in the £
instead of |d., but these are matters of detail
which can be put right when the general principle
is accepted. To his graduated-tax proposals
O’Malley adds one dealing with absentees—very
brief, very effective; that they shall pay double
the foregoing rates. The absentee proprietor, as
said before, combines the two diseases; he not
only in most cases locks up land against set-
tlement, but he takes the proceeds out of the
country. If an absentee landlord owns £250,000
worth of landed property, from which he draws 6
per cent., the effect is precisely the same as if this
community had borrowed a quarter of a million
from a foreigner at 6 per cent. . The absentee
landlord thus adds injury to. injury, and can only
be reached by a double taxi Hardly, however, is
this a tax at alb in either case. No part of the
money which would be taken would be value
created by the man who pays it. The community
would simply ask for the return for public purposes
of a small proportion of the value'which it has itself
created. There is, of course, most urgent need of
the money. While old-age pensions are admitted
to be a necessity of modern civilisation, only two
Australian States are paying them. Moreover,
through the constant passing of population from
'State to State, a merely local pension system has
proved inadequate and unfair. A graduated land
tax would provide the funds for a complete and
fairer system, and if it was the kind of tax it should
be it would also provide the means for the equally
essential Public Debt sinking fund. At the same
time it Would, by hastening the break-up of big
estates, provide land for the people, bring popu-
lation to the country to occupy that land, provide
a host of new taxpayers to relieve the Treasury
and a host of new customers for the local manu-
facturer, and a lot of new people to supply
railway revenue, and it would diminish the
public debt per head by bringing here a large
number of new people to carry a share of the
burden. It is of no earthly use to wait for people
of the Philp stamp to move in this matter. The
democratic Parliament of Australia is the onlv
body in which there is any hope, and it is handi-
