The Fluent Judge.
boJthnThiTt after he ha<l declared in the witness
that his tongue never. got .awey from him so
wantPrl*w m u , sln F the exact word he
wanted, Victoria’s Waxed Chief Justice found one
&JIJL oPPftunities that he never misses for
digging that sharp forensic implement into the
subject of human depravity— the depravity that
generally consists of a weeping female making a
vigorous endeavor to imprison and flog a man
who, as a rule, hasn’t assaulted her. In this
case where two men were found guilty of
drunkenJyoutraging an old woman, Dr. Madden
probably awarded what they deserved, although
there was an extraordinary conflict of testimony
on the part of the prosecutrix herself. But the
waxed one notwithstanding his lesson in
the witness-box, couldn’t resist saying a few
words on the “continually growing disposition
among some of the community to hold a thorough
unbelief in the honor of women,” which made it
appear more and more indispensable, the longer
his experience of the courts, to “firmly resent
these cases.” Also, the C. J. stated that “ per
cent, of the trials then before him were similar
in character. This being the case, there seems to be
either a very rapid growth of sex lunacy in Vic-
tona, or the hysterical female is growing increas-
ingly wanton in her desire to ruin some man’s
reputation. Mr. Madden having quoted his
statistics, it would be as well to have some figures
from the other side, showing exactly the propor-
tion of cases in which the accusations of the
allegedly outraged female have fallen to the
ground, usually through the final admission
of the lady herself that she is a brazen
liar. _ These figures would completely upset
Justice Madden’s estimate so far as con-
victions are concerned. Investigation of the
kind in respect of, say, the last five years’ criminal
assault charges, would show at once that the
Waxellency and his brother Judges have been
neglecting a public duty in failing to send about
half the complaining females to prison for per-
jury, and have been pandering to crime, and
shielding about the worst class of criminals from
justice. _ A good, honest attempt to deal with the
matter in all its bearings—especially in the way
of gaoling the lying female—would do more good
than all Chief Justice Madden’s tongue-wagging.
N.S.W. requires 60 to 100 new locomotives, and
the See Government has announced thatit will have
them built in the State, even if they cost more than
the imported articles. Solomon never made any
wiser remark in his Book of Proverbs. It is
cheaper to pay a man ss. a week above the English
standard for doing something useful than to pay
him 10s. a week relief money for digging silly
holes somewhere. You save ss. a week to begin
with ; also you develop a useful artisan instead of
a semi-pauper. And if £250,000 is kept in the
country which would otherwise have gone out, it
probably means £300,000 difference to the revenue.
Few people realise that the revenue is such a com-
prehensive thing that it annually mops up
and spends again all the money there
is in the country, and more. Judging by
the _ bank returns, and allowing for the coin
m circulation, it is practically certain that there
is never more than £8,000,000 of cash in all
N.S. W. And out of that the Government collects
and spends again £11,000,000 in a single year. It
practically collects all the money there is in the
State every nine months. Therefore, a policy
which will leave £250,000 more in the country
may easily prevent a slump of even more than that
sum in the public income, and in the present state
of the public income that is worth considering.
The Jenkins Government has withdrawn most of
the charges on which Dr. Ramsay Smith, the City
Coroner, was suspended, and has substituted
others. One of the accusations was that he had
taken the head of a man named Hickman. Hick-
man, during an altercation, was knocked down
and died of skull-fracture. At the inquest a
witness stated that the assailant had used a stick,
and the doctor who examined the body agreed
that the injury might have been caused by a
heavy blow with such a weapon. The jury, acting
on this testimony, returned a verdict of
manslaughter. Eventually, Dr. Ramsay Smith
was ordered to exhume the body and to
make another autopsy. Accompanied by the
police he went to the country town where
Hickman .was buried, and obtained the head. He
carefully inspected it, and decided that the injury
was caused by a fall on a jagged stone. The police
searched the scene of the fatality and found a
stone with hair and blood on it, which exactly
fitted the wound (bloodstains'last a long time in
rainless S.A.). Seeing that the doctor was ex-
pressly, sent to get Hickman’s head, the fact that
he got it doesn’t seem much of a case for suspen-
Sion. Another charge withdrawn is that regarding
the head of Bertha Schippan, which, it was
ultimately proved, had never left the custody of
the police.
So soon as the Queensland Treasurer’s new Stamp
Tax proposals were announced, Brisbane’s cham-
ber of Commerce was hurriedly called together,
and, after inviting the city bankers to join and
help to express profound horror, proceeded to
wildly discuss the affair. After getting very red
and heated, the Chamber unanimously resolved
to. appoint a deputation for the purjiose of
pointing out that it was “distinctly a form of
class legislation, and calculated to hamper trade
and drive it out of the State.” In England the
citizen is taxed on an affidavit (2s. 6d.), agreement
(6d.), taking a furnished house (2s. 6d.), valuation
(from 3d. up to £1), for being a house agent (£2 a
year), apprenticeship indentures (2s. 6d.), for
being articled to a solicitor (£80), for being an
auctioneer (£lO a year), for an arbitrator’s award
(3d. to £1 155.), for selling playing cards (£1), for
a motor car (15s. to £5 55.), for being a London
solicitor (£9 a year), ocn tract to buy stock (Id. to Is.)
delivery order for goods (Id.), for being an ecclesi-
astical lecf*B-er (10s.), church (10s.), dog (7s. 6d )
for permission to sell certain kinds of fowls (£2 a
year), permission to own a pistol (10s. a year), to
keep a place for curing drunkards (£5), to own a
house (2d. to 9d. in the £ of rental) — The Bulletin
here stops in sheer weariness when not anything
like a quarter through the list. But to put it
shortly, m England it is hard to find anything,
and especially any mercantile document, that
isn’t taxed, and the Englishman takes it quite
philosophically. Also, it doesn’t drive trade
away, though it is about the most aggravating
system of small and eternal taxation that could be
devised, and twenty times more aggravating than
any Protectionist tariff—England even taxes you
on your male servant 10s. a year, and demands
£5 ss. a year for a license to sell sweets, and Id.
on a proxy to vote at a meeting of shareholders in
a bursted bank. Gonsidering that trade survives
this sort of thing in England, the Brisbane
Chamber of Buyers and Sellers has no need to
grow so excited.

“ Secret Service,” an American drama
which is not new to Australia, doubtless owes its
popularity to the novel character of its plot and
the very sensational situations of Act 111. For
it is hot at all well constructed. With an ambi-
tious effort to preserve “the unities ” by bringing
the whole action within one day, and a still more
ambitious attempt to enlist sympathy for a man
who is a spy, and a very mean spy at that, the
play is full of faults of detail. In Act 1.,
for instance, a good deal of time is taken
up with light comedy between Wilfred
Varney and Caroline Mitford. The action of the
play is just then at a critical point, and
the amorous bye-play of the juvenile lovers
seems at first to be so shaped as to help the plot
on, to prepare for and develop the next situation.
But it does nothing of the sort. It is mere
“ comic relief ” dragged in needlessly, like the red-
headed servant’s maunderings in a cheap melo-
drama. Compared with, say, a Sardou play, in
which every incident is significant and essential,
“ Secret Service ”is poorly done. The author had
a novel idea, but not enough skill to work it out.
Still the unusualness of the piece gives it attractive-
ness. The central figure is a Yankee spy who has
entered the city of Richmond—practically the last
stronghold of the falling Confederacy—to betray it
and anything else that is necessary. In pursuit
of his plans he worms his way into the house
of Mrs. Varney, wife of a Confederate
general, and makes love to her daughter
and uses her servants to carry his messages.
The spy in war is not at the best a
figure commanding sympathy ; still his work is
sometimes necessary, and since it is necessary,
honorable. But in this play the spy’s methods
are so contemptible as to take from him all excuse.
Yet he is the hero, and his love for Miss Varney,
and his spying, and his final betrayal of his own
friends by refusing to give them the secrets he
had basely won, and his prospective happy
marriage with the lady, make up the piece. Only
the very highest of art could carry such an idea
through successfully ; and that author Gillette
has not.
It is nob the best of plays, however, that
are most popular, and “ Secret Service ” wins its
way to public favor in spite of all its faults.
The Frawley co. certainly plays it admirably.
Daniel Frawley’s style is exactly suited to the
cool, desperate character of Lewis Dumont, and
he carries the sympathy of the audience with the
spy all the evening. Miss Van Buren (as Edith
Varney), his lover, has a part of fine dramatic
intensity and plays it with great success. A
dignified figure is that of Mrs. Varney, the old
general’s wife; Miss' Blanche Douglas, who has
hitherto taken ingenue parts, acts it with quiet
grace. Harry Plimtner, as usual, has to take up
a thankless part —that of Arrelsford—a man who
is true to his cause and attempts no treachery, but
who is very hardly treated by the dramatist,
becoming in reality “the villain of the piece.”
Others who stand out prominently in a big cast
are Miss Christine Hill (in a broad negress part),
Miss Eva Dennison (as the arch and kittenish
Caroline Mitford), and .T. R. Amory. “Secret
Service will be withdrawn on Saturday, and there
will follow, in quick succession, “In Mizzouri,”
“ Arizona” (for two performances), and “Barbara
Freitchie ’’—presumably the lady who shook the
H ig at Stonewall Jackson.

Leinare’s final Sydney organ recital on
August 29 was interesting because the programme
was packed with the items that the player had
found most popular during his season; and of
the nine favorites a crowded house evidently
most favored the mock-heroic “ Funeral March of
a Marionette.” After that C'nipp’s Fantasia on
the Austrian National Hymn, Lemare’s own
Bircarolle, and the crash-bang “Ride of the
Valkyries.” Final accounts have not been made
up yet; but it is said that the City Council hasn’t
lost by Lemare’s engagement, £5OO being left over
and above the organist’s £BOO wherewith to pay
for incidentals.

The “ Midsummer Night’s Dream ” closes
its brilliant career at Sydney Royal to-morrow
(Friday). It has been a very brilliant career, yet
Ceorge Rignold presented the same play with
a more capable company, and made a better
Nicholas Bottom than the present wearer of the
hearing apparatus of the ass; and though he
didn’t mount the play so expensively he deserved
quite as good luck—and didn’t get it. The human
materials of which that, and many other, fine old
shows were constructed, are either battling round
Australia in small parts, snowed under by the
rush of imported and widely-advertised Eng-
lish actors of very medium quality, or
else are battling individually in many corners
of the earth. If a wealthy manager wanted
to try something new, he might do worse than
take an Australian company (Australian by
birth or long residence) to London in return for
the many English companies that have come here ;
and if the English companies we have seen are a
fair criterion, he could show London something
much better than it expected. Certainly he could
snd back as good a “ Midsummer Night’s
Dream ” as England has sent to us.
Manager Musgrove stages “"Twelfth!
Night ” at the Royal on Saturday. This isn’t
exactly one of the lost plays of Shakspeare—lost*
to the stage, that is, as are “ Henry VI.” and!
“ Titus Andronicus,” and other more or' less;
lamented productions of blood and tonnage—but.
it is a thing that arises only at long intervals-,, andl
is therefore all the more welcome.

“My Lady Molly ” will be snowed under-
sometime next week. She has drawn good audi-
ences, for life and love and color and calves, not to*
speak of attractive solos and rousing choruses, are;
what the majority of audiences appreciate. The*
comic operator doesn’t worry about sequence and
the classic unities. The story halts in the telling;
whilst a man servant dances with a chambermaid,,
or it pauses to let the ingenue be presented with*
a fox’s brush by a crowd of hunters. The chronic*
visitor to “Lady Molly” gets captious towards,
the close of the season, and says that the brush is*
half a lady’s boa, and he is critical about the.
stockings of the chorus and says some of them*
are not silk but lisle thread—for through
silk the leg complexion should be quite apparent.
After the first fortnight he knows the music by
heart, and can tell if principals or chorus omit one*
mechanical smile or add an extra mannerism. By
now he has come to the conclusion that Florence*
Young’s voice is a wonderful stayer, and that her
somewhat hard face is greatly softened by the*
wearing of a white wig. Evelyn Scott’s voice,
he discovers, won’t “stay.” It is “ tinny,” but
the fact that her little beak is Hebraic is forgiven*
because her smile is so pretty. When he turns*
to male principals he wonders why Harold Thorley
doesn’t keep his long coat buttoned to hide his in-
artistic legs. As for the grooms, hunters, guests
and peace officers—he loves them all, but all the*
same, he’ll be glad to see them in fresh disguises.

There’s a whiff of old-time paganism about
the atmosphere of Wirths’ Circus, and judging by
the crowds which nightly cram the big green tent,
there is little difference between the taste of the
Australians under Consul Barton and that of the
Romans in the days of Honorius and the empe-
rors who tottered immediately after him. The
Alexandrine and Roman got slightly more in their
free show than the Australian does for cash ; but
that is the fault of the times. Circus appetite is a
depraved taste. The crowds who flock to the
great tent nightly go to see a tremendous gamble.
The small child ties herself into double and treble
knots, and the public sits half-hoping, half-fearing
that she will not be able to untie herself. It’s the
horrible possibility that that infant may have to
go through life done up in a hard knot,
that makes the performance fascinating.
There’s always a long, half-regretful sigh when
she suddenly twists herself back into the
shape and position in which God made her.
When the Flying Eugenes play rounders with
themselves away up in the dome of the tent there
is an unformed hope that the smallest Eugene
will be shot right across the horizon and come
down “ wough ! ” If there wasn’t a chance of
him coming down there would be little satisfaction
in him being up. The Simon Magus element is
supplied by the Howards, and that boss fakir
himself only got off one trick that looked cleverer
than that of the Howard Brothers —when he made
his two creamy horses gallop up from the floor of
the Coliseum through the empty air, dragging
him in his chariot behind them. Simon knocked
the Howards right out —and a just retribution
awaited him. He went up all right, but he fell
with such a bang that bits of his outfit were
scattered over the Emperor’s Sunday frock coat
and disarranged the Csesar’s top hat. Occasionally
the Roman circus frequenter saw the tamers eaten
in a tired sort of a way, and there are fine possi-
bilities about Wirths’ in that direction, too.
Mons. Ragoul seems to have his beasts fairly
under control, but Pagel’s lion is getting too big
for that large Teuton, and some night the two
will hold a sudden argument on the floor, the
result of which will greatly depend on who speaks
first.

At Sydney Lyceum “The Worst Woman
in London” is repeating her former triumphs.
People who saw her before turn up to see if she
has discovered any more villainy ; husbands who
reckon themselves ill-used turn up to get conso-
lation in the thought that it might be worse; and
it is alleged that numerous females have turned
up with notebooks, and have been kept busy
jotting down sins that they haven’t tried up to
now. So far, William Anderson has been kept
too busy finding room for these various people to
think of any change of programme.
The skilful and humorous De Wynne Bros,
are now the chief attraction at Sydney Tivoli, with
W. C. Fields, a close second. Fields does not
seem to have enough variety in his budget to
enable him to run a long season and maintain the
public interest. A new arrival at the Tivoli is the
■ old favorite, Bobby Watson, who sings a couple of
comic songs with some success. It is a pretty
; solemn thing, when you think of it, to belong to a
profession in which a grown man finds it advisable
to call himself “ Bobby.”
** * *
“ Are You a Mason 1 ” as produced at
'Melbourne Princess’s last Saturday, is the same
elegantly-furnished trifle that Sydney saw five
months ago, when the cast of characters was
slightly different. Nowadays Miss Susie Vaughan
(vice Miss Munro) plays the somewhat severe
matron, who is the most important and least un-
suspicious member of a farcical comedy family.
Mental blindness is a universal failing in “Are
You a Mason ?” Even the somewhat severe lady
seems a strangely confiding creature by compari-
son with all former mothers-in-law, and this
.abnormal trustfulness on the part of the bad old
man’s partner is the main point of originality in a
very orthodox kind of farce. The bad old man
.and his son-in-law have respectively been going
where they were not supposed to go, and doing
the things they didn’t profess to have done, and
both of them have cloaked the harmless truth
•with a fictitious apron—the apron of Freemasonry.
* * * *
The fact that they are not the Freemasons
they claim to be leads them into a number of
extravagancies; also it involves the tiresome
foolishness of an agriculturist who marries a cer-
tain Angelina, and it provides opportunity for a
casual actor-man —the typical gesticulating actor-
man of stage invention —to levy blackmail on the
son-in-law. When the mock-Mason idea is nearly
worked to death, the ‘ ‘ Charley’s Aunt ” idea is
introduced into the complications, and a third
male character disguises himself as the suppositi-
tious daughter of Angelina aforesaid. Then
the bad old man imagines that he was respon-
sible for a spurious offspring under distant
conditions of immorality which never existed, as
far as Angelina can remember, and when the
make-believe result of this phantom liaison reap-
pears in male attire, and calls himself the brother
of the girl he had previously pretended to be, the
bad old man revels in the delusion that Angelina
and himself were the joint authors of twins.
Which aspersion on Angelina’s character is too
complicated a misunderstanding to be unravelled,
so the curtain falls on the story, and the audience
goes home, remarking that the entertainment is
“funny enough.” Present scribe reckons
“A.Y’.A.M.” a funny enough farce, sufficiently
well played. George Giddens, an easy comedian
with no individuality to speak of, takes the part
of the bad old man, in an ill-fitting wig ; Cecil
Ward is the son-in-law as before; George Desmond
repeats his spry impersonation of a hard-faced
spinster ; and the ladies, other than Miss
Vaughan, are the same imported actresses who sat
around and did needlework in April last. There
is little else than needlework for them to do, and
they don’t make much of that little.
At about the time when the modern Mel-
bourne youth was getting born, Bland Holt staged
a melodrama by G. R. Sims and Wilson Barrett,
titled “The Golden Ladder.” On Saturday he
brought it to light again at the Royal, trying the
effect of this forgotten thing upon a new genera-
tion ; and the new generation found it a higher-
toned melodrama than the ordinary sensation
nightmare of later years. In the kingdom of
dramatic jerry-builders Sims is King, and Barrett
is the Archbishop. Seemingly Barrett opened
their collaboration with prayer, and directed it
with a be-eautiful text.
’Tis sorrow builds the Shining Ladder up,
Whose Golden Rounds are our Calamities.
And he prayed that the sorrowful climber in this
narrative might be a noble young curate of
missionary tendencies pursued by a persecutor.
Then Sims said—“ Let there be a comic cockney
pastrycook to lighten the darkness,” and it was
so; and the parson, the persecutor, and the pastry-
cook were the pillars of the parable. The actors,
as well as artist Brunton, get encouragement to
spread themselves thoughtfully, if not enthu-
siastically, upon the B. and S. drama. It con-
tains many “telling” situations and lines, and
the curate on the ladder is a victim of circum-
stances that come natural to the plot, anyhow.
* * * *
The persecutor (Albert Norman) is a
candid villain. In the Ist scene he warns Rev.
Walter Baker and Miss Ross to expect all
the harm he can heap upon them, and there-
after he chases them round the world, making
himself horribly unpleasant. Charles Brown and
Styan assist him to steal securities and bring
false charges, whilst the pastrycook and his wife
(Bland and Mrs. Holt) offer shelter and jelly, and
sympathy and sponge-cake, to the oppressed.
They spread jam on the golden ladder, as it were.
In the last act Bland strikes the true note of
cockney heroism when he defies a foreign adven-
turer to shift him. “I’m old—but I'm
Inglish,” cries Bland. The foreigner falls
back, staggered by the force of that remark.
Miss Brightie Smith creates a convincing
young daughter for the comic couple; in short
Brightie would be the most admirable performer
in the piece, if Walter Baker didn’t happen to be
exceptionally strong this time. Baker proclaims
his solemn determinations and utters his threats
of vengeance as though the soul of the curate
possessed him. His trouble turns his hair grey,
and brings wrinkles into his eloquent voice. And
when Baker, turning his commanding profile
towards the d.c. entrance, loads himself to the
muzzle with earnestness and pulls the trigger—
well, where is there another melodrama hero who
can go off with so impressive a bang? This
country has never seen the other man.

The Musgrove-Slapoffski situation is very-
mixed. Firstly Madame Slap, issued a writ
against Musgrove for what may be called “breach
of privilege” as regards the unwritten law
relating to “leading ladies” and leading parts.
Secondly, Musgrove has issued a writ against
Madame Slap’s, husband for breach of contract,
claiming £2,000 damages, more than double the
amount that Madame claims on her little writ.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s a drunk and disorderly
poet is still tearing the Aurora Borealis from the
heavens to make a necklace for his heart’s best
idol. Also, the six men who routed the Bur-
gundian forces with great slaughter have not ceased
to cry “Yah!” when King Louis proposes to
hang their Constable in the interests of
humor. “If I were King” appeals to the
feelings of most people, including the noisiest
and least sober. The wages of disrespectability
in France, five hundred years ago, were a cup of
wine and a pocket full of money. If you slung
treasonable verses at the lean old head of the
reigning sovereign he transferred you to Court,
clothed in white kid boots a yard long. It was a
great period for publicans and sinners, and Mel-
bourne will be rather sorry to see the last of it on
Friday. On the other hand, there is gladness in
the thought that Sherlock Holmes and the Pro-
fessor are about to resume their feud for a week.
For the last six nights of the season the Professor
will breathe his orders down thespeaking tube, and
inconvenient citizens will be removed (with, or
without, chloroform) whilst the Man of Destiny
waits in his high-backed chair.
** * *
The Mexican eagle who loops the loop at
Rickards’ is a useful bird insofar as he aids, by his
avoirdupois weight, a practical demonstration of
‘ ‘ loopage,” but in his character of star performer
the anxious captive is a delusion and a fraud.
All the other birds trained by Madame Mar-
zella go about their business on their own—or
seem to —and their air of willingness is an
attraction in the show. The eagle looks like a
dissenting party to the loop transaction, but he
preserves a majestic silence under the ordeal, and
subsequently reappears without his bicycle to con-
vince the audience that he didn’t die of fright.
Madame and the Birds are proceeding until
further notice at the Opera House, likewise Dan
Crawley, and Hallen and Fuller, who have
changed their wheelbarrow sketch for a rather
better one.
An annual agricultural show is due in
Melbourne this week, therefore some of the
theatre matinees are dated for Thursday
instead of the usual Wednesday. The influx
of fat cattle and bloated capitalists from the
interior accounts for several unusual events. A
circus which is only a first cousin to the circus
that comes at Cup time will remain at
Fitzgerald Bros.’ building until the beginning of
next week; also, something is bound to happen
at the Town Hall, and the Aquarium, and Krist-
mayer’s Waxworks. The Waxworks always puts
up specialties at holiday seasons, and its heaviest
specialty just now is a gentle Clara, who weighs
43 stone.
** * *
Hill and Sylvainy, who manage to imp;vrt
something new to the well-worn trick-cycling
business, are the chief attractions at Adelaide
Tivoli. Grace Emmett (the programme con-
sistently calls her Gracie, but there is no obvious
reason for this familiarity) wisely went back to
her best work—as the middle-aged Irish bride with
a “sure thing” in an unhealthy husband fully
insured —for the end of her season. Her small co ,
which includes a typical Gibson girl and two pretty
good men, has now moved on. Dan Barry has also
gone from the Royal, after giving a remarkable
speech on praying for rain. He stated that he
did it once and got floods, with much suffering
and loss of life; did it again and got bush-fires !
Meanwhile, he produced a large slab of crude
melodrama entitled, “When the Sea Gives Up
its Dead;” and Adelaide, which had expected
from the name something about the Day of
Judgment, received it rather coldly. William
Anderson has now come along with the “Death
of Nelson,” including “ Hearts of Oak ” and other
naval curiosities of great antiquity.

Messrs. Gillespie Bros, and Co., of the Anchor
Roller Flour Mills, Sydney, have placed an order with
The Austral Otis Engineering’ Company, of South
Melbourne, for the construction of a modern semi tandem
horizontally coupled triple expansion surface condensing
engine. The engine will be capable of indicating 450
horsepower ; the cylinders being 12", 19" and 30" diam.
respectively, with a stroke of 48 inches, and will be fitted
with specially designed inlet and exhaust steam valves
on the Sultzer principle. This engine will form another
excellent example of modern high class engine construc-
tion to the credit of the builders, who have already
attained a well deserved reputation throughout the
Commonwealth for design and construction of this class
of work.*

Scottish Critic, October, 1902, writes re the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society Limited :—“ The
Company was established in 1873 and the management
has always been characterised by abundant enterprise,
tempered with the prudence which can alone make
energy valuable in a life assurance company.”*
* * * *
British manufacturers have been beaten iii an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

Thousands of pounds are yearly thrown away
because insufficient care is taken to save the extreme
fines and slimes in concentrating mills. There is only
one ore dressing machine which is unquestionably entitled
to first place as the best and surest means of saving this
material. We refer to the Luhrig Vanners, which have
proved their superiority on all leading mining fields and
may be seen daily in use at the gold, silver, lead, zinc,
tin, copper and other mines where up-to-date ore dressing
is in vogue. Messrs W. and J. Lempriere, of Melbourne
and Sydney, are the Sole Agents, and will be pleased to
give full particulars on application.*

To empty old abandoned shafts, or to cope with
inrushes of water at a minimum cost, may be effected by
the use of the Vertical Plunger Sinking Pumps, manufac-
tured by The A. S. Cameron Steam Pump Works of New
York ; and represented by Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, who hold stocks of these and various other
sizes and styles of steam or air pumps. These pumps are
distinct from any other make of so-called “Cameron’
Pump. They work automatically, and have no dead
centre, neither have they springs nor outside valve gear
to become deranged. They occupy less space in a shaft
than any other Sinking Pump of same capacity.*

* * * *
The Great Cobar deal is practically completed.
In the absence of details the terms of flotation in London
are incomprehensible. It is evident that the promoters
could not raise the whole of the capital in cash, and that
there was even great difficulty in getting the amount
requisite to pay for the properties, and provide the work-
ing capital which Mr. Kendall says is necessary to alter
the system of working. There is a debenture issue of
£700,000, bearing 6£ per cent. As the remainder of the
capital is necessary for the purposes stated above, it is
to be assumed that the option syndicate is taking its
purchase consideration in debentures, and, also, that a
large amount of debentures have been issued to under-
writers as bonuses. This method of financing the scheme
may be compulsory, and it may be satisfactory to the
syndicators, but it must severely handicap the mines by
making a big hole in the profits.

This year the directors of this magnificent
monopoly have some more mournful remarks to make
regarding the “continued falling-off of business”; still,
in view of the fact that on the shareholders’ actually paid-
up £250,000 a profit of £33 11s. 7d. per cent has been
made, they are implored to bear up, and remember that
things might have been worse—some people find it hard
work to make even 30 per cent. Certainly the M.T. and
U Co. 8 is a wasting security ; in 1916 the property goes
out of its hands. But even now, after these shockingly
bad times through which it has been passing for years,
during which it has never paid more than 24 per cent,
dividend, it could make the writer a present of the whole
concern, pay its debts, and still have something over
£1 9s. for every £1 of cash or £1 worth of horse and ’bus
and tram that it put into the business.

Teature is that the directors announce that “no

pretty considerable for a co. with a paid capital
only £so,ooo—an average of 11J per cent.
• A,,d 4hat P rofit haa b een made

last years profits were equal to £l2 12s. 9d. percent.

* • • •
N.S.W. Public Works Committee has come to a
decision with regard to the proposed supplementary
water supply for Broken Hill. Only two schemes found
supporters ; the proposal to complete the Umberumberka
weir and provide Broken Hill with another distinct
service had three votes, while the proposal to give the
present co. an extension of time on its undertaking to add
to its drainage area by diverting the waters of Yancowinna
Creek had four votes. The latter proposal has therefore
been recommended to Parliament. It has one quality
which will appeal to the Government: the Treasury
won’t have to find any cash in order to carry it
out. The length of the time-extension shall, it
is proposed, be determined by actuarial calculation.
The scheme recommended is the simplest and least
expensive of all those that have been mentioned ; on the
other hand, it is the least satisfactory. Apart from the
fact that it involves an extension of the private monopoly
of water supply (and it is generally agreed that water
supply, at any rate, ought to be under public control),
there is a strong objection to the diversion of more water
into the present polluted reservoir. Further, from its
extreme flatness, the present reservoir is not suitable for
holding a large quantity of water ; the loss by evapora-
tion in that climate is enormous. In that respect the
Umberumberka scheme has an advantage ; the water
would be confined in a comparatively small area, and the
loss would therefore be minimised. The proposal
adopted by the Works Committee will certainly give much
less satisfaction to Broken Hill than any of the others.
Locally, connection with the Darling is for the moment
the most favored. That, however, would probably cost
about £250,000, and the cost of maintenance would also
be heavy. Moreover, the present co would compete with
it for the mines supply, at any rate. No doubt it would
give a much more permanent supply than any other; but
it is economically impossible, and the only result that is
likely to follow an agitation in its favor is the hanging-up
of a decision in the whole matter.

A year ago it was decided to take counsel’s opinion as to
whether it was lawful for shareholders to have a bite at
the profits, and counsel said it was. It was not a very
large bite that followed—only about lj per cent.—but
still it was more than had been eiarned during the year,
and,, in fact, nearly five times more if the losses on
property sales are taken into account This year the
profits are better ; but, as the improvement is mainly the
result of spending about £2OO less than usual on repairs,
it can’t be depended upon to continue. In fact, the
society’s one source of revenue—interest and receipts
from properties- returned less in 1903 than in 1902. Pro-
fits even at the enhanced rate were equal to only £ll3s.
6d. per cent, on the paid capital (£57,777); obviously
much severe writing-down is still waiting to be done.
The assets consist almost wholly of properties acquired by
foreclosure and still taken in at boom-time book-value ;
and very little is being done (for the good reason, no
doubt, that very little can be done) in the way of realis-
ing. Progress in this respect in recent years has gone
like this :
Loans and Fore- Realised
closed Properties. during year.

In seven years property to the extent of £5342 has been
realised, and in the same period liabilities have been re-
duced by £5443. But whereas seven years ago there was
a credit cash balance of £562, on the payment of the pre-
sent dividend there will be a debit balance of £96. If the
shareholders are willing to wait long enough it looks as
though they may get their money back by the slow
realisation of properties ; but it is fairly plain that thev
can’t hope to do that and draw even small dividends into
the bargain.

The annual meeting of the Australian Star,
held last week, was not a very cheerful function.
Adverse comment as to the neglect of directors to carry
out the suggestions of the previous annual meeting cul-
minated in what was practically a vote of censure on the
directorate. This was lost on the casting vote of the
chairman. The adoption of the report was then carried
by a majority of three A poll was demanded, but the
chairman refused to sanction it. The dissatisfied share-
holders speak now of calling a special meeting to deal
with the situation.
* * * *
The Queensland Agricultural Bank completed
its first year’s work on June 30th, Under its Act it is
entitled to raise £250,000 “ for the purpose of promoting
the occupation, cultivation, and improvement of the
agricultural lands of the State ”; but, as advances may
only be made on improvements, and then to no more than
13s. in the £ of their value, the full amount is not likely
to be called for for a good many years. In the 12 months
267 applications were received and 177 were put through ;
the others have either been refused, hung up, or with-
drawn. The 177 represent £22,612, of which so far,
however, only £6253 has been paid. The experience
with regard to interest payments has been unsatis-
factory ; of £177 due only £79 had been paid on June
30th. A further £45 was received within the en-
suing seven days of grace. Interest is payable at the
rate of 5 per cent, per annum for five years; after
that a half-yearly payment of £4 os. 3d. per cent, for
20 years will extinguish the debt. Expenses of manage-
ment (including contingencies) amounted to 1617, or at
the rate of £9 17s. 4d. per cent, of the advances. On
the other hand, valuation and other fees realised £7lB,
more than covering the expenses, which, no doubt, to
some extent, were of a preliminary and special character.

The paid capital is £14,500. As in most brewery busi-

feature disclosed is the substantial writing-down of plant,
&c.; but, even allowing for that, the 5 per cent, dividend
is not at all justified.

during the half-year to June 30th, after writing off
£4493 for depreciation of plant. For some past few half-
years its record has been :

The “ gross profit ” above is the profit less the variable
deduction for wear and tear. The surplus of liquid assets
over current liabilities at the end of the half-year was
£38,918 (this is after payment of the half-year’s dividend),
as against £23,287 six months previously. The least
satisfactory feature disclosed is the rather serious
reduction in the percentages of metals recovered from
the ore by concentration. This reduction has, moreover,
been accompanied by a fall in the metallic contents of
the ore; the average was 15'96 p.c. lead and 8‘450z.
silver against 17 72 lead and 7'46 oz. silver in the previous
six months. The total quantity of metal recovered in
the concentrates was 9061 tons lead and 301,8700 z. silver.
The gross profit, as stated, was £25,504, or at the rate of
£2 16s. 3d per ton of the lead. The average price for
lead has been £lll9s Bd. ; for silver, 2s. Id. With silver
stationary at that price, the South can thus produce lead
at £9 3s. sd, but without making any allowance for wear
and tear of plant. The co. has, however, been re-treating
large quantities of cheaply-handled old tailings ; when
these are exhausted it is doubtful whether it will be able
to show such good results. As to mine prospects, it is
now estimated that above the 800 ft. level there are 1,100,000
tons of ore available; and the ore bodies were generally
widening between the 700 ft. and the 800 ft. level. At the
present rate of hauling, the quantity named would last
nearly nine years.

The Broken Hill mines have a long way to go
yet before they reach anything like perfection in the
treatment of their refractory sulphides, although they
have now been working on them for six or seven years,
and have spent in the aggregate hundreds of thousands
on experiments and plants. The B.H. South ore, though-
hard. is less refractory than most; yet, in spite of
extremely careful management, less than a third of the
market value of the metallic contents is as yet recovered.
Here is that mine’s record for 12 months :
Lead. Silver. Zinc.
Ore contents .. 28,200 tons I 1,179,4680 z. 28,652 tons
Recovered.. .. 19,335 ~ 605,241,, Nil.
Unrecovered .. 8,865 „ j 574,227 ~ 28,652 „
Or expressed in terms of approximate sterling value :
Lead. Silver. Zinc. Total.
Ore contents.. £324,300 £122,861 £587,366 £1,034,527
Recovered .. 222,352 63,046 Nil. 285,398
Unrecovered. 101,948 59,815 587,366 749,129
Thus only 27-7 per cent, of the metal values is at present
recovered. Certainly, in addition, a very small percent-
age of zinc is included in the silver-lead concentrates;
but it is valueless there, and, indeed, detracts from the
value of the other metals. What dividends the South is
paying (and it is a typical Broken Hill mine) are therefore
being won on a recovery of just over a fourth of the
metals ; the other three-fourths (nearly) represent the
field still open to the metallurgist and the engineer. The
zinc in the ore is worth more than the silver and lead put
together; and yet, up to within the last few months,
nothing whatever was done with it, and even now only
the smallest beginnings have been made upon this branch
of the industry.
* * * *
A couple of moderate showers have at last fallen
in the right place at Broken Hill, and there is now thre#
or four months’supply of water in the reservoir and the
mines’ dams. It isn’t much, but it’s something to go on
with, and other showers may happen within four months
even at Broken Hill. The famine lasted two months and
a day or two. Even, however, with water available, three
of the mines—the Junctidn, the North, and the Junction
North—will not be able to resume work on account of
the low price of lead.
* * * *
Myall’s United (near Peak Hill, N.S.W.) shows
some remarkable results, but theyare not of the sort that
are likely to please shareholders, although the3 r may be
satisfactory to the disinterested practical man. Last
report published states that 2647 tons of ore were treated
during the month for 5390 z. of gold, and that there was
a profit of £245. The stone is worth about 15s. per ton,
and that it is mined at a depth of 600 ft., hauled, crushed,
amalgamated, and cyanided at a profit of Is. 9d. per ton
are facts which reflect much credit on the capable
manager—Mr. James Hocking. He is one of the few able
men engaged in the business in N.S.W., and deserves to
have charge of a better mine.
* * * *
London papers of the 23rd July apprised share-
holders in the Cobar Gold Mines (old Port Bourke) that
the June return was £7852 and all expenses £5400,
showing a surplus of £2452, and that the return for
May was £6553. Australian shareholders have to wait
a very long time before the lingering Bull parts with
information. The opinion previously expressed—that it
will take all the profits to be made out of the oxidised
lode to make provision for the treatment of the ore in
the cupriferous zone—is reiterated. Shareholders may
not like the prospect, but the only alternative is a div.
to-day and a call to-morrow.

State age and experience.
Apropos, a lady, said to be the daughter of an
Anglican parson, used to have a regular advt. in
certain London papers to the effect that she was
prepared to call and flog young ladies at their own
residences in town or country. She charged 7s. Gd.
for consultation with the fond parent or guardian,
and 15s. for the assault. She explained to an
interviewer that her chief business lay among
young ladies from 16 to 21, who were rather large
for their parents to handle; but she had called
upon and spanked them up to the age of 24. Her
connection, she said, lay mostly among the “ best
society.”
THE BRUTE.
“What would you do if I died? ”
“ Oh, I don't know—same as you would, I suppose."
“You wretch I And you used to tell me that you would never marry again."
N.S.W. Agent-General Copeland has come
back weeping (in a Sydney Telegraph interview)
about that alleged family which was coming to
Australia, but didn’t come because Copeland told
the old gentleman that he couldn’t, under the Im-
migration laws, bring his old family servants with
him. If Copeland really did tell anybody
that item of pernicious rot, then he ought
to be sacked from his billet with ignominy.
The fine old English gentleman certainly couldn’t
bring his servants here bound by contract, but
unless he was a very bad character indeed they
would have accepted his assurance that they would
get a formal engagement after landing here, if
they wanted one. There was no difficulty in
the way—unless these servants knew that
their employer couldn’t be trusted. The Federal
Government should ask Copeland to explain
exactly what he said about the Act, and unless
the explanation is a great deal more promising
than seems likely, it should advertise in London
that it isn’t responsible for Copeland's mis-
representations.
Victorian Chief Justice Madden's habit of
talking at large lately led a respectable, hard-work-
ing citizen into trouble. The citizen (M‘Culloch,
of the Age) reported the Chief-Justice as saying
in one of his loose and whirling lectures :
All intelligent foreigners who visited Australia were
struck with the vulgar and unintelligible chatter of
women and their graceless carriage in the streets.
The fluent and waxed Chief Justice no sooner
saw this in print than he denied having said it,
and the Age meanly sacked its representative and
blamed him in a printed p iragraph for his “erro-
neous report.” Then the reporter sued the Age
for damages, and Victoria saw its Chief Justice’s
beautiful moustache sticking out of the witness-
box as he denied the correctness of M‘Cul-
loch’s report. And yet the chances are
that the Chief did, quite unconsciously,
use the words attributed to him. A re-
porter is almost always a cold, unimpassioned
machine, taking down what he hears. A speaker
is sometimes a fatally fluent Irish gentleman
whose eloquence has bolted with him, and who is
rushed down a steep place by his own beautiful
language. A man may get just as drunk on elo-
quence as he can on whisky, and be just as un-
conscious afterwards of what he said. No loosely-
corked gentleman like Mr. Madden should be too
positive as to what he said in some moment when
he let himself go—in fact, he shouldn’t let himself
go. Then he wouldn’t strike depressing snags
like that lie is impaled upon now, for the jury
decided, in effect, that the Clhef-Justice had
unconsciously used the words which he denied
using, and decided that the reporter was libelled
in having his report described as incorrect.
The country pays a judge to be an aloof and awe-
some personage on a bench, and, when he wanders
abroad to give views on extraneous subjects, he is
liable to fall in, and indirectly bring the law into
contempt before he gets out.

A man has been committed for trial at
Newcastle, N.S.W., charged with having made a
false statement, in burying his paramour, that slio
was his wife. It seems to be a case that might be
well met with a nolle prosequi. The desire of the
man to shield his old woman’s reputation on the
grave-stone was a manly sentiment and might
excuse his lie.

L.O.L.’s are protesting wildly against any
demonstration in connection with the celebration
of the Robert Emmett Centenary, which occurs
this month. The L.O.L.’s, like many other
ignorant people, are under the impression that
the leaders of the ’9B rebellion were R.C.’s. As
a matter of fact, most of the men of note were
Presbyterians or Church of England people.

| An undertaker is an enthusiast in the pomp and
circumstance of an interment. His professional
pride in the achievement of a beautiful polished
oaken coffin, price 60 guineas, is something that
even an ordinarily snobbish chief mourner can
not quite understand. I have known an under-
taker to turn pale when he found that a lovely
coffin, with the corpse inside it, was to be confined
to the privacy of a bed-room until called for. He
thought the proper place for such a noble piece of
furniture was the drawing room.—O.K.
* » * *
The latest news regarding that strange
discovery or myth or joke about the “Jennings
millions ” is that a certain Jennings “lays claim
to the baronetcy.” This sort of thing makes the
story more and more tangled. A previous
narrative was to the effect that the original
Jennings died in the fifteenth century or there-
abouts, and his property went into Chancery, and,
owing to the utter contempt for riches displayed
by his descendants, it has lain there ever since and
accumulated at compound interest until it now
amounts to £6,000,000 or so. Quite apart from the
fact that recent, returns showed the total unclaimed
moneys in Chancery to be just about a fraction of
the alleged Jennings pile, the baronetcy is a dis-
turbing influence. If the Jennings fortune went
into Chancery and the Jennings family went under
at the alleged date, which was a century or two
before baronetcies were invented, how comes a
baronetcy to be mixed up with the business?
Certainly no big event of this kind has been
complete without a baronetcy, especially since the
Tiehborne time, but on the surface of things it
looks incongruous. Perhaps the baronetcy was
thrown in by Chancery as interest on the money.
• * * *
Daylesford has started a quite lively
agitation for an improved train service. One of
the oldest and pleasantest of the settled districts
in Victoria, poor little Daylesford is about the
least considered by the Railway Department. For
years the mid-day train from Melbourne has been
a languid joke. It leaves the marvellous metropolis
at 11.30 a.m. or thereabouts. Passengers for
Daylesford are turned out to cool at Woodend,
where they are presently hitched on to a wood
train which brings them to the end of their 80
miles journey any time between 5.15 and 6.30 p.m.
—say six hours for the 80 miles. The locals inflict
a moss-grown “ wheeze ” upon the unsuspecting
stranger. They tell him how the ruminative
official in char ,e at Bullarto, the last long stopping
place before the engine puffs onward to Davles-
ford, is “the highest paid station-master in
Victoria,” the explanation of this excruciatingly
funny remark lying in the fact that Bullarto has a
prouder elevation than any other shunting ground.
Passengers are expected to while away the time
by sniffing mountain air from the open window of
a narrow worm-eaten carriage.
* * * *
The Maoiiland Supreme Court has just
wrestled with another problem in connection
with the State’s fearful and wonderful licensing
law. A man was on a bicycle journey on the
Sabbath, and after having travelled many dry
miles called in somewhere for a refresher. He
said he had “arrived from a journey,” and the
local magistrate said he hadn’t—that he was still
on a journey, and therefore that, in getting a
drink, he was an enemy to his country’s laws,
and so was the publican. It took the whole five
Supreme Court judges to say what “arrived from
a journey ” meant. Two of them thought that,
so far as the-publican was concerned (and it was
he who was prosecuted), the man had arrived
from a journey ; he couldn’t tell whether the man
was going on another journey or not. The other
three, including the Chief, said that from where
the traveller left to his final stopping place was
only one journey, and that he couldn’t arrive
from that journey before he had finished it ; and
as there were three judges against two, that rule
prevailed. Practically this altogether knocks
out Sunday trading so far as travellers are
concerned ; for the only man who can get a drink
is the man who has pulled up at a pub and
intends to stay there for life.

THE GHOSTS THAT NEVER WALKED.
GREAT BUT NEGLECTES TRAGEDIAN: "Yes.
Oswald, dear boy, I was once superstitious
and believed in ghosts; but, dear boy, that was
before I entered the profession—years before! ”
In Pitt-street., Sydney, near the City Bank,
a new building is going up on the site of the old
Shakspeare Hotel, a noted house in its time.
Nearly 60 years ago—in 1847, to be precise—Tom
Stubbs, the Australian Robins, offered the pro-
perty by public auction. He styled it “a real
English freehold,” producing then =£3l2 a year
rent. Tom Stubbs’ auction-room was in King-
street, about the present Arcade entrance. It had;
been Blackman’s, and, after Stubbs, was occupied;
by Andrew Allen, a political auctioneer. Stubbs;
went to Melbourne in the late 40’s, and was a;
successful auctioneer. He was also a corn poser of
no mean ability and a good flautist. Miss Maud
Fitzstubbs is a granddaughter. The Shakspeare
had many notable tenants. One in particular,
Thomas Spencer, was the first to introduce electric
“shocks” into his business. A metal rod ran
round the counter, and the battery was connected
therewith. The unsophisticated inhabitants
thought they had beerf flicked by the devil, who
was very active in those days.
MY LADY’S TEETH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My lady’s teeth, like ivory,
Flash whitely as she smiles on me.
And ah ! the heaven which then I know,
Suffused with pleasing passion’s glow,
When she smiles thus bewitchingly.
What charm makes my heart sing with glee
When ’twixt her rosy lips do show
Those pearly gems in sparkling row—
My lady’s teeth 1
But, ah ! that Beauty should not be
Entirely Nature’s artistry !
That there are times the glance I throw
Beyond those red lips may not go—
When in a basin I can see
My lady’s teeth I

The sky is blue, and nothing grieves

A-down the meadows in a stream,
Thro’ Cape flower—gold as Dame’s dream ;

Across the fence the guelder rose
Sways to the breeze’s idle touch,
Whilst lilac breathes, and blooms, and blows.
Why must I suffer, God, so much ?
The sparrow hops beneath the tree—
The tree with sunshine in its leaves ;
The sky is blue and nothing grieves
Save 1 .;.. my mate is < ns from me!

Troubles.
B. R. Wise, in an amendment to the
Girls’ Protection Bill in N.S.W. Legislative
Council, has done something far more practical
against child vice than any age-of-consent law
could effect. This amendment provides that any
person who harbors in a bad house a girl between
the ages of 12 and 17 shall be liable to five years
penal servitude; and any person residing in or
frequenting the house is liable to the same penalty.
That should clear away most of the evil of child
prostitution.

Kew, where Malkum M‘Eacharn, the
anti-white Australian, plays the host to Prime
Minister Barton, is the prettiest and most
peaceful of Melbourne suburbs. Its inhabi-
tants are a subdued and quietly disposed
crowd. In Kew there is a lunatio asylum,
an inebriates’ institute, and a cemetery;
and the Kewite can never tell when ha
may bump into an escaped lunatic or a
runaway-drunk pursued by bucketfuls
of snakes, or a casual ghost from the
burial ground.
Another legacy from the war.
In the London Lancet, Professor
Whittles describes a cheerful para-
site which causes thickening of the
gums, andd isfiguring protrusion of
the upper lip. He has come across
a lot of these cases lately, and believes
that the parasite (which he is ap-
parently rather pleased with, and
has named after himself) has been
“ brought from South Africa by re-
turning troops.” The patients are
mostly women ! Australian girls do
not show any special desire to kiss the
returned cohentingenter nowadays ; but
in case any of them want to kiss him
when his sentence expires, here is one more reason
for not doing it.
* * * *
The Adamson divorce petition, just dis-
missed by Victorian Justice Hood, was an affair
with amusing side-lights. The co-respondent—
the eternal lodger—seems to have had a pretty
exciting time all the way through. It suddenly
dawned on the husband one day that his wife and
the co. were on very friendly terms, whereupon
“ he spoke to her seriously about it ” ; also he had
a .few words with the lodger about it, but allowed
him to stay in the house. Twice he broke up his
home in the hope of breaking off this undue in-
timacy, but the pestilent lodger, who never paid
for his board, always turned up again and dwelt
with the family, like a ghost or a drought. The
husband admitted he had serious thoughts of
getting him by the scruff of the neck and running
him violently out, but thought better of it, as the
co was reputed to be handy with his fists, though
only a drapers assistant. He once, however,
really lost his temper, and called in a constable to
eject the co., but cooled down after a while and
actually slept that night in the same bed with
him, his wife having put him there; “but,” said
he, I didn’t speak to him.” In a casual sort of
way the petitioner admitted having misconducted
himself more or less frequently, also the co. owned
up to being a married man, though he had never
lived with his wife owing to her having miscon-
ducted herself with another co.—the strange co-
respondents who creep sideways into a ease like
tins are numerous. Justice Hood ordered the hus-
band to pay his and his wife’s costs, and the co.
to pay his own costs, and discoursed at great
length, and left things at that. The Law is very
learned and elaborate, but it doesn’t settle diffi-
culties worth a cent.

CONFESSING THE OBVIOUS.
WIFE OF HIS BOSOM : “ Where have you been all
night, you brute?”
RESPECTABLE CITIZEN: “ There, I knew you’d
never guess. But I’ll be honest wish you, my
love. Fact ish, I’ve been out on a tear.”
In a bigamy case at the late criminal sit-
tings in Adelaide, John Downer, K.C., for the
defence, tried to do away with the first marriage
by picking a hole in the old Government pro-
clamation under which the officiating clergyman
acted. The horrified Bundey pointed to a general
validating clause, but more than hinted that he
would, in any case, refuse to consider a technical
point which would make of no effect most of the
marriages celebrated in the State for years past.
Parson Hosking, a Collingwood (Vic.)
gospel dispenser and a vigorous Prohibitionist,
is said to have forwarded the following letter to
R. H. Lemon, secretary to Victorian Licensed
Victuallers' Association:
Sir, I am astonished at your statement, that I seriously
damage the reputation of the liquor trade. Why, I would
damage it any hour if I could, and send the whole traffic
to the devil. It is the most damnable traffic on the face
of God’s earth at the present time. It is blasting the
homes, crushing the childhood, womanhood and manhood
of the State. It is impossible for me or anyone else to
slander the liquor trade. It is a dirty traffic, a traffic in
slavery of the worst kind, a God-hating, soul-destroying
traffic, a menace to the health, life and morals of the
people. To slander such a traffic is impossible. My
prayer to God is, “ damn the liquor traffic, and send it
back to hell 1 ” Wishing you and your association every
success in trying to stir the mud and stench of this traffic,
yours, etc., John Hoskins.
If a man can use language like that on cold tea
what a noble bullocky he would make after two
rums!

its historical spots “ with view of above,” and the
fellow who wants a female of uncertain age, repre-
sent the extremes of sentiment.

M.L. Government is out with a brand
new proposal in regard to the social derelicts of
the community. They are to be shifted north,
when raked in by the police, made to fell hush,
and transform the rugged scenery into vegetable •
gardens, the produce to be sent to Government
institutions. The idea of the average physical
street wreck battling with the mighty forest is
humorsome, but he should be able to transplant
cabbages and pick gooseberries all right.

Victorian Justice Hood has been getting
off a lot of superheated steam (much of it quite
justified) about husbands deserting their wives.
The matter cropped up while he was in the
Divorce Court, and he endorsed the opinion of
brother Justice A’Beckett, expressed some time
ago, that it would be a good thing to make wife-
desertion criminal. _ “If men and women,” he
said, “ could be criminally punished for a violation
of their duty they would think twice before they
offended.” That is very pretty, of course, but in
some cases there is no doubt that, although she
remains in his house, the woman has, in fact, de-
serted the man long before he clears out. She
makes things as unpleasant for him as she can,
and there is no living thing that can create half so
much unpleasantness in a house as a healthy
woman. Poor old Solomon knew considerable
about women, and in his bittetness he once wrote,
“ It is better to dwell in the corner of the house-
top than with a brawling female in commodious
premises,” or words to that effect. -Most men’s
houses are not very: wide, and the Australian roof
slopes too much for a man to.dwell on it when- lie
comes home from work, so the owner of the lady-
described by Solomon moves -on and leaves the
other party on her own. Then the divorce comes
in. due course, and sour Judges make remarks
about the absentee. If nagging, and the un-
pleasant habit of periodically digging up the home
skeleton and throwing its bones at the other
party, could be made criminal offences, then it
would be absolutely fair to make desertion
criminal also. The only thing a woman can
always throw straight is the family skeleton. As
a shot with it she is an unprecedented success.
* * * *
There were humorous points about Vic-
torian Chief Justice Madden’s appearance in
the witness-box in the case of M'Culloch v.
Syrne. The hand of the Judge’s associate shook
visibly as he handed , the Bible to the august
witness. The C.J. looked as uncomfortable as
the average man in a similar position. The second
question, “What is your occupation?” was un-
expected, and his Waxcellency gave a start and
answered stammeringly, “ What ?—er—ah—oh—
Chief Justice.” Then His Wax. was asked if he
had used any such phrase as “the vulgar and un-
intelligent chatter of the Australian women, and
their graceless carriage in the streets,” and lie re-
plied withafineshowofindignationthathe had said
no such thing, although he admitted that he might
have said the women walked badly and talked
badly. He believed that on the occasion under
notice he spoke possibly for a quarter-of-an-
hqur, though he would be “sorry to contradict ”
anyone in the audience who was prepared to say
that he spoke for 45 minutes. Thereupon the
C.J. proceeded to tell an unoffending court what
he really intended to say, and the exposition
lasted for six or seven minutes of desperate volu-
bility. But the C.J. was badly broken up when
he was asked if lie had noticed.the position of Mr.
Beale —“the man who works the limelight.”
“ The limelight man—l don’t know him ! ” snorted
the C.J. in a voice which seemed to suggest that
lie had been asked for particulars about a cock-
roach.

The Victorian railway authorities joked
cruelly at the expense of the Melbourne Age re-
porters who journeyed to Nhill the other day with
Premier Irvine, by accommodating them in the
identical car in which Tren.with was accused of
having “luxuriated” when Minister of Railways.
This car, built more than a generation ago, is
about the saddest old rattletrap now running on
the Victorian lines, and one or two of the press-
men were so shaken that they suffered from some-
thing akin to sea sickness Those who had a hand
in reviling Trenwith for his alleged luxury .must
have been badly stricken with remorse for the
lies they told about plain Bootmaker William.
* * * *
A year or two ago some meaningless aid
needless ceremony connected with the estab-
lishment of the Commonwealth took place in a
shoddy pavilion at Centennial Park, Sydney.
Then the usual active member cadged the pavilion
as a trophy for his electorate, and got it removeil
there and set up in Cabarita Park, and because
neither the blastiferous cadging member i or the
proud electorate would pay the expenses of
removal, the Government gave £lOO out of the
impoverished pi. 1 c revenue. So now the
building where the thing happened is away up
the Parramatta River, and the site where it
happened is at Centennial Park, for the ener-
getic and cadging member quite forgot to ask the
Go\ .r.iment to dig up the site and shift it also at
public expense. (It wouldn’t have been much of a
job; if the earth had been removed to a depth of
four feet that would probably have been as far as
the associations had soaked in.) So the active
local member, whose seat looked rather shaky
before the pavilion happened, will probably get
back all right at next election. And up at
Cabarita. Park the; shoddy and bathetic pavilion
is standing the weather reasonably well so far,
and will probably last a year or two, if the goats
don’t lean against it.
* * * *
N.S.W-. Board of Health has warned
Premier See that, owing to the fact that a large
proportion of the population is unvaccinated, his
State is in grave danger of endemic smallpox,
and that this danger, unless precautions are taken,
will, sooner or later, culminate in disaster. The
Board recommends systematic vaccination in
infancy ; re-vaccination on arriving at puberty ;
establishment of a station for the preparation of
lymph, and the passing of a Compulsory Vacci-
nation Act. N.S.W. is the only State in the
Commonwealth that does not possess such an Act.

■ Almost, every week now, at one or other
of jhe‘Melbourne courts, some hapless widow is
refused State aid for her fatherless children and
thrown on the resources of the charitable. The
Iceberg crowd has sweated the old pensioners to
the last gasp, has cut down its charity dole to the
last grudging half-penny, has robbed its servants,
and maintains its imane little better than savage
beasts, and, as if that were not enough, it now
throws starving orphans to the wolves—and for
what? To save the rich Melbourne landlords
from paying land-tax.
“ D'Harum " : More of the S. A. soldiers
who went to the war full of patriotism, and re-
turned full of beer, have appeared in court, with
the usual charges against their names —vagrancy,
drunkenness, or the customary demand for the ss,
a week for the support of the infant. In almost
every case the offenders were the loafers who
shipped to S’Africa for Horstralia’s good with the
last eohentingents. Apparently, Australia’s
genuine “ patriotism ” died out early. The first
cohentingent seems to have .been respectable.
The later ones were mostly bummers, skunks and
incendiaries.

SOMETHING FOR JOHN NORTON TO
CONTEMPTATE.
“ An interesting’ question was discussed by the Liberal
and Refoiqn delegates— viz., Should a woman vote for
immoral politicians or virtuous opponents?
Trustees of the N.S.W. Public Library,
in their last report, complain again of the failure,
of the Government to make any provision for the
Mitchell Donation. Mr. Mitchell lias presented
to the State a collection of Australian books,
valued at £lOO,OOO, and a big cash sum to provide
for the care of the volumes, stipulating only that
the Government shall house them suitably and
make them accessible to students. And the
Government, so far, shows no intention of doing
anything but keep the books in a cellar. But
Sydney University was able to get a big grant to
build its new library. What is the unworthy
motive at the back of the Government’s refusal
to open up the Mitchell treasures to the public ?

THE EXODUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T> ARM AID, going Ohjna early September, like to
hear of another lady. East, G.P.O.—S.M.
HeraLd (4/8/’O3).
There’s a maiden of the bar going forth
To the land of Confucius in the North,
Throws the dull old world behind ; let it rip.
Wants a mate (a lady, mind !) —partnership.
For the Chow is calling, calling
For a girl to fill his can
And the brown man’s dry and bawling
For beer in old Japan.
So a damsel bound for Asia is hastening away
To sell grog unto Confucius in the land of old Cathay.
There languid Chinese Johnnies breast the bar
And call for milk and soda, or three star.
And like a sun there beams the mandarin,
And the greasy pigtail gleams in Pekin.
And to help the white girl land,
Up there by the Yellow Sea,
Is Asia’s claw-like hand,
Extended on the quay.
S ° ”°„ one P ack her bandbox, write to “East” at
(jr. P.O. f
For the Chow is dry and anxious in C'a iton and n
Ningpo.

THE POINT OF VIEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Jew is cursed in many a land,
Because of old he took a hand
In heat of blind conviction,
In lifting up a thing of wood—
(Ye hear it called the Holy Rood)
To do a gruesome deed of blood—
That awful Crucifixion !
And 3’et the Jews may justly plead—•
Although they sinned—not all their deed
Deserved the curse of Ages ;
For when in hate and wicked pride
A spotless Saint they crucified,
They nailed a brace of thieves beside
And paid the Devil’s wages.
The Gentile’s hand hath ever thrown
At Jacob’s race a ready stone —
Hath spat upon and spurned it;
But Jacob’s seed may yet reply :
“ Two-thirds of them we nailed on high
And haled a felon’s death to die
Were felons who had earned it.
“ But ye— your thieves—they prance in pride,
They strut abroad at festal tide,
And flout the gallows gaily ;
And ye—who say the Christ we slew
Was Prophet pure and Teacher true—
Oh Hebrew-hating Christian crew,
Ye crucify Him daily ! ”
Q.
