A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 1,1903,
Mr Dear Arini,—
Mis 3 A. G. Murphy, the first of Australian
women journalists to try her luck in London, is
in Sydney on a visit after an absence of seven
years. Through her newspaper work she has
been to a considerable extent the means of
Australian Society keeping in touch with the
Australian colony in London. On her way
through the American States hither, Miss
Murphy secured an engagement for Miss Marie
Narelle at £l5O per week. This information will be
received with a gasp by a group of local birds
who are spreading their wings for homeward
flight. But Narelle’s engagers will not regret
their bargain. New York and Chicago halls,
filled with audiences 4000 to 6000 strong (mostly
named Patrick and Michael, not to speak of
Eileen and Norah), will frantically welcome
Narelle’s “Wearin’ o’ the Green” and “Kil-
larney.” Persons who called upon Melba
during her stay in Sydney and who “couldn’t
make it out ” why she didn’t receive
them, would understand if they could hear our
visitor mention the style in which Melba lives in
London. Does the Sopranie Majesty deign to re-
ceive you ? Then two flunkeys, more gorgeous than
a sun-burst, stand one at either side of the swing
doors and roll them wide with an air of ushering
you before the Great White Throne itself. Why,
Lady Londonderry and other grand dames bow
over Melba’s hand and kiss it. No wonder
Madam was mad at the “ Who be you, Mum ? ” of
the Tasmanian waiting-maid and at the casuals
who strolled up to call upon her. No wonder she
had dining-room walls knocked down and ordered
dining-tables and engagements to be lengthened
or shortened as she capriced.
By the way, Eva Mylott, writing to a Sydney
daily, says Melba is sending her for a short course
of study to Tosti. The “Good-Bye” man is a
great chum of Melba’s and often plays her.accom-
paniments when she sings his songs.
Speaking of Ada Crossley’s concerts, Miss
Murphy mentions that they will take a recital
form—the contralto will sing several numbers at
one standing. As Miss Crossley has been pro-
nounced by topmost authorities the greatest living
warbler of sacred songs, it is to be hoped she will
treat us liberally in that direction. Concerning
Ada’s command to sing before Edward and
Alexandra, it transpires that the commandeered
gets no fee. For singing at a State concert a
singer receives a large one, but the former honor
is the greater of the two. Since present King’s
accession only two singers, Melba and Crossley,
have been commanded. As for the Australian
quartette which is cabled as having sung before
the King at Cowe3, it simply warbled at a concert
there, which the King attended. Herr Jacobs is
the violinist accompanying Crossley. He is a 22-
year-old genius, a pupil of Joachim.'
Of the Phil M*y menage Miss Murphy used to
see a great deal. Mrs. May will probably be put
on the Civil List, and possibly Punch will see to
it that she does not want. Poor May left nothing
but a beautiful home, not even a life assurance
policy. The artist’s mother and elder brother
used frequently to come to see him in London.
Former is a very handsome old lady, dressed
pictorially in velvet and black lace. Their home
is at Leeds, where the son is a carpet designer.
Our friend Longstaff is reported as flourishing;
he has now a fairly large family of small children.
Did you ever stop by the way “to watch a
potter thumping his wet clay.” Such a fascinating
sight. Biblical recollections and memories of
Omar make one half-expect to hear, “ What
make’st thou?” or “Gently, brother, gently,
pray ! ” from the thing that may be an urn or an
ash-tray. I went to a local pottery works lately
to look at the ware that is being made for Mrs.
B. R. Wise’s stall, at the Cranbrook Fete, in aid
of the District Nursing Society. By the way, the
Fete opens on Friday, and endures from 2 to 6
p.m. for two days. A procession of floral
go-carts, with baby freight, will be a fair
sight. There will be an immense refresh-
ment-tent where you may buy any eatable,
from a robust sandwich to an ortolan (which
has been killed by holding its beak under
Heidsieck). At the pottery-stall you may choose
your morning or afternoon, tea-set. Get for the
morning set a tender sunrise hue. Color counts for
so much, especially on mornings when you wake to
meet the nerve-desolating nor’-easter, and to
wonder miserably where and when you lost your
backbone. The sight of a tea-pot, jug and sugar
basin flushed rose or amethyst will give you energy
to pour out your orange pekoe, and perhaps
determine you to stay in this beastly world longer
than you intended after you heard the moaning of
that vicious wind.
Another fairly new thing for Sydney you may get
at the pottery stall—a baking dish in which to cook
a fowl. It has a heavy, close-fitting lid, and shut in
this the chookie is a real gaol-bird. Put in three
onions, a tablespoonful of water —I forget the rest
of the instructions, but the result is something
that would throw Emily Soldene into asterisks.
The potters have also copied several other cooking
pots from originals brought from Marseilles. You
bring these pots straight off the fire on to the
table—no bother about dishing up.
The tender mercies of the wicked Post Office 1
A friend sent me what had been a cigar box
full of Westralian wild flowers. Box arrived
in nine splinters with a handful of crushed flower
petals and some paper and string adhering. But
that’s not as bad as the Paris floral toque that
arrived last week for a certain smart Sydney
dresser. It was doubled up and forced into the
letter-box on her gate.
The picture post card is a splendid labor-saving
device. Everyone hates letter-writing, and the
picture card keeps the sender’s memory a forget-
me-not-blue, although it leaves space for only a
mere line of greeting. You get a card from Paris
showing a wonderful bit of staircase in Notre
Dame, and underneath the scrawl: “Was here
for a day, love to all.” Max Schluter’s picture
cards carry a map of Denmark, and lately I saw
a curious one from Paderewski. It showed a
pianoforte key-board with rays springing forward
from the white notes at every octave, and light
projected from above on to the black. In the
centre of this spectral piano was a heart. Most
individualistic one is from violinist Franz Wilczek.
It pictures Franz on his bicycle with his two fox
terriers looking out from a box secured to the
handles.
Cards are out from State Governor and Lady
Rawson for a garden party at Cranbrook
on the 18th. Mrs. Fanshawe has a dancing
At Home at Admiralty House on the Bth.
On the evening of 22nd oocurs Mrs. Fitz-
gerald’s concert. Australian Club ball takes place
on the 16th. Union Club, evidently, has
ceased to ball. It didn’t give one last year, and it
won’t give one this year. U.C. members are now
mostly at the bath-chair stage of life, and retire at
9 p.m. with a basin of hot gruel and the prayer,
“ Remember not, O Lord, the sins of our youth,
because we’ve forgotten them ourselves.” A
shame to expect the U.C. to give a dance at its
time of life ; yet Sydney Society is grumbling and
sneering because it won’t. Masked ball, in aid of
the Waitara Foundling Home, comes off to-mor-
row, Friday night, at Sydney Town Hall.
A new matrimonial agency has been incubated in
Sydney. It says of itself that it is an entirely
new idea in Australia, and not in any way con-
nected with any other society or agency. Also its
circular entreats the patronage and goodwill of
bishops and clergymen, and says that it is pat-
terned after the exact lines of a similar society
inaugurated in London by editor Stead. The
prospectus mentions feelingly that there are thou-
sands of persons of both sexes in Australia and
Maoriland whose lives are wasted through lack of
opportunity for meeting suitable life partners.
How far the entrance-fee of ss. and subscription
of 21s. will debar those who pine in single blessed-
ness from entering a society that encourages
double cussedness, it is hard to say.
If the political women who are expending so
much energy on temperance questions and the
morality of legislators would give some attention
to the shameful law that allows a wife to be
robbed by her husband during his lifetime, and
ignored in his will after his death, and have that
wrong righted, they would do some good for their
sex. In most civilised countries, a wife may claim
a share of her husband’s property. In Scotland,
the widow can demand her share of the property
left by the husband—whatever his will may say.
Here, where the English law obtains, the husband
may bequeath everything he has away from his wife,
who may have materially helped him to accumulate
property. Take the case of the old man who was
brought before a Sydney magistrate, lately, for
not making adequate provision for his wife. This
ancient was, a. few years ago, possessed of pro-
perty which brought him in from £7 to £lO a
week, but he made over the whole of it to the
Salvation Army, on condition that it gave him an
annuity of £2 12s. a week. He’s 92, the wife is 86
and helpless from rheumatism. The Army col-
lects all rents and pays the annuity to the hus-
band, who seems to have neglected to look after
his wife. The arrangement should be reversible
at law. Any decent asssurance society would
have given a vastly larger annuity on both
lives on such very old age securities. The
Booth gang must have a face of brass and
a heart of clay to allow the old man to thus
rob his wife in the name of “ God and the Army.”
This is more of a woman’s question than the
Neglected Children’s Act.
The all-conquering widow again ! Miss Ethel
Knight Mollison, of the “ Are You a Mason?”
co., has harpooned Mr. Bertie Kelly, one of
Sydney’s matrimonial prizes. During the pretty
widow’s stay here, Mr. Kelly rushed her about
considerably in his motor car, and floated her
round in his yacht. He is a son of the late “ Tin ”
Kelly, who made his money in tin smelting, and
has an income of about £6OOO yearly. Family has
a beautiful home at Rose Bay; the only girl is
travelling in Europe. Wedding was celebrated
quietly in Melbourne. Mr. Cuyler Hastings and
Mrs. Flood, a Sydney resident, signed the register.
The wedding announcement was made in Miss
Mollison’s maiden name—no mention of the dear
departed’s cognomen, which may or may not have
been Jorkings. At the close of her present engage-
ment with Mr. Williamson, Mrs. Kelly will quit
the footlights.
Maggie Papakura, the Maori guide, who is
visiting Sydney, is so grieved at the death of her
cousin, Joe Warbrick, that she will probably
return at once to M.L. Warbrick was one of the
guides with the unhappy trio of visitors killed by
the boiling geyser, Waimangu, at Rotorua last
Monday. Maggie and a sister of the unlucky
guide were at the Palace Theatre last Saturday,
dressed in their mats and feathers, to see “ Secret
Service.”
Mrs. Leland Stanford, widow of the millionaire
founder of the U.S. A. University which bears his
name, arrived by the Yentura last week and goes
to Melbourne to visit a brother of her late hus-
band. For many years Mrs. Stanford has borne
the fairly heavy burden of the management of the
institution, and is now on a two years’holiday,
accompanied by a lady secretary and two servants.
Mrs. Stanford, who celebrated her something over
70th birthday on board, is a bright and vigorous
woman.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“‘Stonington’ is in mourning just now for
quite a number of reasons. First of all Private
Sec. Fitzpatrick fell sick. Then his brother A. D. O.
Browning was listed among the hunting casualties
of the season, and put to bed for repairs. Then
followed the cabled news of Rev. Clarke’s death,
and the Governor found himself suddenly an
orphan. So the Stonington shutters are up for a
time. The Clarkes have been going everywhere
and doing everything with the patience of a whim-
horse. A period of quiet will be a blessing to
them.
“Word comes from the Holy City that the Le
Hunte Governorship starts with too fervid a
wooing of popularity. The new Governor gushes
too freely to last—like a Maoriland geyser—and
will yet suffer from his amiability. Society
doesn’t like the over-pleasant Vice-Regal. It
aches rather for the stings and arrows of out-
rageous hauteur. The cold Countess was regarded
with awe and reverence in this State, while the
lady who followed her and stooped to conquer is
regarded with some pity. Vice-Regal snubs are
better than smiles for toadies.
“The Earl of Lonsdale and the Countess put
in an appearance at the Australian Parliament on
Wednesday and took an unimpassioned view of
the proceedings. They struck the Senate what
time those two rough-hewn South Australians
Tom Playford and the jesting M‘Gregor—were
exchanging political civilities. The Countess
looked on with cold eyes, but thoughtfully. It
seems to be the notion of England’s fairest to
padlock the features, to freeze all expression from
the eyes, and by every means to mask thought.
A. point of the lady’s toilette was its subdued
hue, deep blue, while the figure showed a scorn of
the Gibson effect or the Russian slope. It was
the unexaggerated form with a defined waist.
“That Wednesday saw no end of fashionables
call at the House. A beautifully-trimmed Aus-
trian count paid attention to the orating Reps,
and added a few notes to his diary. The Forrests
were simply in ecstatic glee over the rush of
visitors. As these were of various grades they had
to be sorted and galleried accordingly. The
family of Justice Hood were in the places of the
Elect ; also Judge Chomley’s. The fairest
member of this party regarded the talkers with
frank curiosity and a kind of zoological interest
through an elegantly-mounted lorgnette, making
critical remarks the while. One of these days the
lorgnette business will be overdone, and an angry
Parliament will clear its galleries of impertinent
starers—even when they are pretty girls.
“President Wrixon’s dame postscripts the
opening of State Parliament next Tuesday (7th)
with the usual tea-scramble. The ebbing season
has been singularly free from functions. The
subscription ‘At Homes’—once a winter after-
noon institution —were struck off the lists. With
them were erased sundry noon rallies once precious
to the woman of dress. Mayoral receptions had
no part in the social bill of fare. Consequently
race meetings provided the only public Cake
Walk. The afternoon Bridge corroboree can’t be
counted as a frocking affair, for the players have
ears and eyes only for the gamble, and dress
doesn’t get a look in, even.
“The Tennysons looked in at the military foot-
ball match, on the M.C.C. ground, on Saturday.
Senator Drake played the social r&le of his new
Defence dignity rather feebly. The Banana-
lander can’t drop the parsonic cut of his clothes or
manner. Even Governor Tennyson, with his
boxer atilt and his suede gloves half-way on,
looked rakish and exuberant beside Minister
Drake.
“ Governor Clarke is the promptest governing
person we have ever known. The morning after
the official multitude had wired and penned its
sympathy at the family bereavement to his Ex.,
all were answered fully and in due measure of
thanks. The Governor, too, has a gift of polite
letter-writing. He writes more pleasantly than
he speaks.
“ Cuyler Hastings lost no time in begging the
Melbourne girls to dry those tears, fie is not
married, nor likely—no, he didn’t quite say that.
But he proclaims from the house-tops that he is a
bachelor at large. Apply at Her Majesty’s Theatre,
for all further particulars.
“ A certain beauteous mummer, not long arrived
this way, falls into the error of scenting all her
stationery with subtle perfumery. There is
danger ahead for the woman who does this.
There are days when many a woman wants to dis-
guise her handwriting, but how swear away a
subtle breath of mignonette, or violet, or rose ?
“ Mrs. Norman Menzies is scarcely to be known
these days for the chubby Dorothy Vane with soft
thoughts to a convent bent. She has grown thin.
This was particularly noticed the other night, at
the ‘Sweet Nell’ farewell, when the small dame
sat among some towering ladies, whose chiffons
spread far beyond their booked circle space. A
heliotrope cloak made for pallor, and a mop of
fuzzy fringe only served to diminish the size of
the face.
“One of the most interested members of the
Lemare recital audience at the Town Hall, on
Thursday, was the elfish daughter of Nellie
Stewart. While an infantile neighbor fidgetted
and played with her frills, her sweets, her gloves,
that little one sat on, with hands folded and a
look of deep concern on her colorless face. Per-
haps she likes music; perhaps she is learning to
act early in life.
“ There is no sympathy to spare for the Johnnie
who enters, promiscuous like, into a promise of
matrimony with a stage girl. People are laugh-
ing at the flight of one who, alarmed at the fair
one’s vagaries, begged to be released from the
bond, and had to pay up handsomely as balm to
the lady’s bruised feelings. He was so glad to get
out of the contract, and so relieved to do it quietly,
that he poured all his available cash into her hands.
And she, with a healed heart, prepares to leave
for other shores.
“ They tell all kinds of yarns anent the friskish-
ness and buoyant spirits of Fritz Mueller, highly
reminiscent of the young Mozart who had a way
of skipping from the side of a sovereign to frolic
with a youngster of his own age, or to ask the
most captivating of the Court ladies (Oh, dis-
criminating youth !) for a kiss. Master Fritz
doesn’t ask for kisses ; he gives sauce instead, but
he will come out of a Chopin dream, a Mendelssohn
spell, or a Bach enchantment to turn a somer-
sault, to vault over two or three chairs, or to clear
a space for a Catherine wheel. The music moment
past, young Mueller drops his artistic soul and
plays the kid quite unaffectedly—well, it appears
to be unaffectedly.
“The most successful tootler at a recent big
concert was a stout lady, who lent additional
width to her frame by a loose, flowing Empire
gown—the kind that starts to flow from the com-
modious bust line. It was a terrible mistake,
almost as bad as that of the bony platformer
whose angular parts stick out aggressively from
under a meagre stretch of chiffon. One lean lady
I know invariably recites in a frail muslin garb.
The sleeves flutter when the arms get in motion
like flags from a knobby pole. There’s a
kink, very often, in the female mind that
sends the fat form into fabrics with leopard
stripes or moon circles, and the lean into
black, or some elongating material. With such a
candid friend as the looking-glass always at hand
for safe counsel, isn’t it a wonder that Woman
doesn’t get to know herself ? Wh3 7 must she al-
ways know the other cat so much better ?
“Melbourne vegetarians, who lag behind N.S.
Wales co-feeders in modern methods, but will
soon come to the front with an enticing cafe, gave
an excellent tea the other evening at the Thistle
Rooms. Tomatoes and rice, lentil rolls, bean
salads, stewed prunes, fruit, and caramel coffee
seemed the favorite items, not forgetting brown
bread, and clean-tasting wheat ‘triseuits’ (thrice-
baked).
“Friends of the Rev. George Robinson learn
that he is hatching a scheme for the biggest
bazaar ever conceived. The germ of it was in his
brain-box long before he boarded the ’Ome
steamer, but time and a short residence in Dublin
have led to its development. At no distant date
we shall have the diplomatic guardian of the
Castles’ flock returning to set the colossal project
afloat.
“ The season of beet-root has its importance in
the eyes of humble feminines —it being used, after
boiling operations, by the homely sex as a harm-
less ‘ make up.’ Then, of your Cynthia, you. say—
‘ Red as a beet is she. *
“Some of the she-reformers of this city are
upset at the appointment of some horrid men
down your way to inquire into the causes of the
decreasing birth-rate. ‘ What do they know ? ’
say the members of the Sairey Gamp sex, with an
indignant snort. How may a committee of he-
citizens pry into a state of things so strictly
feminine ? Set women to catch women.
“Whatever statisticians may aver in regard
to the dwindling figures in the year’s birth
columns, it must be admitted that society has
had twinges of conscience lately. It isn’t, even
now, pledging itself to any baker’s dozen or any
vulgar number, but, for the last year or two,
it, has been resigned to the lot of limited mother-
hood. Children are the fashion just now, and
is not good form for a matron to be without oue
or two.
“ Such a show of male finery at the annual meet
of Trinity College Dialectic Society ! Bishop
Clarke, who sported scarlet, with big over-cape of
ermine, apologised for his fine feathers, explaining
that to please Dr. Leeper he had donned the
wampum appropriate to a Cambridge Doctor of
Divinity. This year’s Prelector, Dr. Frank
Andrew, son of a former professor of Melbourne
’Varsity, is a handsome youth, who showed, when
he wound up with a quotation from Victor Daley,
that a musical voice is another of his properties.
For a Trinity Prelector he sounded fairly Radical.
In his utterances on ‘ Australian ideals ’ Chief-
Justice Madden dealt very tenderly with the
young democrat. The Waxcellency knows that
butter is the best remedy for youthful outspoken-
ness. It’s so hard to keep on being independent
and antagonistic when the big hands stroke puss-
patriot’s fur the right way. Dr. Madden was
extra bright and genial that night. Previous
speakers had all chaffed him for criticising the
accent of Australian girls. He affected to think,
when his turn to speak came, that girls had been
offended by his advice, because his years are now
too many and his attractions too few to make him
an acceptable Mentor to the roses, whose thorns
had pricked his fingers. He advised the young
and handsome Prelector to mission the fair sex
with regard to their vowels or any other foible,
while he is still charming enough to win woman’s
ear.
“The political romance, ‘Jacob Shumate,’
penned by Mr. Henry Wrixon, receives very faint
praise from the London oracles. The Athenaeum
starts promisingly with the announcement that
President Wrixon is a most distinguished Vic-
torian statesman, but it deprecates that person’s
skill at ‘ picturing Australian politicians and their
difficulties.’ The Athenaeum instances Mrs.
Campbell-Praed as a pleasanter and abler autho-
rity on the matter. Then occurs this nasty rub :
‘ The reader who has the patience to do what we
have been compelled to undertake.’ Have been
compelled !

AKENEHL.
In Sydney Telegraph’s report of one of
George Reid’s open-air election meetings, that
wonderful man is stated to have said : “My great
objection is that the Australian Government has
broken in the grossest possible manner the laws
which it had passed. If a man defrauded anyone
of his elector’s right that man could be imprisoned
for two years. Yet these law-makers were
strangling the votes of tens of thousands. ’ Then
a voice asked : “What did you do in 1896?” and
the gerrymanderer of 189frhad the front to reply :
“ You can’t get out of an indictment by saying
someone else did it.” And then he went on to
wax righteously indignant over the alleged crime
which he himself had perpetrated only seven
years ago. And some of the audience actually
thought he meant it.
“THE MERRYMAN, MOPING, MUM!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The clown has turned tragedian. "—Barton on
Reid.
“ I have a game to play.”— Jack Pointless.
It’s played with a lie
And a glass in the eye,
And a tumbling turn, and a No-Yes-No !
It’s the game of a paunchy and punful clown
Who stumps a Commonwealth up and down,
Now taking the biggest hall in town,
Now tickling bucolics, bearded, brown,
With a fiscal feather long obsolete
From the tail of the arid dog effete.
It’s also the song of the Doleful Dane,
With a bloodshot wrath in his window-pane,
Who thought with twaddle to catch the crowd,
And saw no shadow of Someone’s shroud
As he cheerfully danced on a cumbrous toe
To the lilting strains of a No-Yes-No.
He saw in the sky no rising cloud,
Nor the gathering shadow of Someone’s shroud.
H. C. C.

SICK KIDNEYS.
An aching, breaking back—sharp, sickening stabs of pain—That’s kidney trouble.
Look at the picture. See how the kidneys are really a spongy filter—a human filter to
take poison from the blood.
But sick, weak kidneys can’t filter the blood properly. The delicate human filters get
clogged with impurities, and the poison is left in the system to cause backaches, headaches
rheumatism, dropsy and fatal inflammation.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are the one sure cure for sick kidneys. They make new blood which
flushes them clean and gives them strength for their work. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills set the kidneys ri<*ht
and make lame backs stiff and sturdy.
“ Thirteen years ago a terrible dragging’ pain came in the small of my back,” says Mrs. Caroline Courtney, 85 Phillip Street, Moore Park
Sydnev; Dull aching kidneys wouldn’t let me sleep. I felt sore and bruised, and afraid to walk. My eyes grew dim and strange lisrhts
danced before them. I was too weak to work and the smell of food made me sick. I suffered day and night till I took Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People. They gradually filled my veins with pure, healthy blood and my kidneys ached ho more. Now I never have a pain in
my back, my kidneys are clean and souud, and I am a happy, healthy woman.” F
By making new blood, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are a simple, scientific cure
for anaemia, paleness, skin diseases, biliousness, indigestion, heart St.
Vitus’ dance, nervousness, neuralgia, sciatica, partial paralysis and locomotor
ataxia.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Of the present 57 members of the N.S.W.
Legislative Council 33 have held seats in the
Legislative Assembly, and four have sought seats
and failed.
Sam See (John’s brother), of Grafton
(N.S.W.), pronounces definitely against the white
labor policy of the Commonwealth. That should
about wreck Sam’s lately-expressed pdlitical am-
bitions.
Heading in a daily paper :
A Legislative Councillor Found Dead.
Most Legislative Councillors are dead, but they,
as a rule, manage to avoid being publicly dis-
covered in that condition.
For this year’s exhibition of the N.S.W.
Oil Art Society, “Mr. Long will show some good
portraits, including one of Mr. Perry ” —the
Minister in charge of the Art subsidy. That’s
what one might call Tact.
Both Argos and Age, in reviewing ’Enery
Wrixon’s political novel (which is as long as five
ordinary-sized novels), start off by mentioning
that Disraeli was a statesman who also wrote
novels. Why drag in Disraeli ?
In removing Bent from the Railways to
the Health Department Vic. Premier Irvine has
done a wise thing. Bent is now grappling with
the swine fever, and if there is anyone in the
Commonwealth who ought to understand swine it
is Bent.
A youth who should make"' a champion
criminal in a few years keeps on extending his
record in the Melbourne courts. He is only 20,
and already owns to 29 previous convictions,
mostly for petty larceny, vagrancy, and insulting
behavior. The law still fines him 20s. or a month,
though it hasn’t the sense to make him earn his
living while doing the month.
Hobart possesses two bishops, the
Anglican and the Coadjutor R.C., who scan-
dalise Fatman by their
talk from the pulpit
about wages and work.

Though N.S.W. Jus-
tice G. B. Simpson
claims to have spent
many years in active
Parliamentary prac-
tice, his sphere all the
time was the Leg.
Council. An ambi-
tion to enter the As-
sembly possessed
him for a couple of
months about forty
years ago, and he
started up Gosford
way to fascinate
electors. There were
no trains and few
coaches on the track, and
barrister Simpson, unlike the average
native, was but a poor hand with bush
horses. The result was that he was badly
thrown and had an arm broken. He went to
bed, but allowed his name to go to the poll, where
it was duly wiped out. He dropped politics after
that till Robertson sent him .into the Council as
Attorney-General in 1885.
“R.S.A.” : One morning, when the Age
remarked that Bent had shed tears of emotion,
a huge smile swept over Melbourne. Neverthe-
less, I have seen Bent cry—with rage. One of
the most amusing sights in the city is to see
Tommy wound up—ramping, raging, snapping,
snorting, and trying to climb over his own head.
Obit. Mrs. C. E. Pilcher, wife oftheK.C.
The deceased lady was a daughter of Mr. John
M‘Phillamy, of Mount Tamar, Bathurst (N.S.W.),
born in 1851, and married in 1871. Her father
represented West Macquarie in the reformed
Parliament of 1859.
Said that ex-politician Jacob Garrard, who
mismanages Sydney Water Supply, insists always
on being addressed as the “Hon.’ Mr. Garrard.
Some men’s claim to honor, like some women’s
claim to virtue, has to be insisted upon to get any
sort of recognition.
Apropos Frederick Bridges and the N.S.W.
Under-Secretaryship of Public Instruction.
Bridges is in his 64th year; according to the rule
of the service he should retire in a year’s time.
Naturally, the question is—for whom is Bridges
keeping the billet open ?
N.S.W. Acting-Chief Justice Stephen has
now been nearly 54 years in the law and on the
Bench; Justice Owen, 44 years; Justice G. B.
Simpson, 45 years; Justice A. H. Simpson, 35
years; Justice Cohen, 31 years ; Justice Gregory
Walker, 30 years; Justice Pring, 29 years.
Apropos the late Tommy Walker and his
missing remains. When the last of the male
Tasmanian aboriginals died in ’69, in Hobart
Hospital, his head was stolen and replaced by
that of an old Scotch shepherd who had died in
the institution at the same time. Notwith-
standing the dual contents of the coffin, prayers
were read for our “ dear departed brother.”
Hee Hem Smith, member for Taranaki in
M.L. House of Representatives, has just been
requested by the New Plymouth corporation to
resign his position as turncock to that borough.
Although Lis son does the work, the little man in
the belltopper is primarily responsible when the
water isn’t quickly turned on, and as, during the
session, he is absent at Wellington, it has been
decided that he can’t be relied upon.
Bishop Broyer, of the Navigator Islands,
now visiting Sydney, has still much of interest to
say of Robert Louis Stevenson, whom he knew
well in Samoa. The novelist has become a kind
of sacred memory among the natives. Ihe Bishop
has been about those islands the last 28 years.
N.S.W. Rep. Conroy has taken to farm-
ing, and makes it pay. _ The only thing he has
to guard against is orating near the crops, lie
inadvertently expressed his opinion of the Barton
Ministry the other day in the home paddock, and
gave a beautiful crop of oats, just in ear, the
blight.
N.S.W. Premier See and Attorney-
General Wise have appointed themselves trustees
of N.S.W. National Park. The trusteeship of
this park is a fine, comfortable billet. The
trustees are masters of the beautiful reserve, with
a comfortably furnished house, a steam launch,
and a small fleet of boats thrown in.
The recent death of John Hindmarsh,
son of the first Governor of South Australia,
indirectly recalls Australia’s first land boom.
There were only 546 inhabitants in S.A. when
Hindmarsh arrived. A big boom set in after
the first sale of town allotments, which rose
from £5 ss. and £6 6s. to £2OOO and £3OOO an acre,
and everything else was at a ridiculous figure.
The bubble burst in ’39, and left S. A. with about
7d. worth of genuine assets.
The current Australian Review of
Reviews, outrages all Christianity and decency
by publishing a set of verses, coming from a man
at Cairns (Q.), depicting Kingston dying, and
“the ghastly thing” which serves as his soul
foing to the Judgment Seat, where God expresses
is scorn for this “ Thing.” Then there’s talk of
the “murky stench of that Federal Parliament,”
and so on. It’s a woeful exhibition of how far
rage at an honest man can carry a parson editor.

HARD TIMES.
“The policy pursued by the Ministry has brought us
to the time when it is necessary to be just to the country
before we can be generous to the old-age pensioners.”
S.M. HERALD.
OLD GRANDMA HERALD: “Back, Mr. Waddell.
This is a time to be economical and just; and
this is the best victim of economy.”
Premier Jenkins will complete his 52nd
year on Tuesday next (September 8). On the
following day Spofforth, the erstwhile Demon
bowler, will be 48. Twenty years ago Spofforth
was the greater man of the two, but cricket is a
game that wants continued application and
youthful freshness, whereas the game of politics
is often won by the man who can take advantage
of the freshness of other people.
Samuel Summers, who has been superin-
tendent of the postal money-order department in
S. Australia ever since such a department has
exiadd, has just retired from the service. Sum-
mers was a comparatively old man when Deputy
P.M.Gr. Todd, who is usually reckoned the
ancient of the office, arrived 48 years ago. It is
said that not so much as a penny has ever gone
wrong in the S. A. money-order office.
The deceased Charles J. Pharazyn re-
signed from Maoriland Upper House in disgust
when fined £lOO for dual voting (which he didn’t
deny) at a city election, being over-anxious to
secure the return of an old crony. The old man
was a familiar figure at the Assessment Courts,
where, even up to his hundredth year, or near it,
he inveighed volubly against the scandalous
figures placed on his properties by official valuers.
It was his only annual recreation, and as the
valuations were rarely reduced he hobbled out in
high dudgeon.
Coroner Candler, of Melbourne, has been
sitting on fires and corpses for half-a-century in
Victoria, but he will be best known to posterity
by having given a “case” to the law books.
Thirty years ago, an old doctor named Cornelius
Casey, who had been surgeon at Port Arthur,
gave some “ cheek ” to Candler, who committed
him for contempt. Casey sued Candler for false
imprisonment, and the case went to the Full
Court on the question whether the coroner’s court
was a “court of record” and the coroner had
power to commit. Casey v. Candler is known to
all lawyers and law students in Vic.
Victoria’s Premier Irvine has publicly
announced that he is not going to enter the Aus-
tralian Parliament. He says he finds his hands
too full of State matters. Irvine’s friends made a
great effort to secure him a seat in the House of
Representatives three years ago, but Pharez
Phillips beat him by over 500 votes. Irvine tells
his private friends that he is losing money by
remaining in politics. Since he took over the
job of Victorian dictator he has let his law
practice go hang. He is reported to have made
about £2OOO a year at the Bar while in practice.
Of oourse, he may annex one of the judgeships
which must become vacant shortly.
The other day, Adye Douglas, President
of Tasmania’s Legislative Council, was one of a
deputation that waited on Archbishop Murphy to
congratulate him on his 88th birthday and wish
him many happy returns. Douglas bragged that
he was six weeks older than the Archishop, but
said that if his youngest son, who was six years
old the day before, was a little older, he didn’t
care how soon he went under. “ When a man
gets to my age the essence of life is gone.” Then
the old prelate began to think how he’d feel six
weeks hence. But at a festive meeting last week
Adye told the assemblage that he meant soon to
have a pleasure trip to Europe to see life and
bring back the lost essence.
“ Spring ” on some politicians:—
Recently re-visited
N.S.VV. Legislative
Assembly. Had a
seat in the Speaker’s
Gallery. You must
be distinguished to
get there. The com-
mon man is in the
Strangers’ Gallery,
whence he gazes
fondly down upon
the top of honest
Edden’s pink head.
In entering I tripped
over about 10 police-
men within as many
yards of passages.
The most awesome
official is he who con-
ducts you to your
seat. He has that
large air of import-
ance always noticed
in men with author-
ity in small matters.
The first remarkable
object I noticed on
the floor of the
chamber was Dan
O’Oonnor drifting
about like a lost snow
storm. He seemed
greatly agitated or
something. Dan’s
eyebrows are always
in motion, and like
two white mice creep up, up almost to the back of his
head, and then scamper back again. The members of
the Ministry have grown remarkably fat. Even Waddell,
whom I remember as a thin and fretful Oppositionist, is
now sleek and calm. Crick is the exception. He is
thinner and more careless in his dress, and seems out of
place amongst that brilliant array of meat and broad-
cloth. He is the head-piece, Owe’Sullivan is the stomach,
and dour John Kidd is the sturdy, dumpy legs of the
Ministry. I didn’t see its soul ; that is in Cohen’s safe in
London. The Opposition is, generally, pathetically lean,
bar Oarruthers. He is fat enough, hut pallid, and not
hearty. Amongst the Ministry and its supporters, there
is now no really amusing person. See’s three most ridi-
culous supporters—Dan O’Connor, Eden George, and
the harmless Dr. Ross—have recently left him. “ It’s a
bad omen when the clowns leave the circus,” said a
showman.
John Langdon Parsons, M.L.C., who
recently died in Adelaide, was a leading member
of the Cremation Society which recently con-
structed the expensive crematorium, in which, so
far, only two sheep and an Afghan have been in-
cinerated. However, his friends did not permit
his remains to be cremated, but placed them in
the earth in the conventional way. Parsons began
life as a Baptist preacher, but though he retired
from the pulpit 30 years ago, in the few semi-
conscious moments before he passed away he was
back to the old calling.
At a recent excursion of the Australian
Historical Society to Parramatta, a house in
George-street was pointed out as being the
residence of John Batman. If it be true that the
Victorian pioneer land-grabber lived there, the
house must be nearly 90 years old, and possibly
older, as John Batman settled in Van Diemen’s
Land in 1820. Batman was a native of Parra-
matta, born in 1800, his father being one of a
missionary band which had failed to get a footing
“in the islands, ’ and settled down in “Botany
Bay.” Its members took to various occupations,
money-lending being the specialty of at least one.
His name was Clode, and having lent a soldier
a sum of money, he tried to collect the same, and
got murdered for his trouble.
“ Allan Dale ” writes :
Touching “ M.’s” pathetic par. concerning the passing
of poor Parkes, all the old man’s soul-weariness and deep
despondency at the time referred to were fully accounted
for a few hours after he died. On opening his desk the
sum of one-and-ninepence was found, and that was all the
wealth he possessed in this world. The constant scraping
and struggling and manoeuvring to “make ends meet”
had crushed all the vigor out of him. It was small use
helping Parkes with a mere loan, for money went through
his hands like water through a sieve. It is a fact that,
having spent the greater part of a day trying to “flv a
kite ” for £5O, which was urgently required for home
necessaries, he spent £3O out of it on his way home on
rare old books, pictures, and bric-a-brac , of which he
deluded himself he was a clever judge. Parkes was like
an empty sack. Lifting him up was a mere waste of
time, unless you held him up continuously as well.
About a very old identity :
Charles Johnston Pharazyn, a wealthy citizen of
Wellington (M.L.), died on 17th August, aged 100 years
and 10 months. One of the old man’s brightest recollec-
tions was the Waterloo celebration. When aged 39 he
left England for Maoriland. The “good”—they were
always “ good ’’—barque Jane, 356 tons, was found to be
quite unseaworthy, and she put into Rio in a sinking
condition. The captain held that Jane couldn’t be
repaired, but Pharazyn took the opposite view and paid
out all the money he had to cover expenses. One of the
crude methods employed was to bind the hull with chains
fore and aft--urider the keel and across the deck. Jane
struggling under her curious ironmongery repairs’
arrived in Port Nicholson in May, 1841, after havin <r been
given up for lost. Pharazyn had to sue the owners in
bydney to recover the money he had spent in repairing
Jane. He subsequently took up land in the vicinity of
,?, a „ . lser miles from Wellington) in partnership
with William Fitzherbert. In 1869 he was called to the
Legislative Council, and retained his seat until 1885
when his son, Robt. Pharazyn, was appointed in his
stead. The centenarian was married three times—on the
last occasion to Miss Jessica Rankin, a poetess of some
note None of his four sons survive him. It is told of
. man t'hat he had slept in the same room with his
eothn for over twenty years.

“Sweet are the uses of Advertising,” especially
if tiie article advertised has merit to sustain it, as is the
case with Mr. Charles Nuttall’s historical painting of the
opening of the Commonwealth Parliament by H.R.H. the
Prince of Wales. Photogravure copies are already being
distributed all over Australia, and finding a ready sale
amongst all classes. The metropolitan papers speak of
the work as being “ a fine and faithful impression of the
scene,” and as “ an historical record of undying interest
to every Australian.” See next week’s Bulletin.*
Our New Selection, by “Steele Rudd,” will
be ready in November. Even more humorous
than his first book, with more pictures. Price 6s.
Aboriginalities.
“War E.”: Animals often meet with
peculiar deaths. Recollect many years ago a
“piker” of a bullock getting his head between
two saplings ; it was wider at the top than bottom.
He stayed there, and his bones marked the spot
for many years afterwards. On another occasion
a young bullock had been coupled to an old stager.
The young ’un took fright and bolted into a scrub
dragging the other with him. Searched several
days for them without success. Months later
came on their remains in a little ravine; the young
bullock had fallen and broken his neck, and there
had stood the other patiently awaiting death.
Often merino rams, when feeding near wire fences,
get their horns caught in the wire and perish.
Have seen where a dingo, in making a jump, was
caught by the tail in the cleft of a stump.

“Opal King” : A swagman in the Wir-
rabara district stopped to inform a cocky of an
incident that had happened not far from his place.
Mrs. Cocky met him at the gate, and jumped at
the conclusion that he had come for rations.
“Can you” he began, but she broke in:
“No; I’ll give you nothing ! ” “Here, I say,”
said the wanderer, “will you” “No!’’said
Mrs. Cocky, firmly; “my husband told me to
give nothing. Travellers will ruin us. First one
comes for a bit of flour, and another for some
meat, and ” “D—n you and your flour!”
said the person with the swag. “ D —n your bit
of meat ! Can you tell me who’s the bloke wot’s
hung himself down the road—him with the
dungaree pants, and the bluchers, and the galatea
shirt?” “I expect it’s my husband,” said Mrs.
Cocky.

“Mimosa”; A resident of Gippsland
asserts that the buny ip-amphibian bogey of the
bygone black is nothing more formidable than a
duck—just a little musk duck, no bigger than any
other wild duck inhabiting the swamps of that
moist locality. I can testify to the weird blood-
curd le of its music in the silent midnight watches.
With the melancholy mopoke and the keening
curlew already on hand, it needed only the shout
of this unholy fowl to make the blackfellow cower
and shudder in his mia mia, and long for the
cheerful kookaburras in the morning.

“ Combo ” writes :
Concerning P. 0. Cole’s inquiry (26/7/’O3). The “ bull
roarer,” or “ goonanduckyer,” which fills the Australian
savage with
such terror,
is merely
what was
known in
my boyhood
days as a
“hummer”
—a piece of
rather thin,
flat wood
aboutafoot
long and
threeinches
wide, with
deep
notches cut
into both
sides. A
string is
fastened to
one end,
and when
theconcern
Is whirled rapidly round the head
it makes a strange humming or
roaring noise which, if heard in
the bush on a dark night by
anyone who didn’t know the
real cause, would induce either a
black or a white to fall on his knees and remember about
99 per cent, of his sins—and be rather sorry for some of
them. The “bullroarer” is to the black gin what the
Masonic hot poker is to a Mason’s wife, and plays a lead*
ing part in the ceremony of “ making a young man ” of
youths who have attained the age of puberty. I
once whirled a “hummer” of my own make behind an
old gin in the Bathurst district. She was not the least
alarmed for herself, but warned me : “ Baal you show dat
to Georgie"—her husband, a chief—“ him make you
go jute (die).” She had discovered the Masonic secret
and reckoned it up at its real value.

A day’s North Queensland court-work :
Ingham: A Kanaka, found guilty of indecently
assaulting a white girl, was sentenced to three months’
imprisonment. Another black boy was fined for violent
behavior; a Cingalese pleaded guilty to a charge of
attempted suicide, and was committed for trial.
Townsville : Wm. Ah Kit, a half-caste Chinese boy, on
committal from Normanton, charged with entering a
warehouse and stealing cigarettes and tobacco, was
ordered to attend for sentence when called upon;
Abesykr Powles, a Cingalese, charged with’attempting to
kill himself, was imprisoned till the rising of the court;
a time-expired Kanaka, named Teena Tow, was sentenced
to 14 days, for assault.
Cairns : Five Kanakas, named Todos, Geenah, Wanga,
Kow and YVarra Warra, were charged with drunkenness
and disorderly conduct; two Chinamen were prosecuted
under the Betting Act ; an Indian and a man named
Emanuel de Gessray (presumed to be an Asiatic nobleman
out of luck), were charged with vagrancy; another
black man, name unknown, was charged with an unnatural
offence, and remanded for a week.
* * * *
A selector near Barcaldine (Q.)has turned
bore water to a useful and novel purpose. He
ran a small pipe round the inside of a properly
partitioned box and then diverted a small portion
of his bore water into the pipe. The box was
thus converted ihto an inexpensive incubator,
and many chickens have been hatched therein.
MR. “ BILL” LYONS,
Winner of Talt.’s Club Billiard Competition.
Photo, by Eden, Sydney.
Eureka Stockade will not have a jubilee
until 3rd December of next year; but it was just
50 years ago, at the end of August, that Victoria’s
other great goldfield, Bendigo, was at white-heat
over the same business. They called the Bendigo
protest the “red-ribbon movement; ” every man
who joined in it wore a flaming rosette. Two
monster meetings were held —one on August 21
and the other on August 27 ; the roll-up was some-
thing like 20,000 strong. Gov. Latrobe, who had
been talking about cannons, cut a miserable figure
over the demonstrations at Bendigo and Waranga,
and the Government departments were in a state
of panic. For the moment, the diggers’ license
fee, which had been run up to £3 a month, was
brought down to 10s., and the lesser amount was
collected more or less decently; but when the
diggers, satisfied with the change, threw the red
rosettes away, the old game started again, the
licenses were jumped up, the administration
became worse than ever, and Eureka followed.
One or two of those who wore the badge
at Bendigo are still on deck: among them
W. D. C. Denovan, afterwards clerk of the city
council, and G. E. Thomson, who then, or a little
later, kept an eating-house, but was afterwards a
solicitor. Other more prominent men in the
movement have died in the last few years—among
them “Old Bob” Haverfield, for a generation
editor of Bendigo Advertiser, and Angus
Mackay, owner of that paper, and one of the
ounders of Sydney Telegraph.
“ Viator ” : Philp Government pro-
poses to introduce a drastic measure for
the suppression of cattle-stealing. A good
part of the fault lies with the squatters
themselves, or with the controlling
financial institutions. Writer knows from
personal observation that several station
properties of huge area in the Gulf coun-
try employ no labor beyond a manager
and several blackboys. Mustering comes
round, and a couple of aboriginal stock-
men are sent out—often without a white
man. Warrigal musters just as many as
he finds convenient, and the result is the
run is overrun with clean-skins. Recent
condition laid down in fencing contract by
wide-awake squatter : That the successful
contractor buy 121bs. of beef per man per
week during the term of the contract.-
* * *
“ Lando ” : Shearing has started in
several sheds in the Gwydir, Gunnedah
and Lower Castlereagh districts, and the
general date for the north-western part of
the State is Ist September. The roads
converging thereto are lined, with men,
and it is estimated that about four times
the requisite labor is available. What the
surplus three-fourths will do no man
knoweth. Lambing may be taken at 80
per cent, on an average, so matters are
better than anticipated.
* * *
“ Kooka ”: In the Yungella Range,
Mackay district (Q.), there is found a
curious stone called locally a “sand ball.”
These stones are more or less rounded,
with a pitted surface (no sign of break or
crack), of a rust color, and of similar for-
mation to sandstone. On breaking them
the interior is found to be made up of loose
dry sand, which can be shaken out, leaving
but a shell of hard stone, varying in thick-
ness in different balls, but not always
varying with the size of the ball. Some
times the ball is not quite full, and by
shaking it close to the ear the loose sand
may be heard to rattle. The stones vary
in size ; some are as small as a hen’s egg,
others so large that when carefully sawn
in two the halves make unique flower
pots. No local geologist' seems able to
account for the curious formation.
The Bulletin hears that between Clon-
curry and Camoweal (Q.) many aborigines are in a
fearful state of disease. The Protector might see
to it.

“R.A.”: Living in Gippsland, where
there are nine wet months in the year, seems to
affect people in the top-piece. Within the last
three years seven men I have worked with have
gone to the lunatic asylum, and another suicided
whilst insane.
“Nym”: What is looked
upon as a new fish species has
been bothering the minds of
Albury anglers. Early in the
year a Mr. J. Flower caught
a peculiar-looking fish in the
Wodonga Creek It was
jumping cheerfully in a shal-
low place when Flower kicked
it out of the water and thus
diminished its harmless mirth.
The fish weighed about a
pound, and resembled a bream
more than any other fresh
water fish; but the stranger
possessed all the internals of
an animal as distinguished
from a fish, the most peculiar
difference in this respect
being the gizzard. It is said
that fish of this description,
known as the Murrumbidgee
herring, have been known for
many years in the waters of
the Murrumbidgee. I think
the finny specimens are a
cross between a perch and a
cat-fish. [Probably a cross
between the cat-fish and the
rabbit. —Ed. B.]

intersected by “gidyah”
belts. Again, in an article
published in the De Rouge-

“ Elmore ” : I was sitting alone on the
form outside the pub. A man passed by remark-
ing, cheerily, “Not to-day, old man.” I turned
round and stared after him in astonishment, but
was suddenly brought round again with a jerk at
an irate female voice, snapping out, “You great
THE PEARL OF MINSTRELSY.
COUNTRY VISITOR (at Zoo): “I wishter ’Eaven
they’d begin to sing!”
idiot! you ought to be ashamed of yourself!"
Things were getting serious. I looked over as
much as I could see of myself, but everything
seemed satisfactory. The next who passed by
said simply, “Fool,” and the fourth said,
“ Blanky fool.” Then a seedy individual wanted
to fight me. I reckoned by this time it was up to
me to get an explanation, so I asked him why he
wanted to fight. “ Trying to make a fool of me
with that,” he said, pointing to a neatly-tied
parcel lying on the ground a few feet away
from me, and which I then noticed for the
first time. “Is it a brick or a dead eat ? ”
Whilst I was trying to prove my innocence, an
old sundowner happened along. He dropped his
swag and looked at the parcel. Then he skirted
round it. Finally, he picked it up and opened it,
and found 21b. of cheese. The man who wanted
to fight me looked foolish. I felt so sorry for him
that I went into the bar and ordered a drink for
myself.
BILL MAYNE’S STORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were yarning in the bar-room of a shanty In the
West,
And wetting intervals with rum and clo’;
Fish and snake-yarns, shearing-tallies, sheep-dog tales,
and all the rest
Of bush-prevarications got a show.
We were “ broke,” and Mayne considered that the time
had duly come
For us to have a cut-in at the game.
So he told a little story which, though strange and
puzz’esome,
Was a truthful little story all the same.
“ ’Twas in Gippsland, where I come from,” said my mate
in solemn tone,
“ My evergreen, belovfed home in Vic.,
And I went out shooting dingoes with a brother of my
own—
His name, I state in passing, it was Dick."
Here the tears began to trickle down the cheeks of
William Mayne
Till they offered him a tumbler full of rum.
“ Ah, my story is a sad one, and to tell it gives me pain.”
He sobbed and then he dried his eyes with some.
Then went on, “ And we had shinned it down the cleared
side of a hill,
When in front of us beside a blue-gum log,
In a burnt-off fern-tree gully, standing beautifully still,
We espied a full-grown reddish native dog.
“ With the rifle Dick could beat me, so I let him have the
kill ”
Here another flow of tears caused Mayne to stop.
But they quickly filled his tumbler, and incited him to
swill,
And he drank and thought his mate would like a drop.
I refreshed ; and he continued: “ Yes, I gave the shot to
him,
And considered that there dog as good as dead,
When I saw my brother aiming with his gun against a
limb,
To dispatch his never-failing dose of lead.
“ But imagine, mates, my horror when the dingo twisted
round
Just the moment when the bullet reached the cuss !
And, of course, instead of passing through the off side of
the hound,
It came out the side the brute had turned to us!
PEOPLE WE PUT ON THE LAND.
DAD : “ Old? Why, demit man, ye can see for
ye’self. She ain’t cut her top front teeth yet!”
“ And the cruel, deadly missile came back the way it
went,
And it killed my only brother on the spot!
Ever since my heart is broken, for I caused the accident,
By allowing him to have that fatal shot.”
Then Bill Mayne broke down completely and began to sob
aloud,
And his sorrows sent a lump into my throat.
But the fellows at the shanty were a sympathetic crowd,
And shouted rum enough to drown a boat.
We were soothed, and just felt happy when a kangarooer
said :
“ This ’ere dingo-varn is nothing else but rot.”
And that started them debating, and their faces colored
red,
While their lingo grew from warm to hoiling-hot.
“ You are right,” then yelled a drover, “ we were mugs,
we now can see,
To swallow and believe such bloomin' stuff 1 ”
While some others, mixed with curses, hollered out,
“ The thing could be
If the dingo only turned round quick enough 1 ”
And a ginger-headed shearer, whose profanities would
burn,
Demanded, in a brutal voice, to know :
“If that crimson, slaughtered bullet, when the dingo
took a turn,
Could any other blanky elsewhere go ? ”
The debate grew hot and hotter, till there rose an all-
round fight,
And I wished my mate had never told the tale !
For they called poor Bill a liar, and a ‘ * take-down,” and
a “skite,”
And they swore they’d knock his head off without fail 1
Then Bill thought it just as well to get our swags and
move away,
So we hurried off across the Blacksoil Plain ;
But the fellows at the shanty talked and fought till break-
o’-day
'Bout that truthful little story told by Mayne.

NODDING!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a nod of recognition in the bowing of the trees,
As they spread their rain-blessed tangles to be wantoned
by the breeze ;
While the flowers in the paddocks have a curtsey all their
own,
Since the season is the brightest that the last decade has
known 1
All the world’s a church these mornings with the singing
everywhere,
And the congregation nodding, and sweet incense in the
air—
Ah! methinks the fields a-nodding in their toques of
green and gold
Nod the benison of harvest for the six-bag yields of old.

Spring Time Cleaning 1 .
Spring has arrived, and now the careful house-
wife goes through the annual Spring house clean-
ing. The summer will soon be upon us, and at this
time of the year we should take care to cleanse
the whole system, and fortify it to withstand the
enervating effects of the Summer. The increasing
hot weather of this period has an extremely
debilitating effect on the system if it is not in
perfect working order all through. It will be thus
seen that a medicine that will regulate and cleanse
the interior mechanism of man is especially
valuable at this time of the year. Such a medi-
cine is Bile Beans, and no better preparation can
be found for toning up the digestive organs,
cleansing the blood, and invigorating the liver.
Spring generally brings with it an assortment of
ailments, such as Spring Fever, tired feeling, loss
of appetite, &c. For these take Bile Beans,
which are also a specific for biliousness, indiges-
tion, constipation, debility, which are also preva-
lent at this season, As a purgative Bile Beans
are acknowledged without an equal in Australia.
Their price places them within the reach of every-
one, and no one should run the risk of being in
ill-health through the Summer when a short
course of Bile Beans would effectually purify the
body and keep it in thorough working order.*

“W.H.”: At Cassowary Creek, between
Port Douglas and the Mossman (Q.), four years
ago, one of the bridge hands sang out to me from
a waterhole. Going over, I saw a big brown
snake, head above the surface of the water, hold-
ing down a water-lizard with a couple of round
turns of his tail. On snake being shot the lizard
disengaged himself and disappeared in the bushes
seemingly unhurt. Snake measured between
four and five feet in length. Thought he
meant to drown the other reptile.
though, of course, he may only have
been teaching him to swim.
OVER-INDULGENGE.
THE BRUNETTE ONE: “Ah! there goes that
beautiful hubby of mine. He's been indulging
in so much missionary of late that he fairly
looks like one.”
So far the efforts to get artesian water at
the head of the Australian Bight, somewhere
near the track of the proposed trans-continental
line, haven’t been overwhelmed with success. A
supply has lately been struck near Eyre at a
depth of 2041 ft., but it is small, and saline at
that. Generally it is supposed that the great
Australian artesian system, which is fed on the
western slopes of the Queensland and northern
N.S.W. coastal ranges, has an outlet somewhere
along the Bight; but Westralia’s boring has, so
far, not advanced that theory, so the present
supply may be only a local one.
* * * »
The other day, the remains of a man who
had died of thirst two years before were discovered
on Caradoc station, just outside the Mildura
boundary, almost within coo-ee of a tank and
within a few miles of the river. The body was
identified by his widow in Mildura, after two
years in the inallee scrub, by the contents of his
swag, his billy, and the clothes he had been wear-
ing. He had left his home, bluey up, after a tiff
with his wife, and the immeasurable distances
swallowed him.
» * * *
It was found, on a recent Sunday, that a
hill had subsided from underneath the Wellington-
Napier (M.L.) railway line at Mungaroa, and left
the sleepers and rails suspended high in the air
for a distance of over two chains. This timely
discovery averted what might have been the
biggest railway disaster ever known in Maoriland,
for the spot is hidden by a bent cutting on either
side, and no danger was ever suspected there-
abouts. This sinking process in the Rimutakas
will give humanity such a shock one of these days,
that Joe Ward will be forced to take the line
around the valley that opens out near Martin-
borough. Nothing else above ground will move
him to rectify the evils of that huge political job
—the Rimutaka incline.
* * * *
The other day the Afghan camel-drivers
employed by the Western Camel Co. went on
strike at Wilcannia (N.S.W.) because one of their
number had been summarily discharged. The
company immediately filled their places with
white labor. Ram Dam Singhi’s views on Aus-
tralian labor matters now are probably a bit mixed.
■* * * *
“E.”; A peculiarity of Victorian streams is
that no eels are found in those running north from
the Dividing Range, and no perch or bream were
found, in streams running south until they were
introduced by Anglers’ Societies within recent
years.
* * * *
“War E.” ; The fox in Australia cannot
have the same powers of endurance as the fox we
read of in the Cold Country. I have often run him
down with a slow sheep-dog, and for any sort of
a greyhound Reynard is the “ softest snap ” pos-
sible. The Australian fox can keep ahead of
an island or a tombstone certainly, but he can’t
race a dog—not in my locality, anyhow.
What is claimed to be the largest cedar
tree ever felled in Queensland was lately cut in the
Barron Valley, back from Cairns. Its girth was
36ft. The scrub around it was so thick that it had
to be hand-sawed where it fell.

“Periwinkle” : Jack Clarke, the Murray
fisherman, who died, t’other day, in his tent, from
heart failure, . was a
well-known identity
along “thestream”
for miles above
Echuca to Strath-
mertonway. Like
all Murray no-
mads Jack was a
character, and,
like the rest,
had some
physical
failing (his
heart, it
proved,
though he
guarded
his secret
well)
otherwise
he would
never have
been there.
Never met
a healthy
man camped
on the Murray
yet. Jack, like the
rest of ’em, was a
terror for patent phy-
sics, and like mighty few
of ’em he rarely imbibed.
What he didn’t know about
the river and its denizens no
one could teach him, and it
was one text in his gospel never to let
a big cod beat him. Like the Murray men,
too, he was as full of crotchets as an egg
is sometimes full of badness. All he could
talk of was fish, the price of fish, the close
season for fish; in fact, he was impregnated
with fish—and tobacco. He posed, in his more
ambitious moods, as the first discoverer of
molasses as a cure for mange in dogs. Like all
Australian nomads, he loved the canine tribe,
and the first chop and dose of physic went to
Nelly or Dick, his lop-eared mates. The old-
timers are dropping off one by one —though Jack
wasn’t old, only 47—and with their going the soli-
tude of the Long Thin liiver becomes more
oppressive.
• * • •
“ Jumbuck ” : At the cessation (or alleged
cessation) of recent drought in Central Queens-
land around one dam alone on a big station there
were no less than 40,000 carcases of sheep. During
the drought the station had men always employed
pulling the dead and bogged sheep out of the dam,
yet not a single sheep was skinned, nor was any
save made of the wool. Nearly all the pelts
would have been worth 3s. a piece. The same
story can be told of most of the stations out back
—their neglect to save the half loaf has led to
nearly as much evil as the drought.
• * * »
“P.M.D.” : Re cockatoo-fences, I heard
them so called in ’5B. All the old clearing leases
in the Illawarra district were fenced in with what
we now call dog-leg fences, at that time called
cockatoo fences. When John Robertson’s Free
Selection Act came into force, all the selectors at
first fenced in their selections that way owing to
its simplicity and cheapness. The cockatoo-fence
came first, then the cockatoo—named after his
fence.
Extracts from “ Hop.’s” letter from Hong
Kong :
VVe arrived here this a.m. in a heavy rainstorm after a
remarkably fine passage all the way from Sydney. The
weather has cleared, and it has turned out frightfully hot
and muggy, and though I have seen very little of the
city, I feel quite used up. I am dazed with the utter
novelty of Hong Kong, and looking over my balcony on
the street below is like being a spectator in a nightmare.
What would the O’Sullivan say to seeing potential voters
doing the work of horses and other beasts of burthen ? I
saw one team of a dozen men or so “ stuck up ” on a hill.
They were harnessed to a sort of trolly, laden with soda
water, intended, no doubt, to dilute the whisky of the
“ foreign devils ” who pollute the sacred streets. There
was no driver, but all the human team were chattering
and swearing at each other in Chinese. China is the
only country where the bullock swears at himself when
he gets bogged.
Another thing which struck me as odd was the Chinese
boats which surrounded our ship as we came to anchor,
“manned” mostly by women. I noticed one lady
especially, with a six months old baby strapped to her
back, propelling a sampan with a single oar, the drowsy
youngster’s head wagging back and forth between its
shoulder-blades, keeping time to the action of the
mother’s sculling. The husband and father of this lot
was sitting in a sort of cock-pit with an awning over him,
trying to light a metal pipe.
The harbor of Hong Kong certainly presents some of
the most curious sights I have ever seen. We struck it
by daylight and met a large number of junks of
ail sizes putting out to sea in a stiff breeze. How
vessels built upon such lines manage to keep above
water is a mystery to me. I wonder why poverty is
always so picturesque. There was scarcely a whole sail
among the vagabond fleet, and the crews seemed to have
hardly enough clothes upon them to wad a shotgun. Yet
they looked a happy, well-nourished lot, and certainly
made a charming picture in the grey morning. Though
I have seen little of Hong Kong, I have come to the con-
clusion that society here is divided into two classes —
those who drag or carry their fellow-men about the
streets, and those who are dragged or carried about.
The police amuse me—both the Indian and the Chinese
variety. The only policeman-like things they possess in
common with our variety are the big feet and the
policeman’s walk ; and another thing—the police-
man’s absence when most wanted. I saw this after-
noon, over the way, a ’rickshaw man set upon
by five or six fellow ’rickshawists and hit with
something hard wrapped up in a wet cloth, and, of course,
no policeman about. The poor fellow lay there all the
afternoon gradually regaining consciousness. The whole
thing was so pleasantly done, and the Chinkey who-
wielded the hard substance did it with a, smile on his
face.
So far my impressions of China are received from my
balcony where I sit and swelter and curse the Yellow
Agony. And my superficial observations so far have done
more to commit me unreservedly to the White Australia
doctrine than all the leaders and cartoons of “ your
highly esteemed and very able publication.” To see the
Image of God playing horse in earnest is not an edifying
spectacle.
I leave by the Coptic on Saturday for Frisco via
Shanghai, Yokohama, and God knows what other out-
landish places.

Nonotice (except of a hostile character) will betaken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether tent by post or handed in ; and in
no case mil MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.

A PALTRY EXCUSE.
VOICE AHEAD: “ An ’ what excuse did ’e give fer
not cornin’ shootin ’ with us?”
BILLY CAN: “’E didn’t give none. ’E only said
’is mother told ’im not to go.”
Bodley, in a book just issued out King
Edward’s Coronation, mentions that George IV.,
going to the putting on of his crown, had 18
notorious pugilists surrounding him, dressed as
pages, in resplendent suits of satin and lace.
These pugilists included Cribb, Spring, Belcher,
Richmond (a blackfellow), and all the cream of
the profession. There was an impression that
His Majesty’s loving subjects were waiting for
His Majesty with road metal and large slabs of
hard mud, hence the precaution.
Sale of Phil May’s Drawings.
The Bulletin Newspaper Company, Limited,
has instructed Messrs. Walter Bradley and Co. to
hold an Exhibition and Sale of the Original Pen-
Drawings by Phil May, contributed to The Bul-
letin during 1886, 1887, 1888, and subsequently.
The sale will take place at the auctioneers’ show-
rooms, 324 George-street, Sydney, on Friday,
September 18. The drawings will be on view for
several days prior to sale.
Phil May was in many respects the most
talented pen-draughtsman of his generation, and
he ranks undoubtedly with the greatest English
masters of black-and-white art. Such a critic as
Whistler wrote of him that “his work interests
me far more than that of any man since Charles
Keene, from whom he is quite distinct. There is
a brightness and daintiness in what he does, com-
bined with knowledge. These, together with the
fact that in his drawings the wit is the artist’s,
make a great difference between him and his
contemporaries.”
In Phil May’s Bulletin period many of bis
very best drawings were done. On the whole, his
“line” was never more sure, his “character”
more vivid, and his wonderful dexterity more
manifest than they were in Australia. Some of
the examples of drawing in this collection are
worthy to hang in any gallery in the world. (
In addition to their artistic value, Phil May s
drawings have an Australian value peculiar and
unique. He has drawn or caricatured nearly all
the most notable Australian personalities of the
eighties and nineties—men and women familiar m
society, on the stage, or in public life. Many of
these likenesses have become famous. The
originals are now for sale.
The collection comprises upwards of five
hundred drawings especially chosen from a total
of some eight hundred contributed by May to
The Bulletin. The artistic and historical value
of these, very great at the present day, is bound
to increase as time goes on.

VOU need to take particular care at this time of year
against colds and chills, more especially when throw-
ing off Winter Underclothing for lighter Summer wear,
and the only material which may be relied upon as an
absolute safeguard from the effects of sudden climatic
changes is /ERTEX CELLULAR.
When one wears underclothing of this fabric, he or she
is safe from “taking a chill” as, owing to its peculiar
structure, it operates to retard a too rapid evaporation
of the body’s natural heat in its insensible vapour—the
cause of the “ chill ” —allowing simply any excess of over-
heated vapour to disperse gradually and imperceptibly.
In its functions it resembles in this respect the true
epidermis and acts, in fact, as a supplementary skin, and
for this reason so many medical men order it for invalids
and delicate children, and recommend it to all their
patients for general wear all the year round.

Crone Wrong.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Adelaide Chamber of Commerce has resolved “ that
the expressed intention of the Premier of Victoria to
hasten the construction of extensive irrigation works,
with the object of creating vested interests to enhance
the demands of that State as against South Australia
leaves the Parliament of this State no option but to in
stitute legal proceedings, as they may be ad\ ised, as
speedily as possible to at once protect the rights of the
State.”
Three years ago. it was not thus,
It was— pro tem. —quite otherwise,
“ One Destiny,” we cried, “ for us
One Flag, and wheresoe’er it flies
Fraternal mateship, give and take ;
Not the cold ‘ Where do I come in ? ’
Of greedy States, each on the make
From all the rest, its kith and kin.”
Three years ago of gain or loss
We couldn’t stop our cheers to think—
We cried “ Behold the Southern Cross
And added “ Come and have a drink.
Hang the expense, whate’er it is,
We’ll be a Nation, and we’ll drown
All differences deep in fizz
At every pub. in every town.”
We set them up then. State with State
Shook hands across dividing ropes
That fenced them off, and mate with mate
Quaffed to their high resolves and hopes—
“ White and Australian, that’s the talk.”
vVe swore—“ White skins and Saxon blood
Then steadied down and tried to walk
The new-chalked lines of Brotherhood.
Oh, we felt good three years gone by
Towards our Australian fellow-men,
If one had plugged us in the eye
We’d not have slugged him back again.
But flesh is grass, and all our vows
Were like those made in love or wine—
We still, as a divided house,
Declare “ Thine is, or should be, mine.”
Three Premiers met in Sydney town
To fix and make it past all doubt
How Federal water running down
The Murray should be portioned out,
And when at last they did agree
We shouted “We have been betrayed !
They have conspired, the guilty three,
To sell our rights to Adelaide ! ”
Victoria started digging lakes
And channels deep to fill the same,
And said “ To guard against mistakes
We’re going to play the safety game—
The greed of South Australia’s such
As we must labor to forestall;
While we’ve the chance we’ll take too much
Lest she should leave us none at all.”
Three States at daggers drawn, and one
Effusively fraternal State
About to set the lawyers on
Its sobbingly fraternal mate.
The Murray “ whaler” from our side
Begrudging to his fellow-man
Out of the waters that divide
Enough to fill a billy-can !
And from the North a voice foresworn
Which cries “ Hands off our sugar-cane
Else will the Commonwealth be torn
In pieces ! Give us back again
The right to buy from chief or king,
For colored stuffs his colored ‘boys,’
The right to go forth ‘ blackbirding, ’
Which we surrendered of our choice,”
When points were being figured out,
And Hope told many flattering tales,
The Capital, we had no doubt,
Would be upreared in New South Wales;
But that condition, made express,
And signed, and witnessed, in the deed
Of partnership, the Melbourne press
Implores Australia not to heed !
Alas ! We still are States at heart—
Our chief desires are still the same ;
We swaggered in the better part,
Then lapsed, to play the same old game
For keeps ;to do the best we can
For our own gain, by force or stealth—
Ourselves before our fellow-man,
Our wealth before Our Commonwealth !
WOLLONDALE.
Sydney police allege that they cannot shut
up the gambling shops. The B jlletin favors the
day-labor system as a rule, but it seems to have
failed in this case. Any smart man, given the
use of the police and the law, would contract to
shut up all the shops in a week for £2O, and to
keep them shut at £5 a week afterwards. Why
not let the job out on contract ?

All necessary importation should be done
by the State; there should be no class whose
interests lie in the direction of suppressing local
industry, slandering local products, lying about
local manufactures, and depriving the local
artisan of his bread and butter lest, in earning it,
he should make goods which will compete with
those imported. Or, if we must have the im-
porter, he should have no vote and be shut out of
Jrarliament.. He ranks as a foreign ambassador—
the industrial ambassador of foreign manufac-
turers and no country lets another country’s
ambassador run its Government.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The latest want of confidence motion in
Queensland showed the Philp majority down to
four votes.
The trading-coupon disease is apparently
going to be a live question at next N.S.W. elec-
tion, if the matter is not dealt with sooner.
Quaint logic by Melbourne Argus in a
spasm of joy over Lyne’s “ less drastic ” methods
at the Customs :—“The best proof that there is
no organised effort made to cheat the Customs is
the number of mistakes that are made every day.”
Premier Propsting, of Tasmania, will be
remembered, even in this country of amazing
financiers, as the man who abolished a lot of
taxes and then squared the accounts temporarily
on paper by putting down the small-pox as an
asset. «
There is ihore than a suspicion that one of
the reasons why Reid ran away from Parliament
was to avoid voting for or against the Arbitration
Bill, on which he had yes-noed. And the Barton
Government is actually keeping that Bill hung up
until Reid comes back !
Notice of motion has been given in the
Tasmanian Assembly that all unclaimed moneys
held by “Tattersall” should be annexed by the
State and invested for charitable purposes. Tas-
mania supplies very little of the unclaimed money
though it proposes to seize the lot. •
After all, one perhaps couldn’t expect the
Australian Parliament to take much notice of
alleged Senator Reid’s gibe that members don’t
pay their way. Most of them come from States,
and have taken part in the management of States,
that don’t even make an effort to do it.
In the course of a long and ill-spent politi-
cal career, Yes-No Reid has faced both ways on
almost every conceivable question. He once
moved a vote of censure on Parkes for seeking
to take off the ad valorem duties which a previous
Protectionist Government had imposed.
Now that Irvine has clearly intimated to
them that salaries are still to be docked, and that
his Government has only hesitated on the brink
of sacking some hundreds of them, the Victorian
public servants have a glorious opportunity for
reflecting upon their fine old cant phrase of two
or three years ago—“My country, right, or
wrong.” Then they cheered for the Empire’s
Chamberlain taking the Boers’ country, right or
wrong. It is now up to them to cheer for the
Empire's Irvine taking their own salaries, right or
wrong.
BLESSED IS THE PEACEMAKER.
REV. GENT (severely): “ You young scamp , how dare you tease the poor creature?”
JIM : “Nit, blokes, ’ere’s ’er ole man!”
The very latest candid friend of Philp and
Co. is little Cowley, ex-State Speaker, and his
questions lately are waspish to a degree. As the
policy of the Boodlewraith is to either hobble,
nobble, or gobble inconvenient opponents, Cow-
ley’s fate will be watched with interest.

N.S.W. Assembly will be demanded to inquire
4d. a day . „

proposals involving sundry stamp duties on the
commercial interests of Brisbane. Immediately
the commercial representatives (Poll-taxers almost
to a man) rose in wrath, talked fiercely of a new
