The centenary of Tasmania, to
be celebrated on Sept. 12, recalls that
there are no public records from the
foundation of the province to 1811.
When Governor Collins died sud-
denly in 1810, two officers of State
placed soldiers at the door of the
apartment and burnt all the papers.
In a country in which even “ free ”
persons were flogged pretty regularly
on general principles, there was much
to be concealed. Even women were
whipped through the streets of
Hobart for using abusive language
to an English officer. Unsatisfactory
witnesses were quickly ordered to the
triangles. As late as 1823 an English
Judge ordered a witness to be taken
out of court and receive 100 lashes,
with the object of getting more
information from him. And there are
records of men being ordered 25 lashes every morn-
ing for eight days consecutively, to induce them
to make confession. Among the Englishmen who
ran Australia in those days there was hardly one
who couldn’t give a Spanish inquisitor points in
fiendishness.
During the debate on the Crown Lands
Consolidation Bill in the S.A. Assembly, the
astonished legislators were asked to agree to a
clause which said :
That the unimproved value must not exceed the area
of any section. t
Sounds like the efforts of a draftsman with vine
leaves in his hair.
Letter (in S.M. Herald) from an Austra-
lian who, after spending 28 years in the Common-
wealth, was lured to emigrate by the flattering
word-pictures of agricultural prospects in Canada:
It is the biggest swindle I have ever had anything to do
with. It is a case of misrepresentation altogether, both
as regards the land and everything else. The greater
part of the people that can get back are doing so, but it
is not many that have money to get back with.
The arrangement contemplated under the
new Lands Bill extends the Western Division of
N.S.W. —the one which is practically a
desert and a bad debt —so far east that that divi-
sion will constitute a good deal more than half of
the State. It is a dismal commentary on the
“ splendid natural resources ” to see the admitted
desert crawling all over the map in this fashion.
The latest railway management idea in
Victoria is that Sunday labor is not to be paid for.
Yet Bent’s pay goes along on Sunday just the
same. The cost of the carriage of the rich squat-
ter’s sheep, and the interest on the millions spent
on railway land resumptions, when the rich land-
lord sold his property to the State at ten times its
value, and the interest on money sunk in de,ad and
buried railways built in the landowners’ interest,
are all things that must be made good somehoiv.
Somebody must be robbed.
The Bowels of Compassion is making him-
self felt throughout Queensland. Up near
Croydon, an old battler, aged 72, who has been
fossicking and mining for 52 years, was until
recently in receipt of a weekly dole of 55., but
this sum was considered too liberal, so his pittance
was reduced to half-a-crown a week. His case was
discussed in the State Parliament, but as it is
necessary to avert a land-tax at any cost, it was
decided that the ancient warrior must just feel
glad over his 2s. 6d.
Simultaneously with the passage of the
Naval Agreement Act, the announcement is made
that the flagship Royal Arthur is off for a three
months’ gallivant to Singapore. Though de-
termined on long ago, the trip was kept a dead
secret until the Bill became law. So the
first year of the new agreement sees the Common-
wealth with a flagship for nine months instead of
twelve.
Motion to make the sale and manufacture
of tobacco a Federal Government monopoly was
only lost in the Senate on an even vote, 13 for and
13 against. v The chief argument against it was
the citing of “the ghastly revelations as to State
management at Fitzroy Dock, N.S.W,” Fitzroy
Dock has done more to discredit the principle of
State enterprise than all the preaching of all the
Tory papers for a decade.
As a prognosticator, Queensland Treasurer
Cribb would run a bad second to rainmaker
McCarthy of Broken Hill. In 1902 he predicted
a surplus confidently, and got a huge shortage.
In 1903 he was dead sure of a surplus, and got
another deficit. In 1904 he says he will get a
surplus for an absolute certainty—it is a “dead
bird,” so dead that the burial is a matter of
urgency ; and already on figures he is drifting to
leeward.
AT THE AGRICULTURAL SHOW (FIRST
WEEK IN SEPTEMBER).
The principal exhibit.
Sydney Evening News says bitterly that
that dreadful Socialist Tom Mann wants to
establish a system whereby “anybody who had
anything that could be taxed should be taxed.
The Noose evidently thinks it is saying something
severe when it makes remarks like that. A
country must either tax the person who has any-
thing, or the one who has nothing, and the idea of
taxing the former is a shock to the Henniker
Heaton paper.
If Vic. Premier Irvine has the courage
and the honesty to go the complete hog on his
compulsory land resumption scheme, he has a
unique opportunity. For the first time in its
history there is shortly to be a dissolution of the
Victorian House of Landlords, and if the election
is fought in dead earnest on the land resumption
question, the farmers who are crying out for land
may send back a much more progressive House of
Landlords than is generally anticipated.
The Victorian Citizens’ Deformed League,
according to the Argus, is divided in opinion as
to whether it is advisable to go the whole hog
against Liberals and progress generally. One
speaker advocated a conciliatory attitude as a
good many members.of the League were workers,
and a rigorous and aggressive policy towards
Labor might alarm them. The meeting decided
to remain bogus till after next election, iri the hope
of still throwing dust in the people’s eyes.
A “dead set” will be made, next N.S.W.
Estimates, against allowances to charities run by
the various religions. If the State subsidises, the
State must control. Salvation Booth draws £5OO a
year for running a Sydney restaurant that is really
a competitor with the 4d. and fid. secular eating-
houses. Wages in his place are low, hours are
long, and it is probably no safer whistle to
Booth’s holy sausage than to anybody else’s
secular sausage.
The “expert” report on the barrages
required at the Murray mouth, to keep the salt-
water out of the Coorong when the river is low,
has been laid on the table of S.A. Assembly. It
increases the original estimate of £50,000 (for one
barrage) to ,£113,000 for a complete scheme. Old-
timers are recalling the fact that it is just 50 years
this month since Captain Cadell first made the
passage of the Murray mouth in his little steamer,
to be welcomed at Goolwa as a conquering hero.
Robert Reid is going out of Australian
politics—not that he ever was in them to any
great extent, but he hung on to the rim.
M'Millan is going out to devote himself more
completely to “slops.” Now another mem-
ber who hurls a veiled threat of retirement at
his fellow men is Manifold, whose main occu-
pation in life has been to provide board and
lodging for a few thousand sheep. These
announcements are causing consternation in
the Conservative camp, where the dread is
freely expressed that soon none but Labor
representatives will be left.
There’s one way to satisfy the com-
plaint made by some Senators that the
Senate has not a proper proportion of
Ministers —allow Ministers to be mem-
bers of both Houses,; or, at least, to
speak in both Houses.
Some amendments by Senator Higgs on
the Naval Tribute Bill negatived : That the Bill
be referred to a Select Committee. That the
naval forces be prohibited from using expanding
bullets against the enemy. That tiie naval forces
should not take part in any war unless Great
Britain shall have first offered to submit the cause
of the war to the arbitration of the Hague
Tribunal. This last was specially objectionable.
How on earth can Britain annex a square mile of
country every eight minutes, as it did all through
Victoria’s reign, if it submitted the quarrel to
arbitration ?
The S.M. Herald these times can be
almost as unfair as the Telegraph, or quite as
blind. Monson, English Ambassador at Paris,
having reported that “ the higher duties imposed
in France since 1892 had not affected pricer,” the
Herald remarked that, even if it were true, it
proved nothing : “ Other causes have counteracted
the natural effect of high duties.” And it clinched
the argument: “We have only to ask any house-
wife whether the duties imposed in this State a
year or two ago have raised prices to get a uniform
answer that they have done so.” So if prices
don’t go up under Protection, “ other causes have
operated” ; but if prices do go up under Protec-
tion then the ‘ ‘ other causes ’’—drought, failure of
the harvests, the loss of millions of stock—are all
conveniently forgotten. “Any housewife” for-
gets them; and it is natural enough that the
Herald, being an old woman, should forget them,
too.
Attorney-General Wise, of N.S.W., made
some much-needed remarks, the other day, to
Newman, M.L. A., and the latter gentleman was
ridiculously angry. Newman was one of those
who blathered about the sinfulness of interfering
with the process of law as regards Friedman ; and
when he asked the Attorney-General to interfere
with the processes of the law and remit a fine
imposed on one of his constituents, Wise didn’t
address him on the subject of his political
hypocrisy in nearly such forcible language as he
should have used.
Writes “ Melbournian ” :
Premier Irvine’s forthcoming measure for compulsory
resumption of Vic. squattages, shows how Henry George’s
ideas originated, with regard to property in land being a
mere figment. Once allow that a man can be forced to
sell his land, a principle thoroughly established in Tory
England, and logicians will soon run on to the Henry
George length, that land is like water, sunlight, or even,
air, in its relation to man. There was a curious affair at
Liverpool, while Gladstone was Premier, and Lowe was
Chancellor of the Exchequer. Lowe found that the
Liverpool Corporation had no legal right to certain
wharfage dues, of enormous amount, and took steps to-
confiscate them to the Crown. But Gladstone perceived
that the same arguments would undercut all the Earl of
Derby’s vast property at Bootle. So he squelched Lowe.
For the quarter ended 30th June it is
reckoned that the population of Victoria actually
diminished by 521. The situation of Victoria in
recent years has been this: An immense number
of people who lived in one way and another upon
loans were thrown out of employment when the
huge borrowing boom ceased. A lot more, in the
north-west, were driven out by the drought.
Manufactures, which have been growing very
rapidly, absorbed some of these. The rest would
have gone on the land in the well-watered part of
the State, but the Government had practically no-
land to offer, and the owners of the big sheep-
walks either refused to part with their land, or
asked.such famine prices and rents that it was-
cheaper to go elsewhere.
THE STURDY VICTORIAN FARMER, OR-
KYABRAM’S LEGS.
“What we want is a breed of sturdy, small farmers to
counteract the Labor politics of the cities.”- Irvine.
Sydney Evening News on G. H. Reid
(18/8/’O3)
While in one or two instances—notably those of alliance
with the Labor party and the exclusion of colored labor
from mail steamers under contract with the Common-
wealth Government—he promised a distinct reversal of'
the policy with the Barton Ministry, he was somewhat
indistinct as to the details of his fiscal policy. That he is-
prepared to fight the next general election on the fiscal
issue he announced, but the fight is not to be so much on-
a distinct and wholesale amendment of the existing tariff,
as on the necessity for making a revision in one or two
places.
The great fight between Freetrade and Protection,
is evidently going to be the same old thing—the
fight between the people who practise Protection
because they believe it is good, and the people who.
are going to practise it though they say they know
it to be bad. Anything more despicable in the
way of a Freetrade leader than this Reid creature,
who says he is willing to swallow his principles for
the sake of office, it would be hard to find.

BRUNSWICK, 10 Eveline-street,

Mr. PALMER, March 27th, 1901.
Dear Sir, —My son, who is now fourteen years of age,
has been very ill off and on since he was four years, and
about three years ago he was so ill that the doctor
told me he was developing consumption. He had a ter-
rible cough, which was distressing to listen to; he had
fallen away to almost a skeleton. At this time Mr.
Luxton, Sydney Road, Coburg, advised me to try VITA-
DATIO, which I did. After the first bottle I saw a great
improvement. I continued the medicine ;he gradually
improved until he became quite well. I consider VITA-
DATIO a wonderful medicine, as it has made a different
b oy of my son, whom I never expected to see healthy
again. I can recommend it to anyone, and will be glad
to give any information to anyone suffering from the
same complaint. I remain,
EMILY MACKEY.

Victorian and Queensland Governments
nibble at the very essence of their primary Educa-
tion Acts. The Philp crowd thinks a small weekly
charge would oppress no one, and Irvine thinks a
slight charge on children over 14 would help the
revenue. Free primary schools are the marrow
of the democratic education system.

Max Hirsch spoke to a small collection of
farmer persons at Birchip (Vic.) recently, and
condemned the creature Protection wholesale.
Then he promised, if the Freetraders got into
power, not that they would practise Freetrade,
but that they would practise Protection
sufficiently to get revepue. There would be no
duties on agricultural machinery or implements,
said Max ; and the cadging agricultural persons
cheered vigorously. Freetrader Max didn’t say
his party was determined to practise Freetrade
thoroughly at all costs, and take the duty off the
Chinese egg, the Canadian and Argentine fodder,
wheat, etc. If he had, possibly the cheers
wouldn’t have been so hearty. The Freetrader
gets into Parliament generally by promising to
take the Protection away from the other fellow
and leave it with his constituents.

Anglican Bishop Neligan, of Auckland (M.L.),
at a recent public function, solemnly cautioned
his hearers against encouraging the Government
in making education too easy of access to the
masses. He held that education would be prized
more highly if it were more difficult of attainment.
1 his is the true-blue ecclesiastical way of looking
at the matter, but- no progressive community
wants to prize education in the way of looking
upon it as a rare and wonderful thing—so rare
that a man lies awake at night in sheer amaze-
ment because he has got it. Nor does it want
food, and clothes, and a spare threepenny for the
plate, and being out of gaol, to be so rare that
mi? y P r i ze d in any large, wild way either.
1 hese things should come as a matter of course.
A progressive country doesn’t prize the man who
has education, for education isn’t so unusual as
that. It is simply shocked at the man who hasn’t
got education—which is quite a different mental
attitude.

A more reliable test for
KIDNEY DISEASE
is to place some unne, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass and let it stand until
the next morning. If it is then cloudy, shovvs a sediment like brick-dust, if particles float about in
it, or it is of an unnatural color, then .your kidneys are affected, and no time must be lost in taking
measures to restore. them to health and activity, or you will soon begin to feel the effects by suffering
from Height's Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful complaint.
Rheumatism, Gout,
Lumbago, Sciatica, Anaemia, Debility, Neuralgia, Digestive Troubles, Stone,
Gravel, Bladder Disorders,‘all arise from defective action of the kidneys or retention of
URIQ ACID
is the system, owing to this defective aotion.
The Kidneys.—lt is the province of the kidneys to separate from the blood, surplus water and
urinary impurities. The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three
pints of water in twenty-four hours. In this quantity of water should be dissolved about an ounce
of urea, ten to twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, together with other animal and mineral matter
varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys, through weakness or disease,
are incapable of extracting this solid matter, a certain proportion of it remains in the system, acts as
a specific poison, and produces one or more of the diseases named.

“OnOur Selection.” By “Steele Rudd.
r * ce ’ The Bulletin Newspaper Co. Ltd., Sydney.
“The Disastrous Effects of Socialistic
Legislation.”
Evert newspaper reader at some period of his
existence has, after breathlessly following the
adventures of a deep-sea diver or the exciting ex-
periences of an African explorer for the best part
of a column, experienced the mortification, just
as the shark seized the diver or the gorilla was
about to hit the explorer on the ear, of discovering
that the hero was eventually restored to health
by the use of somebody’s Pink Dog-kennel for
Pale Dogs, or someone else’s Bile Screams. But
the feeling of disgust, bad as it may be, is as
nothing compared to that prompted by the con-
stant recurrence of irrelevant references to the
“ evil effects of socialistic legislation ” which now
permeate the press of Australia. Whether
it be the account of an earthquake at
Warrnambool, or the story of how Tomjiv
Tanna murdered a girl up at the Gulf of
Carpentaria, the stereotyped phrase stands out as
if written in blood. It is a madness, a disease, a
general break up of the intellect. The bogus com-
pany promoter finds in it a plausible excuse for
the failure of the Jumbuck Brass Mining 1 Co.,
Limited, and impatient shareholders are bluffed
by a profusion of incoherent remarks about tho
“ rule of the minority ” and the “ tactics of irre-
sponsible agitators.” A meeting is held to con-
sider the population question and the exodus from
Victoria, and speakers of the M.L.C. type smile
with the air of satisfaction a man might assume
who had unravelled a complex problem, and
grunt forth the usual “socialistic legislation”
lie. They always have a shocking example
and “know a case in which a man,” &c., &c.
That man was always coming here to start aii
industry which would employ exactly 1000 men
and ahorse, but when he heard of our socialistic
legislation he died immediately from lingering-
consumption in the feet, and left a wife and 27
small children. The constant repetition of the
libel in the press, under the most unexpected
circumstances, gets on the nerves of the most
patient, and though a few may be carried away in
the rush, the undertow will assuredly carry them
back again. The casual reader has no desire to
read that “capital is being driven out of the
country,” when he knows that not one penny
ever left the Commonwealth as the result of
legislation for the improvement of labor condi-
tions. The visionary and mysterious investor
who intended establishing a factory for the
manufacture of post-holes and doorways, but
decided not to do so,” etc., etc., is also begin-
ning to lose his old convincing aspect. We don’t
want any industry established that cannot pay
a fair wage, and conform to our labor conditions
though we have no objections to hearing of it
m the proper place, but when the reference to it
crops up in the account of a dog fight or divorce
case it makes the public feel inclined to throw
down the paper and go out in search of some fat
lory person to kick.
♦
M‘Millan made one candid confession during his
speech on the Arbitration Bill in the House of
Representatives:
This class of legislation was no doubt the corollary of
Protection. It was not the corollary of Preetrade, and,
therefore, he was only consistent in opposing it.
True, down to the ground. Freetrade means the
right to buy in the cheapest market, no matter
where, and to get the benefit of the cheapest
labor, no matter what. Under that system there
might be a pretence of arbitration, but it could
never be more than a pretence; for if it raised
wages, and therefore for a time the cost' of pro-
duction, the goods would be supplied by some
cheap foreigner who has no arbitration courts.
An Arbitration Court can only fix wages; it can’t
keep the work in the country. At any rate, it can’t
keep the work in the country except by fixing tho
rates of pay, etc., very low. If it wants to fix high
rates of pay, then, unless it wants to risk driving
the work out of the country, it must be backed up
by Protection. Two things the working man
expects, and the country generally wants—work
to do and decent pay for doing it. Neither
arbitration nor (within certain limits) Protection
alone can give both ; but, dovetailed together, they
fill the bill. Indeed, arbitration without Pro-
tection may be worse than nothing. Without it
local people may get the work to do at some
price ; with it they may not be able to get it at all.
The Freetrader who worries about arbitration is,
therefore, as ,M ‘Mill an suggests, wasting time.
But that isn’t, as M’Millan thinks, an argu-
ment against arbitration. It is simply an
argument against being a Freetrader. The doctor
who tells you that his medicine won’t do you any
good while you remain drunk isn’t arguing
against the medicine ; he is simply arguing
against the drunkenness.
—
The Federal Arbitration Bill is a horrible thing
altogether to Launceston (Tas.) Examiner; but the
proposal to give preference to unionists in certain
cases simply takes away the lady’s breath. “An
outrage on the large section of workers outside the
unions, ” she gasps; and then calls on this large
section to rise against the horrid proposal to
“brand them as an inferior class.” But, even
though it might fairly do so, the Bill does not
brand the non-unionist as inferior to the unionist.
It merely says that it is better for the country
that industrial disputes should be settled by arbi-
tration than by strikes, and it therefore proposes,
other things being equal, to give preference
to those men who bind themselves to settle
their disputes in that way. The law doesn’t
propose to prefer them as men who hold certain
opinions; it simply proposes to prefer them as
men who have entered into an agreement to do
things in the way which is best for the country.
A similar, privilege is enjoyed by the Examiner
every day. The Government says that it is a
good thing, for the country that newspapers should
be registered ; and those that are registered it
agrees to carry at a specially low rate through
the post. Even the Examiner isn’t foolish
enough to call that an outrage, or “a travesty on
democracy.”
It s a cold time for the railway men in South
Australia as well as in Y.ctoria. At the Adelaide
yards they are now booked off every third day,
bringing earnings down to 4s. 6d. per day. In
Queensland much the same thing is happening,
an .d the employees are dreading “further econo-
mies. So the “vigorous public works policy,”
which has usually been so popular with wage-
earners, comes home to roost. The “ vigorous
public works policy,” in Australia, meant bor-
rowed money spent on works that won’t pay their
way (usually on the pretext that they are to
“develop the country”); and, as Bull Cohen
won t think of accepting a lower rate of interest
than he bargained for, and as the taxpayers are
protected by their special representatives against
further taxation to any great extent, there is only
one alternative the wages of the State’s servants
have to be whittled away in the desperate
struggle to make up Bull Cohen’s pound of
flesh.


The platitudinous Joe Carruthers’ latest is
his announcement at Paddington that, if he
becomes N.S.W. Premier, he will “ alter the
present system of financing so as to restore the
country’s credit.” Being asked if that meant
that he would stop borrowing there was a dead
silence. You can get any number of platitudes
out of Joe, but no particulars..
Attorney-General Wise, when interviewed
about the local manufacture of locomotives, in-
stanced the advantage the State would have in
the closer inspection of the work during con-
struction. To this Sydney Telegraph scornfully
replies :
It may be remarked that we have a highly-paid officer in
London who looks after this work of inspection.
The Telegraph does not complain of. paying a
man to reside in London and do the inspection
there. Not a bit. It is when he is to be em-
ployed here and spend his high salary here it dis-
covers something grotesque.
T. Macdonald-Paterson, M.H.R., just
returned from abroad, told an interviewer that on
the same steamer with him were three emigrants
for Maoriland whose joint capital was £25,000;
also that these were not the only small capitalists
aboard for the same destination. That’s the way
Maoriland’s intensely socialistic legislation is
“ driving capital out of the country.

ASPORTING NOTIONS.
Unless he is of the silvertail variety, the way of
the gambler is hard in Sydney. The other night the
police rushed a kip in Oastlereagh-street, and arrested
about 150 men in the act of two-upping. The names and
occupations of the whole crowd duly appeared in
a portion of the daily press. The members of the
school were fined 50s. each, with the usual alternative,
and in addition to the fine they had to endure a Stiggins-
like lecture from the stipendiary on the evils of gambling.
Now, the question is, would Stipendiary Wilshire have
fired off all that snuffle had the police, in a moment of
mistaken zeal, raided Tattersall’s Club ?
* * * *
True Bill ” s Last week’s police swoop on big
Sydney “ two-up ” school has caused consternation among
the many smaller and one or two bigger ventures in the
same line. Money in the business is astonishing. Pro-
prietors in a large way of two-up accumulate wealth
remarkably fast. Writer has heard of one place worth
£l5O a week to its proprietor. “ Two-up ” schools have
taken the place of the time-honored pub. with the super-
annuated bruiser, who is mostly boss or chief assistant in
the concern Writer knows one pug. who, a few years
ago, started a “ two-up ” school in the centre of the city
with about £5 capital, which went to fix up “ the ring ”
and pay the first week’s rent; now he owns several houses
and has a healthy banking account. Two other Sydney
thumpers, finding themselves unequal to winning any
more fights, embarked in a “two-up joint” six months
since, and thrived to such an extent that t’other day they
bought another show out for £l5O, and are running both
with considerable profit, and recent raid isn’t scaring
many of the ordinary habitues away either.

. Ancient, 17-year-old Marvel, by Marvellous
from La Belle, and late Geo. Hill’s sensation of a dozen
years ago, is still in the flesh and apparently able to
gallop some, judging by the fact that owner entered him
for Williamstown (Vic.) Spring Handicap (lm.) Saturday
last. Handicapper, however, settled all likelihood of a
start by weighting him similarly (9.4) to last Caulfield
Cup winner, Lieutenant Bill. Year 1891 saw Marvel beat
Carbine by four lengths in All-Aged Stakes at A.J.C.
Autumn meet. Three years later he went to the stud in
Queensland and has been there ever since.

Unanimous verdict of touts, trainers, and early
morning watchers generally of work at Randwick is that
M.L. champion Cruciform tops all ever seen on that
track at galloping, and her stable mate Orloff does more
than well. On Thursday, Cruciform capped previous
records for ljm. on the grass. With hurdles 10yds. out,
she covered first 2fur. in 27sec., the middle half-mile in
51sec., 6fur. in 1,19, ljm. in 2.12, and last mile of journey
went in 1.45 Gallop was extraordinarily fast, but Cruci-
form had a lightweight up and was scantily shod. Orloff
got over a mile in 1.45 J. Same morning Epsom favorite
Famous negotiated 9fur. in 1.58, last eight occupying 1.44.
* * * *
Duke of Grafton won Rawsori Stakes (lm. Ifur.)
at Randwick, Saturday, almost unbacked—tens being
plentiful at barrier rise. On his running, coming A.J.C.
Derby looks fairly good for him, and, unless something
very much out of the way crops up meanwhile, he’ll strip
a warm favorite. D. of G. carried a lOlbs. penalty, as also
did another Derby colt (Cherson). Bassini’s infliction was
61bs. over. Dumont, Belah, and Kitty Grafton had w.f.a.,
while Jacobite and Postulate were allowed 51bs. Bar
Belah, none of the 3yr.-olds was backed for money.
Duke of Grafton, who kept within striking distance
throughout, came up on the rails near the end and,
cutting Ibex down at the distance, outraced Cyanide and
Dumont and won, full of go, with last named second.
Difficult to see how any of the eligibles named can have a
chance against “the Duke” for the blue riband if he
keeps right, especially as there’s every indication that
Dr. Frazer’s crack colt has picked up the form which
pulled him through the Debutante Stakes and Mari-
byrnong Kate—latter with lOlbs. penalty.

Present condition of Melbourne Derby and
Cup favorite Emir brings to mind that trouble has
always been experienced in the preparation of Emir’s
dam’s progeny. The New Woman, by Newminster, did
no good. That fine Bill of Portland horse, Eminence,
went queer in the wind early, albeit races were subse-
quently appropriated by him. Wallace’s get, Beanba,
retired at 3 years, suffering similarly to Eminence.
Heloise (full sister to Beanba), though she shaped bril-
liantly at 2 years, has done nothing since ; and now
Emmie’s next (Emir) by Wallace is causing his trainer
and the public generally, who were in a hurry to “ be
on,” considerable anxiety.
* * * *
Sydney Tatt.’s Tramway and Spring Handicaps
at Randwick, Saturday, seemed to disclose winners of
coming Epsom and Metrop. in stable-chums Marvel Loch
and Faithful, either of whom looks good for the first-
named event, while Faithful appears more than a possi-
bility for the double. Marvel Loch (8.12 up) annexed
Tramway Handicap (6fur.) very easily indeed, and shaped
like continuing strong ; while Faithful spread-eagled her
field in Spring Handicap (lm.) and came home lengths to
the good. Such an easy win hasn’t been seen for years.
Faithful humped 8.6 on Saturday, but her Epsom and
Metrop. loads are only 7.9 and 7.5 respectively. Marvel
Loch’s burdens in Epsom and Metrop. are 7.12 and 7.7.
*** , *
Some of N.S.W. Minister Crick’s arguments in
opposition to Mr. Sleath’s Tote Legalisation Bill were
amusing. .The machine would bring betting within reach
of the poorer classes, he said. Not bad this, in face of
fact that many Noses in the paddock lay the odds to
2s 6d., and all rush ss. bets, while they take a “ bob” in
the Leger and a “ tray bit ”on the “ outer-outer.” Tote
couldn’t go that low—the works wouldn’t stand it.
* * * *
Blood stallion Althotas, taken to the Manning
three months ago, kicked a fence and shattered one of
his legs so badly that his destruction was necessary.
Althotas was by Phil Athol from Goldine and a fine per-
former. Among other big things he won the Metrop. 11
years ago. %
Melbourne’s big sale of racehorses on Monday
saw Malster secured for £1350 by N.S.W. studmasters,
Messrs. J. and W. Thompson. Malster (foaled in 1897) is
by Bill of Portland from Barley (imp.), by Barcaldine
from Lonely by Hermit, and was bred by the late M.
O’Shannassy. For Mr. R. Orr (who bought him as a year-
ling at a cost of 810 gs.) Malster did well, winning, among
other things, V.R.CJ. and A.J.C. Derbies, and filling
places in Futurity Stakes, Caulfield Guineas, and Mel-
bourne Cup.

Yankee mail, in on Friday, brought information
that world’s mares’ mile trotting record of 2.3 J, long
owned by Alix, was lowered recently. Lou Dillon
covered first quarter in 32J, half-mile I.IJ, 6fur. 1.32 J
and full distance in 2.3 J. Conditions were none too
favorable. Lou Dillon cost her owner 12,500d015.
* * * »
Melbourne’s patent Speedway, which is to
develop the pacing powers of the trotters of the local
naicest, and whereon the common owner of the fast trotter
or speedy pony may not train his nag, was duly
“opened” last' Saturday. Afterwards, Waxellency
Madden, who was presented with a golden key, was driven
round behind pacer Almont. Whatever the Speedway
may eventually develop into, it would at present be
scorned as a training track’by a trainer who knew the
rudiments of his business, being merely a sandy track
with a slight clay dressing atop. This is nice and spongy
for the delicate leg-machinery of the trotters but not con-
ducive to speed, and when the North (vind gets its summer
work in, it will be averitableSaharain motion. Between the
two tracks a few anaemic shrubs and incipient trees had
been planted, but Saturday’s crowd of 6000 or 8000 mostly
walked ov'ir these and swept them into a sandy grave
with its.skirts. Also several stiff-backed seats'have been
placed whereon the multitude is expected to sit
back and admire the trotters. Writer opines that if the
Speedway becomes a popular resort, the club will have to
evolve some way of restraining the local youth and infant
from meandering over the middle of the track, otherwise
it will have to include the maintenance of an ambu-
lance among its expenses. The concern might
become a popular resort if it were open on Sun-
days, but the club is a strictly Sawbath - keeping
institution. Besides the spider-wheeled trotting sulky,
the club prohibits the tumultuous motor and the pesti-
ferous bike. Saturday’s “ parade ” was open to all and
sundry, and the “sundry” duly arrived, including the
milk-oh boy on his pet prad and the vegetable man with
his turn-out. Besides these, there were some splendid
bits of horseflesh whipping lightly along before airy
four-wheeled buggies (bike-wheeled, pneumatic-tyred,
skeleton-framed affairs) that would serve to train them
just as well as the lightest sulky ever built. Also, there
was a crimson-haired damsel who rode her horse, wrong
side, like a centaura, and a lanky youth who rode his like
a monkey on a stick.
* * * *
“Via”: The 5-year-old Mac Donovan (by im-
ported Fitzdonovan) is a “ cunning ” tip for this year’s
Caulfield Cup. Weight 7.1. Having no form, the V.R.C.
Handicapper slung him in amongst the rubbish at 6.7 ; so
should MacD. win at Caulfield his 101 b. penalty for the
2-mile race will only put him 21b. over his Caulfield
weight. Such a paper chance of collecting the two Cups
has not been seen for many a year and if the sturdy little
chap gets one leg in, handicapper Menzies will have an
anxious minute or so on the first Tuesday in November.
It may be a strong point in the horse’s favor that he is
trained by the same man that handled Lieutenant Bill
last year.

Crack Victorian mare Athamoora is emulating
one-time failing of Sydney’s speedy nag T.M.S. by
running into places—particularly seconds ; how many
only a crank would take the trouble to find out. Atha-
moora is a high-class galloper on the track, but seems to
have bad luck with the colors up. T’other day she
finished second to Debenture in Club Handicap and,
later same afternoon, squelched everything but
Mac Donovan. Looked as if, with a huskier lad up,
mare must certainly have accounted for Debenture. It’s
not good enough now to leave her alone whenever the
coin comes from the right quarter, hence Mo will
probably gather in further heaps of shekels through the
same medium unless the luck changes suddenly.

the semi-aristocratic mantle of amateurism, and now ride

Present is certainly a Maoriland year in Aus-

fields brilliantly. Arnst closed on Melbourne-Warrnambool
road bike race. “ Bill ” Lyons won biggest Sydney Tatt.’s

thing he cuts for a great shaking.
* * * *
Coroner’s inquiry concerning death of Sydney
boxer, Charles Best (“ Young ”), ended in a disagreement,
though ’tis known that ten of the jurors were for a
verdict of accidental death. Post mortem proved the
bodv to be that of a healthy, well-trained man. Bones
of the skull had not been injured, but laceration of the
brain occurred through a blow or a fall —more likely the
latter, judged by the locality of the trouble. Evidence
went to show that the contest was anything but hurtful
to either, and had been absolutely tame till Best went
down the first time in the 16th round. Then he rose and
the next fall ended matters.
* * * *
A padded floor would have saved the life of the
deceased boxer Young. Objection to padding is that it
tires boxers and makes for slow footwork, but a slow
fight is better than a dead boxer, so since Young’s
accident all the floors of Sydney boxing rings have
been padded, much to the disgust of the boxers
themselves. During the past 20 years there have been
only three fatal boxing contests in Sydney, and in the
case of both Oribb and Stewart death was due to the
unhealthy physical state of the unfortunates. Cribb was
so bad that had he bumped a policeman while getting on
a moving tram-car he would probably have dropped dead,
and a sustained course of pick and shovel work would
certainly have proved fatal. The blow on the point does
not cause death ; otherwise, 9 out of 10 boxers would be
killed in short order, as that is generally the hit that
ends the scrap.
* * * •
The Gaiety Theatre (Sydney), in which the
young boxer, Charles Best, was killed, has a strange and
eventful history. A little while back, Otto Oribb,
another young boxer, was killed in the same building,
which is the property of the Australasian Holy Catholic
Guild. The place was originally called the Catholic
Guild Hall. Archbishop Vaughan delivered the
foundation-stone address, and at long intervals lec-
tures were delivered in the hall by many celebrities.
Although still in the hands of the pious Guild, the title
Guild Hall was dropped (except when religious lectures
v/ere delivered) since the place was opened, under
lease, by the late Fred Marshall, the comedian,
with “ Quilp, ” on Boxing Night, 1880. The
Montague-Turner co. gave its first season of opera
at the Gaiety, and “ The Private Secretary” was produced
there for the first time by Frank Thornton. Among
other theatrical celebrities who have appeared at the
Gaiety may be named W. E. Sheridan the tragedian,
Johnny Sheridan the comedian, Mrs. Brough, Annie
Mayor", Marion Melrose, Myra Kemble, Alfred Dampier,
and Wybert Reeve. For several years after it ceased
to be used as a playhouse the hall was let as a
“ dancing academy ”of the cheap and nasty sort. After
the Chows had hired the place for a season of Chinese
opera, the Guild leased the premises to the Gaiety Sport-
ing Club as a boxing saloon. Throughout all the varying
fortunes of the hall the Guild has reserved the upstairs
rooms facing Castlereagh-st. as a council-chamber and
dispensary. *
• * » *
The Victorian Royal Agricultural Society has
positively spent £6OOO this year in new buildings and
general improvements at its Flemington show grounds.
The Melbourne people, whose shillings are mainly
responsible for the prosperity of the V.R.A.S., had given
up all idea of ever getting a smarter show for their shil-
ling, but it now seems that the good old Tory capitalists,
who represent the beef-and-muttonbone of the country,
have been saving up this burst of expenditure for a satire
on the recent fiction of drought and distress which was
urged as an excuse for the rise in the price of meat, etc.
The Kyabrammers are mocking their town victims. If an
amazed Melbournian asks the wherefore of the new
pavilion for dairy cattle, and an outlay of £l9OO in
enlarging the sheep sheds, a jovial farmer slaps him on
his back and says—“ Result of the awful drought, me
bboy.”
* * * *
N.9.W. Gun Club made its purchase of Botany
bird-butchering site at a critical period. The adjacent
ground—part of Lord’s estate—has been cut up into
graveyard allotments and sold to workmen on time pay-
ment, and small cottages are springing up. What may
now be hoped for is that some irate workman who wants
quiet because his wife is struggling at the front gate of
Eternity will get over the fence during a match and
knock the heads of some of the pigeon-shooting crowd
against a rock. A few months ago, by the way, these
lordly hen-assassins protested against the smell from a
humble but useful boiling-down emporium, which was
wafted their way while they were being neither useful,
manly nor ornamental at their weekly bloodshed. The
destroyers of small birds have a considerable opinion of
themselves.
* * * *
Tasmania has the walking craze very bad. Any
Wednesday or Saturday, in a score of places, may be seen
hatless, disordered individuals of both sexes, and all ages,
tearing along a road, with arms flogging the air and legs
wobbling frantically, followed by a howling, perspiring
mob, mostly on bikes. Saw the finish of one match
recently. Couldn’t pick out the successful man, owing
to the crowd, but presently perceived a person lying on
his back, with about 16 others rubbing his chest and
pouring water on his head. “Is he the winner ? ” I in-
quired of a leary-looking person near me. ‘ ‘ Him ? Why,
that’s the judge. The cove as won plugged ’im in the
wind with ’is ’ead.”
Collingwood, the Melbourne plebeian suburb,

tion-mongering sky-pilot, owns the most up-to-date tote-
shop in the Commonwealth. The owner always keeps
himself well abreast of the times, and, of course, he
“ toted" the Papal election. Sarto (now Pius X.) paid a
dividend of 16s. ; but had Rampolla, who started as
favorite, got home, the dividend would have been about

* * * *
“San Remo”: Re that Yankee tin-pot yacht
race, Britain has gone under with the old regularity.
Cables say that the British crew was outclassed by the
Yankees. John Bull seems to be inferior to Uncle Sam—
firstly, in ship construction ; secondly, in seamanship ;
thirdly, in originality. This last is plainly seen by
making a comparison of the names of ships. The Yanks
display originality by their choice of national and appro-
priate names —America, Columbia, Constitution, Defender,
Vigilant, Volunteer, Reliance, etc. The Briton, with his
lists of 1., 11., 111. Valkyries, Shamrocks, etc., shows
about as much originality as a Jersey cow. Thechoiceof
an appropriate name for most things is easy enough, pro-
vided you have brains. So, while the Britisher bangs on
to his numbers, this scribe’s money will go on the Yank,
for skill mostly goes with brains, and names indicate the
brain status of the nominator. [As a set off to this, the
Yankee labels his streets in New York by numbers up to
96th street, and the cross streets he numbers as avenues.
And in London you find such strangely picturesque names
as Strand, the Poultry, Threadneedle-street, Paternoster-
row, Bishopsgate-street Within, and such places as Bark-
ing, Tooting and East Ham. —Ed. B.]
* * * *
Guide Joe Warbrick, killed by M.L.’s Wai-
mangu geyser, Sunday, was once amongst that country’s
most brilliant native footballers, and went ’Ome with the
Maori team in 1899. One of the M.L. combination in
Australia nearly 20 years ago, he showed marvellous drop-
kicking. Sending the oval whizzing over the bar from
midway ’tween goals was an ordinary thing to him.
Warbrick chased the leather 17 years, and then took to
farming in the Bay of Plenty district, at which occupa-
tion he prospered. Joe was brother to late Bill (once
famous as a full-back), who died of consumption, con-
tracted while he had charge of Lavender Bay (Sydney)
baths.
• * * *
Poor Mons. Victor left indirect evidence to
prove that he didn’t shoot himself by accident. A little
while before his death he called upon Arthur Russell, the
dentist, and wished him good-bye in a cheery casual sort
of way, at the same time presenting a souvenir to one of
Dr. Russell’s professional assistants. The age of Victor
was the chief thing the matter with him. He had got
well into the sixties, he had lost his reputation as a strong
man by taking up other lines of business and doing no
good in them, the financial outlook was bad, and death
had no terrors for the old athlete.

Responses to Australian Amateur A.thletic
Union’s questions (prompted by Q.A.S.A.) re eligibility
or otherwise of aboriginals and half-castes to compete
with the lilywhite section are favorable. Consequently,
henceforth “'Bundaburra Jimmy” and his fellow
Australians (provided it can’t be proved against them
that they have shaped for money) may be seen
rubbing shoulders with Percy de Vere-Jones on the
track and in the dressing-room, though how Percy
will stand the association is yet to be seen.
“Amachaw” athletics have been practically dead
for a long time. The N.S.W. governing body meets
often and deliberates lengthily, but hasn’t struck a blow
worth mentioning for close on three years—this season it
hasn’t, so far, made a move of any sort. “ Let us be an
Athletic Association in something more than name, let
us be alive,” pleaded a disgusted delegate at last week’s
council meeting, but the Association resolutely remained
dead.
* * * *
By incessant cabling of individual performances
in England, the visit of the Marylebone C.C. combination
is being cleverly boomed. When a bowler takes a few
wickets at small cost, the cable tells of it; but when a
bowler has over 100 runs smacked off him and not a
wicket (which happened to Braund in a recent Gentlemen
v. Players fixture) the fact is sternly ignored. Taken all
through, there is a great deal more booming of the coming
team than any previous combination; and it’s getting
wearisome.
* * * *
Plugger Bilmartin left Maoriland for Australia
last week to be in readiness for some of the sugar plums
which are promised for this season. Bill’s retirement
from the track has been announced many times, but he
always goes back to it like the drunkard to his cocktail.
That Austral Wheel Race scandal took a deal of the glitter
off the Yankee’s performance and reputation, and
Bilmartin will have to put up a lot of straight riding,
absolutely devoid of the slightest taint of suspicion, to
regain his lost popularity.
• » * »
Newtown team lined up favorites for final of
N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union premiership decided Saturday.
Course was from Homebush to Granville and back—about
21m. Contest hummed all the way. Northern Suburbs
(other team concerned) made things so hot that end of
the first half of the trip saw four Norths and three New-
towns round the flags simultaneously in 28m. 40sec. Last
400 yards opened with Biden (Norths) leading Ellis (New-
town) by a yard. Close to finish Ellis (amateur
champion of the continent) jumped, and breasted the
tape barely half a wheel best. Right on the back tyres
of the leaders were Jeffrey (N.S.), Kennedy (N.S.), and
Toose (N.). Time (58.22 f) was fast. Norths won, points
being 15 to 21. Successful club now owns amateur road
supremacy for a year and gets the Dunlop shield, while
gold medals go to first four men.
6 * * * »
J. Arnst, winner of the Warrnambool-Mel-
bourne road race is 21 years old, stands sft. 9in, scales ]2st.
in the buff, and was born at Tai Tapu, near Christchurch
(M.L.). His brothers, Robert and William, who
figured in the race, are also splendid types of athletes.
Robert and J. survived that awful Timaru-Christ-
church road race of 112 miles over snow-covered roads,
through oceans of mud, and across flooded rivers. The
Maoriland cycling trade sent Robert over as second string.
When Ralston and Calder came over last year as M.L.
reps, they prophesied great things of the Arnsts, who were
then novices. The brothers accomplished the fastest and
second fastest time for the great road event, and finished
first and fifth. The machines they rode were, of course,
fitted with Dunlop tyres, 7 inch cranks and 88in. gear,
and weighed about 261 b. When they aren’t breaking
records on bikes they graft on their father’s farm, which
probably accounts for their splendid condition.
Concerning J. Arnst’s world’s record. Fastest
time made in Paris at the Roubaix race, one mile more
than the Warrnambool-Melbourne sprint, was Aucou-
turier's 9h. 12m. 30sec. last year—one hour 29m. 30sec.
slower than Arnst. Exact length of Arnst’s ride was
163Jm., average about 21J miles an hour and about
2m 48sec. to the mile.
* * * *
Don Walker and other cracks from this side are
going over to Maoriland to liven up cycling in that now
well-nigh grogless land. From a spectacular standpoint
the tour will doubtless be a success, and popular with the
Pig Islanders ; but the cyclists over there, especially the
budding ones, don’t view the invasion with any enthusi-
asm, as it means a lot of the plums will leave the State.
Walker has started training and will compete in the bene-
fit race-meeting to Bobby Walne on the 26th. For this
good cause the St. Kilda ground has been granted free of
charge, and everything points to a respectable sum
being handed over to that deserving wheeler.
Aptly named French bikist Champion, on
Charles River Park track, Boston (U.S.), July 14, easily
drove his motor bike over a mile in 58 2-5. Same day, in
a paced race, American Caldwell covered a mile in
1.11 2-5, lowering the previous world’s best (Contenent’s)
by 4 3-ssec. ; and, going on, he threw five miles behind in
world’s record time of 5.51 3-5. Many intermediate miles
were startling—particularly the fifth, negotiated in 1 8.

First two matches of this year’s rubber between
Victoria and N.S.W. baseball teams, at Sydney Cricket
Ground, Saturday and Monday, went to home team by
an innings and 4 runs, and 11 runs. Same State has won
each year since contests were initiated in 1900 ; last time
they scored the honors in Melbourne. Latest result gives
N.S.W. absolute possession of the championship shield.
Neither side showed high-class form in their initial
engagement, and the game bristled with errors. Things
improved considerably the second time, but there was
nothing to indicate that ’Murka’s national pastime is
going to take on in Australia ; for, though it has been with
us for years now, players generally don’t seem to have
come on, and the public won’t enthuse ever so little
* * * *
Mr. Alfred Yockler’s appropriation of Mel-
bourne Tatt.’s billiard tourney, t’other day, surprised
nobody—he had been favorite for weeks before. A.V.
was a jockey once (in the Seventies), riding for late Hon.
James White, John Tait, Whittingham and other owners.
With one mare alone he won 26 races. Mr. Vockler is an
old cueist and has, with fair starts, at different time 9
beaten such good men as Weiss, Memnott and Lindrum,
and acquitted himself well opposed to Stevenson in a
series of pyramid games.
“Bill” Lyons, winner of Sydney Tattersall’s
fat billiard tourney and a big proportion of the conse-
quent unloading by Mo generally, was a Maorilander
originally, and then a Sydney “ book ” for years, till he
made his home in M.L. again. Well-remembered nags
Too Soon and Cambria belonged to him. This is the
third time he has secured a place in same fixture. Six
years ago he whacked second and third with odds-layer
Oxenham. Then he had a great tussle with a fellow-
“ book ” and friend, Paddy Bourke, in a final. Further
distinctions were the winning of a tourney at Randwiek
Bowling Club, Auckland Tatt.’s big annual event a year
or two back, and the position of runner-up to Mr. Rum-
ball in first amateur championship of N.S.W., two years
since. Like most sporting men, Mr. Lyons is super-
stitious, and through that weakness hedged a few hun-
dreds of the coin that would otherwise have come to him
—the prompting cause being a dream by his favorite
daughter (in Maoriland), some time before the final, that
her father met a man named “ Redhill” and was beaten.
Strangely enough, Mr. Lyons’ last opponent was Frank
Hill, whom, at the time of the dream, few expected to
survive the next round.

The success of the Maoriland footbal ets in
Australia has given the Rugbyites of those islands a
slight attack of swelled head. The amateur question is
likely to prove a stumbling block to the proposed Lnglish
tour. The Maoriland appetite for scalps is now so keen,
however, that in all probability a first-class team will be
sent out on the off chance of being recogn sed as
amateurs in a like manner to the Australian cricketers.
Anyhow, King Dick hinted that if funds were not forth-
coming from private sources to send the team away, the
Govt, would finance it!! Even Owe’Sullivan never
dreamed of charging a football tour to the loan account
under the heading of “ reproductive works” : but then
Seddon was always as original as he is masterful.
* * * *
Owing to Melbourne Exhibition track being so
narrow that big fields only court disaster, it’s suggested
that not more than eight cyclists be allowed to race on
that track at once. Something will have to be done,
since the Exhibition track can’t be widened—unless on
the inside, and it is small enough at present; and the
rapidly-growing army of cyclists makes it apparent that
such a minimum number of starters will shortly limit the
running to one race as a day’s programme. In \ ictoria,
the principal events are run over two miles, and the
prospect of about 30 heats of eight riders, with semi-finals
and final, is dreadful. The only remedy is a new and
up-to-date track and ground.

Henry Copeland, it is alleged, will prob-
ably return to active politics in N.S.W. when a
High Commissioner is appointed in London, and
State Agents-General become very obscure and
superfluous potatoes. Possibly his present visit

stand it after the more cultured effluvium of

Legal Notice.
PURSUANT to an Order of the Court of
Chancery of the County Palatine of Lan-
caster Manchester District made in an action re
Margaret Bagnall Chadwick v. de Jong 1902 C
128. The person claiming to be Henry Bagnall
Parkinson son of one Adam Parkinson by his mar-
riage with Ellen Bagnall a daughter of the above-
named Margaret Bagnall is by his Solicitors to
come in and prove his claim to be the said Henry
Bagnall Parkinson at the Chambers and by the
time below mentioned. And in the event of the
death of the said Henry Bagnall Parkinson any
person or persons claiming to be his devisee or
devisees or real or personal representative or
representatives and all persons claiming through
or under any such persons are by his or their
Solicitors on or before the 7th day of December
1903 to come in and prove his or their claim or
claims before the Registrar of the Manchester
District of the said Court situate at Duchy
Chambers No. 4 Clarence Street Manchester
England or in default thereof the said Henry
Bagnall Parkinson will be presumed to be dead
and to have died intestate and without leaving
any lawful issue him surviving.
Monday the 21st day of December 1903 at 12
o’clock at noon at the said Chambers is appointed
for hearing and adjudicating upon the said claims.
Any person or persons having any knowledge of
the whereabouts of the said Henry Bagnall
Parkinson if living or of the date and place of his
death if dead are requested to communicate with
the plaintiffs’ Solicitors.
The said Henry Bagnall Parkinson went to
Australia in or about the year 1855 and was last
heard of from Mount Pleasant Crooked River
Victoria.
Dated this 21st day of July 1903.
HUBERT WINSTANLEY
Registrar.
Messieurs Sutton Elliott Turnbull & Mayne
13 Snnng Gardens
M incbester England
Plaintiffs’ Solicitors.

Lipton’s persistent efforts with his Sham-
rocks deserve better luck. But to wrest anything
from the grasp of the eager Yank, who keeps his
brain as keen as a razor with D.C.L. whisky, is a
feat for the gods. Of late years, D.C.L. has got
a great hold of the American whisky market. It
wets the brightest intellects in that go-ahead
country, and nerves the strongest determinations.
The discomfiture of Lipton, the tea-man, is directly
due to D.C.L. So says the Australian agent for
that mighty spirit, but Th%Bulletin takes his
assertion with—well, a drop of bitters.*
The fiitile Joe Cook on the Arbitration
Bill: —
He argued that compulsory arbitration was in no way
inconsistent with Freetrade, as some members had in*
sisted, and contended that the Arbitration Court in New
South Wales had been a distinct success.
Certainly the N.S.W. Arbitration Court—which
didn’t start until N.S.W. got Protection—has
been a success. And, with the usual astounding
cheek of the shoddyites, old preacher Joe claims
that as a proof that a legal minimum wage and un-
restricted competition with countries which have
no minimum wage law can exist together.
A picture that is likely to attract the notice
of all those who take an interest in the public affairs of
the Commonwealth (says the Brisbane Courier, of August
21st) wa3 hung in the State Parliament House yesterday,
by permission of the President of the Council and the
Speaker of the Assembly. It is a copy of a painting by
Mr. Charles Nuttall of the opening of the first Federal
Parliament by H.R.H. the Duke of York, now Prince of
Wales. The artist has succeeded in reproducing the
scene with singular effectiveness. Politicians and others
well-known to Queenslanders are easily identified, which
circumstance is a tribute to the actual merit of the work
as a whole. Mr. A. S. Gordon, manager of the Historical
Picture Association of Australia, who controls the distri-
bution of the reproduction of Mr. Nuttall’s painting,
arrived here early this week with the view of establishing
an agency in this State, and has secured the service of
Mr. W. J. Weatherill (American Consul). (Mr. James
Best, York Buildings, has been appointed agent for
N.S.W. ; and Messrs. Eustis and Yonds, of Pirie Street,
Adelaide, are the S.A. representatives.) See full particu-
lars in next week’s Bulletin.*
The pitiable tendency to spend its whole
time in pottering over small things that has come
upon N.S.W. Assembly is strikingly shown by a
perusal of the notice paper. Here are some of
the questions on the paper for 27th August:
Was Mr. Raw of the Architect’s Department granted
three months leave of absence on full pay ?
Is a man named Andrew Wales in the employ of the
National Park Trust? What are his duties and what
salary does he receive?
How many fire brigades are there in the State?
Has the attention of the Government been drawn to
the fact that several deaths have occurred through the
escape of carbonic acid gas from machines used for
heating bath water?
Is it the intention to finish the present session of
Parliament in October next because of the hot weather ?
What is the cost per ton of blue metal spawls from
Minchinbury Quarry delivered at Rookwood ?
Is Mr. Justice Foster alive?
One reason why N.S.W. has no decent Local
Government law and no graduated land tax is
that the Assembly has been busy for 20 years
asking the Ministry what are Smith’s wages,
is Jones alive, and how much it costs to bring
half-bricks and broken crockery from Monaro and
deliver them at Broken Hill.
The original drawings of Phil May, to be
sold on 18th September, include the famous series
of “ Things We See When We Haven’t Got a
Gun,” besides upwards of 100 other likenesses and
caricature portraits.
The recreator who is tempted by the balmy
spring climate to go a-roaming, should be prepared for
humorous contingencies, picturesque spots, or frolicsome
experiences (such as frenzied bulls or snakes on active
service) by arming himself with a Kodak, wherewith to
take sudden “ shots.” He who travels without a Kodak
misses half the fun of life. It is a safe and entertaining
companion, which costs little, and yields a splendid profit
upon the outlay in its photographic results. A Kodak
catalogue can be obtained from 284 Oollins-st., Melbourne.
Write for one.*
From “ F.C.” :
The funniest thing about Premier Irvine’s trip to the
backblocks of Victoria to propound his policy was in con-
nection with the run there and back. The strong, calm
man must needs have a pilot-engine sent ahead of bis
train to see that nobody dynamited him. Bent was with
him, and Bent’s person was precious—also Bent knew his
railways and engines weren’t to be trusted. The blessed
pilot ran hot on the way up and put everything an
hour late, and on the way back, when the Ministers
reached Murtoa, the station was in darkness, the signals
were against them, and the pilot was lost. After
much telephoning it was reckoned to be somewhere
between Murtoa and Lubeck, so the Ministerial engine
was uncoupled and sent slowly ahead till it bumped into
the broken-winded, collapsed pilot far away in the
darkness. It took two hours to get it back to Murtoa,
where it was run on to a siding ; then the distinguished
politicians meandered on gradually. And it was all
wasted energy. Nobody made the least effort to dyna-
mite Irvine, or to throw a dictionary at Bent.

There was a dreadful squeal from the
Gippsland coal companies when it was announced
the other day that the Victorian Government
intended to withdraw the subsidy of |d.
per ton per mile on the carriage of their coal.
This subsidy amounts to about £16,000 a year,
and it has enabled the companies to pay dividends
to their shareholders, most of whom are mem-
bers of the reform leagues that whooped so
loudly for “retrenchment over the whole
domain of State expenditure.” Both Mel-
bourne dailies got violently to work on the
matter —one newspaper magnate, by the , way,
is deeply interested financially in the Gippsland
coal companies. So after the Gazette notice was
published a hurried Cabinet meeting was held,
and the strong Retrenchment and Economy
Government will allow the coal companies to loot
the Treasury for another year.
When W. B. Dailey was Acting-Premier
of N.S.W. he wrote a minute to Judge Docker
censuring his antics on the Bench. Parkes,; when
Premier, indited a similar epistle, and almost
every session of Parliament since complaints
of that Judge’s eccentricities have occupied
attention. Another night will soon be de-
voted to the same old theme. Among those
who have denounced Docker’s Bench manners
and insulting loquacity Hansard shows the
names of J. P. Abbott, G. R. Dibbs, W. P.
Crick, John M'Elhone, and men of all kinds of
taste. Dailey’s was the most emphatic of the
reprimands, and amounted to a threat of dis-
missal. That was 18 years or so ago, and the
same old threat; has been hanging round ever
since.

“ Alba ” : Came upon what I thought from
the noise to be a carpet-beating factory t’other
day. Looked through the window casually and
saw a large, red-faced man with his coat off
pounding a woman from corner to corner. Noticed
that he had on a pair of 6oz. boxing-gloves. The
woman took the stoush in the usual feminine way
—holding his knees, clutching his hair and cursing
him for a coward. The red-faced man punched
her in a smart, scientific way, and finished each
round by helping himself to some beer out of a
jug. Day or two later I met him on a tram, and
in a friendly way mentioned what I had seen
through the window. “Oh,” he said, “that was
my wife Emma. I didn’t know the window was
open. Looked funny, eh? No; I’m not a pug.
We’ve been married seven years, and up till now
she’s fancied that I was a species of human worm
—something that came home at night and wriggled
till it got fed.” The fat, red man breathed
heavily as we alighted from the tram together.
“I haven’t a temper,” he said plaintively, “and
when I hit Emma I feel that it’s all for the best.
She’s a slommoky woman and eats lollies all day,
and consumes biscuits in bed. When I reproach
her, she ” —the red fat man dropped his voice to a
whisper—“she threatens to run away with a
vegetable Chow. She’s been holding that Chow
over me for the last four years. When I refuse
money for matinees and picnics she throws the
Chow at me. I don’t know who put her up to it,
but I know that this suburb is full of women who
blackmail their husbands under that threat. Good
night! ” He stayed a moment at the corner to
light his pipe. “They call me a woman wal-
loper about here, but Emma’s been quite a lady
since I started to put ’em on.” I noticed that
most of the Chows in that suburb wore black eyes
about that time.

“Bill”: Serving a sentence once, the
warder who acted as drill-instructor was directing
us in the salute when marching in file. Wishing
to stand directly in front to watch the movement
of the arms, he placed his cap on the gate to a,ct
as an imaginary governor or other tinpot official
entitled to a salute. Then we were given the
word “ Forward ! ” and each swung his right arm
to the forehead in turn as he passed the greasy
cap. A few days afterwards I asked him casually
if he had ever heard of Gessler, the tyrant killed
by Tell, and he said “No.” I believed him—he
was so hopelessly ignorant; so I put the
cap episode down as a remarkable coincidence.
He was instructing a squad of prisoners before
some visitors and explaining the movements.
“ Bring the ’and to the ’ed with a cirklar motion.
There is three movements in a right habout turn ;
first draw back the right foot till the ollow
presses against the left ’eel .” ihen one of
the visitors chuckled and so did I. He locked me
up two days afterwards on a frivolous charge, and
1 did five days’ cells without work or books waiting
for the visiting “beak,’’ who let me off with a
caution. The drill instructor was satisfied, though.

“ A. 8.” writes:
The motion for adjournment nuisance has reached an
acute stage in N.S.W. Assembly. The Government busi-
ness has been delayed to such a degree that there is little
prospect of Parliament passing any useful legislation
before Christmas. The only retaliatory measure adopted
has been, so far, to sit late on Thursday nights. Premier
See might consider the advisability of flouting all con-
stitutional precedents, and calmly agreeing to adjourn
whenever one of the talkers moves the adjournment—-just
accept the motion and adjourn, leaving the aggrieved
member standing. This would throw the onus of delay
on the persons who move adjournments.
This is an interesting suggestion, but the average
obstructionist politician can stand a great deal of
onus. Onus doesn’t trouble him unless it is
deducted from his salary.

THE UNION BANK OF AUSTRALIA LTP.
HEAD OFFICE: 7* CORNHILL, LONDON, E.C.
REPORT OF PROCEEDINGS AT THE 65th ANNUAL GENERAL MEETING OF THE PROPRIETORS HELD IN
LONDON ON 20th JULY, 1903. MR. ARTHUR FLOWER IN THE CHAIR ’
Mr. A 0. Willis, the manager, having read the advertisement calling the meeting, the following Report of the
Directors and the Statement of Accounts were taken as read
~ “ London, 11th Julv, 1903.
The directors have to submit to the shareholders the annexed balance-sheet of the bank to 28th February
1903, duly audited, which, after provision for doubtful debts, exhibits a net amount of profits of £133 793 3s 6d
inclusive of balance brought forward from the previous half-year. From this the directors have applied a sum of
£50,000 to release a similar sum, being the amount which stood in the balance-sheet as a contingent reserve against
doubtful debts, thus extinguishing this contingent reserve. The £50,000 thus released is restored to the bank’s
reserve fund, which is thereby increased from £950,000 to £1,000,000. They have also appropriated a sum of £4OOO
as a contribution in aid of the pension fund of the bank’s staff, known as the Guarantee and Provident Funds. From
the balance remaining (£79,793 3s. 6d.) the directors have resolved upon the declaration of a dividend at the rate of
8 per cent, per annum, equal to £1 per ehare, which will absorb £60,000, and leave £19,793 3s. 6d. to be carried for-
ward. The directors have much pleasure in reporting further rainfall during March and April and since, which,
coupled with the rainfall of December last, has practically broken up the drought in Queensland and in the’greater
part of New South Wales, but in some parts of the latter State the want of rain is still badly felt. Since the last
meeting branches of the bank have been opened at Casrerton and Hamilton in Victoria, at Narrogin and Northam
in Western Australia, and at Martinborough in New Zealand ; and the branch at Norseman (W.A.) has been closed.”

London, 9th July, 1903.
Auditors’ Certificate and Report.
In accordance with the provisions of " The Companies Act, 1900,” we certify that all our requirements as audi
tors have been complied with.
We report that we have verified the cash, investments, bills of exchange, and other assets in London, and have
had access to the accounts sent to London from the various branches in the colonies, and, having examined the
same, and the books and accounts of the London office, are of opinion that the above balance-sheet is a full
and fair balance-sheet, properly drawn up so as to exhibit a true and correct view of the state of the company’s
affairs, as shown by the books of the company.
WHINNEY, SMITH, and WHINNEY, Chartered Accountants, Auditors.

Bolivia, a rather disreputable South
American State, recently made oyer a whole pro-
vince to an English syndicate which was going to
run things on Chartered Co. lines. And then
affairs happened. The adjacent republics of Peru
and Brazil declared that no nigger-driving Char-
tered Co. methods would be allowed to take root
on that continent, and that no State should give
any encroaching European community a lien on
the country. In short, an invading Brazilian
army cast the syndicate out by the hair, and now
Bolivia has to pacify it with many dollars.
“ Claude ” writes in a controversy which
is hereby dropped
“ j.E.W.’s” (B. July 16) knowledge of W.A. Labor
matters wouldn’t crowd a postage stamp. The A.M.A.,
the younger of the two miners’ unions, does not, as a
union, take any part in politics, but 100 per cent, of its
members on the Golden Mile are members of the Political
Labor Party. There are certainly blatant individuals in
the Labor movement, and perhaps they are not unknown
elsewhere. As for “J.E.W.’s” speculations on forth-
coming Federal elections, there is a saw somewhere about
knowledge, and prophecy, which just fits in here. The
W.A. L-tb.br politicians are not a brilliant lot, yet they
compare favorably with other mediocrities. Since Bigjohn
packed his carpet bag there has not been a strong man in
Groperland. As for C. J. Moran he has grown too strong
for two constituencies, and his rankness is increasing.
“P.L.”: The Queensland Black Labor
crowd lias not yet ceased bemoaning the lot of the
hapless Kanaka who is returned to his island to
be killed and eaten. Meantime here are the latest
figures re Polynesian mortality in Queensland.
For last year there died 327 Kanakas out of a sum
total of 8378—an average of 37 per thousand. The
average death-rate among adult white males whose
ages ranged from 20.t0 40 years was per 1000.
Any sensible Kanaka would do well to chance the
alleged cannibalistic club of his compatriots rather
than take his risks on a Queensland plantation
with a mortality like the above.
Melbourne Argus has executed a complete
change of front over the Preferential Tariff busi-
ness. On May 16th it set forth the awful results
which would accrue to Australia if it followed the
Canadian lead. The proposal for preference was
ascribed to the machinations of the Federal
Government. On June 16th the Argus published
a long article to show (with figures and tables ad
lib.) that Australia had nothing to gain by any
scheme of preference with England. But the
Argus subsequently discovered that the plan did
not emanate from the despised Barton Ministry
but from Chamberlain. Then the old lady wiped
her hundred spectacles, muttering, “ I must have
been dreaming,” and spoke on July 28th on the
necessity of falling in with any scheme which
would tend to consolidate the Empire, right or
wrong An extract:
Whatever policy Great Britain adopts, she should have
the loyal, unflinching support of her children.
The returned farm-burner and purlomer
of dead Boers’ whiskers still attracts attention m
Maoriland. Hardly a day passes but m one or
another of the State’s police-courts he is sent to
gaol for thieving, insulting women, or some other
branch of his original business. A couple ot
years ago all eyes gazed upon him as the saviour ot
his country, but now only the inquiring gaze of
the detective watches him with a view to lumber-
ing him under the Vagrancy Act.
The British Cobden Club is not a fighting
animal nowadays. It meets to hold back-scratch-
ing functions at which the Freetrade members
tell each other they are the salt of the earth ,
but when it was recently challenged to discuss
the country’s fiscal policy with an outside opponent
of some size it decided that brawling and argu-
ment were unseemly things and not to be en-
couraged.
The Commonwealth is already finding out
the disadvantages of “ taking up the white man s
burden ” by acquiring a portion of Papua. Its
proposal to give the colored-labor products of that
possession a preference of 33J per cent, on the
usual tariff rates is likely to be bitterly opposed.
The concession is of far too sweeping a character.
It would allow employers to set up manufactures
of some types in Papua and, by employing black
labor, flood the Australian market with goods
which would receive preferential tariff treatment.
Recently N.S.W. received the glad news
that the State Labor party had decided to set its
face against all borrowing except for reproductive
works. It wasn’t quite such glad news as it might
have been, for some people have extraordinary
idea as to what constitutes “reproductive works,’
but it sounded as if there was at least going to be
some change for the better. Apparently there
isn’t going to be any change, and the alleged new
departure is just plain fraud and delusion. The
other day Tieasurer Waddell put through about
the most utterly villainous and unprincipled set of
loan proposals N.S.W. has yet seen—a set in which
the items were almost wholly unproductive and a
swindle on posterity—and the Labor party went
its draggled way unquestioningly at the tail of
Owe’Sullivan and co., as usual.
