There is much joy in the Mount David (N.S.W.)
camp. The first di vidend has been declared. It is small
—3d.—compared with the high-flavor promises of seven
or eight years ago when shares were boomed to over £1.
Reports indicate a probability of an average yield of
6dwt. per ton being maintained for a couple of years, so
that an occasional 3d. may be looked for. Working costs
have been reduced to 15s. 8d. per ton, which is satis-
factory, all things considered. Manager “ Jack” Pearce
is to be congratulated on the suocess which has attended
his efforts to pull the mine out of the ditch, and if he
doesn’t get a substantial addition to his monthly cheque
there is no gratitude in the co.
* * * *
W.A. mines during August paid £225,360 in
divs. Co.’s responsible were : Golden Horseshoe £90,000,
Perseverance £87,000, Princess Royal £4000, W.F. Main
Reefs £4000, Cosmopolitan £40,000, and Lancefield £360.
The total for the first eight months of this year is
£1,347,030, against £625,866 last year. For September,
Great Boulder Prop, has declared 9d., or £65,625, and
Oroya-Brown Hill 2s., or £45,000.
* * * *
Queensland Menzies (W.A.) is getting on fairly
well with sinking the main shaft, which is down 480ft.,
and is being sunk 20ft a month. The next level at 626ft.
should be started in three months time. Each 80ft.
vertical gives 150ft. of backs, and about 10,000 tons of
reserves. The co. is making £2400 a month, enough to
pay quarterly divs. of Is. The original 10-head mill is to
be scrapped and 10-head of 12501b. stamps substituted.
This should materially increase next year’s output and
profits.
* * » *
Cumberland, Norseman (W. A.), after its recent
flutter, is unlikely to provide much business till the mill
talks. Various experts have recently visited the pro-
perty, and their opinions widely diverge. Capt. Pleitner,
who knocked Arltunga endways, knocked the bottom out
of the market by damning the property with faint praise.
Directors took advantage of the boomlet to re-construct,
and announce a call of Is. This will provide £4000 if
fully responded to.
* * ■ * *
Black Range goldfield (W.A.), about which a
good deal has been heard lately, looks like trumps. A
lot of alluvial gold has been won in the vicinity, but
prospectors have now started working the reefs and
leaders. A parcel of 7 tons from the Bilbic, treated at
Lennonville, has vielded 165oz. of gold.
* * • *
During July, the W.A. gold mines turned out
the record yield of 212,501oz. The chief contributors
were : —Perseverance, 17,940oz. ; Horseshoe, 17,590oz. ;
Great Boulder, 14,883oz.; Great Fingall Consols, ll,015oz.;
Ivanhoe, ll,066oz. ; Oroya-Brown Hill, 8042oz. ; Asso-
ciated, 6232oz. ; Sons of Gwalia, 5411oz. ; Westralia Mt.
Morgans, 5213oz.; Cosmopolitan, 5448oz. ; Associated
Northern, 3723oz. ; Lake View Consols, 4635oz. ; Kalgurli,
3371oz. ; Peak Hill, 2718oz. ; Princess Royal, 2657oz. ;
Westralia and East Extended, 1639oz. ; Golden Link,
1919oz. ; Long Reef, 2061oz. ; East Murchison United,
1094oz. ; Bellevue, 1555oz. ; Queensland Menzies, 1248oz. ;
Boulder Main Reef, 1233oz. ; White Feather Main Reefs,
1044OZ.; Ida H., 1181oz. ; Hainault, 801oz. ; Lancefield,
759oz. ; Golden Age Consols, 688oz. ; Hannans Star,
251oz. ; Menzies Consols, 962oz. ; Menzies Alpha, 753oz. ;
Fraser’s South Extended, 651oz. ; Golden Ridge, 620oz. ;
and Champion Prop., 682oz.
» * # •
Island Eureka, Lake Austin (W.A.), appears to
be.in a pretty bad way,- judging from chairman White’s
dolorous ditty at recent general meeting. Shareholders
were told that “it would have been a wiser policy to
have paid fewer dividends, and, if dividends had been
continued, shareholders would soon have been obliged to
pay calls. Erection of cyanide plant had been too long
delayed. He would like to see the mine equipped with
rock drills and heavier stamps. Dividends would be
resumed as soon as justified.” The rock drills, heavier
stamps, and cyanide plant were promised in June, 1902,
and shareholders were assured that they could be pro-
vided without encroaching on dividends. Three years’
reserves of ore were also said to be in sight; yet for the
past six months no returns have been published, and
dividends have been suspended. When Tub Bulletin
predicted a catastrophe some months ago directors and
others were, or pretended to be, righteously indignant.
Somebody should be fired out.
* * * *
Queen Margaret (W.A.) appears to have secured
a real good thing in its new property, the South Gipps-
land, at Vosperton. Four levels are'being driven, and
all the ore broken out is being sent direct to the battery,
and is yielding an average of 35dwt., with 6dwt. left in
the tailings. At the 140ft. level the original prospectors
drove 50ft. and stopped on the reef pinching. Co. has
driven another 50ft., and the lode in the face is 3ft. wide
and worth loz. per ton. At 250ft. the lode has been
opened up for 100ft., and ranges from 1ft. to 5ft. wide.
At 285ft. about 250ft. has been driven, the reef averaging
about 20in. wide. The 370ft. level has been extended
150ft., and a rise put up to the level above. At this level
the reef averages about 2ft. A contract for a cyanide
plant has been let, and, when the job is completed, the
battery will run full time and treat from 5000 to 6000 tons
monthly. The accumulated tailings amount to 8000 tons,
on which there should be a profit of nearly £1 a ton.
Prospects look good, though the mine will never rank
among the big propositions.
Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.) has completed the
addition to its treatment plant, and will in future handle
about 5300 tons for 10,000oz. of gold monthly. Some very
rich ore has been met in rising from No. 10 level, and also
in sinking a winze below it. The ore in both places runs
from 4 to 5oz. per ton. The winze is being sunk on the
incline, following the dip of the chute. This was the
practice pursued on the Associated Northern; and it
enabled the management to say that no sloping was
done, while the winze really gutted out all the rich ore.
The co. should make a very large profit in future. If the
published mining costs of 28s. lid. a ton could be relied
upon, there should be a margin, subject to deduction of
London expenses, of nearly £30,000 a month, or 80 per
cent, per annum on the issued capital.
* * * *
Paringa, Kalgoorlie (W.A.j, is putting down
two bores eastwards from the 800 and 1300ft. levels to
try and locate the Brown Hill lode. The consulting
engineer assured the directors that the lode would enter
the co’s. ground at 1000ft, if the average dip from surface
to No. 5 level in Brown Hill continued. The very nearest
point of the Brown Hill outcrop to the Paringa boundary
is 600ft., so that the underlie would require to be very
flat indeed to bring it into Paringa ground at such a
shallow depth. In any case, the gold chute dips away
south into the Oroya, so that the Paringa can never get it
even if it does eventually get the lode. The co. has done
a lot of legitimate mining since Thomson took charge,
and it is a pity that it has so far proved fruitless.
Millionaire, Mt. Morgans (W.A.), one of A. E.
Morgans’ flotations, doesn’t seem to justify either ex-
pectations or its great name. In the annual report the
ore reserves above the 100ft level were estimated at 14,810
tons assaying 26|dwt., but the published yields barely
average 7dwt., leaving an ounce unaccounted for. Pro-
perty has always been regarded as a low grade proposi-
tion, so that the 26dwt. estimate is pretty certain to be
all wrong. After three years’ work the main shaft is
• only 150ft. deep, and only one level has been driven.
Shareholders were told at the meeting on July 21 that
the approximate profit, after paying all expenses, on the
14,810 tons of ore opened up would be £55,000. As the
results from the mill since it started in May have shown
the ore to be worth barely 25s. a ton, somebody must
surely have made a blunder.
At Poverty Point.
Julius Knight off the stage begins to look older than he
was some years previously, but he battles hard against
ravaging- Time. With his nose adroitly powdered and the
same old kiss-curls arranged upon his brow, Julius is pre-
sumably as effective for stage purposes as when he used
to chase. the Early Christian maiden around the sinful
Pagan sofa.
“ For fifteen long years I was a lighthouse-keeper off
the rugged coast of Almond Rock; a successful light-
house-keeper, till one accursed day a thunderbolt fell and
split the mainbraces —and I had to hold my trousers up
with a life-belt! ” — Slab of rugged eloquence from James
Harvey, burlesquing Wilson Barrett at the Adelaide
Tivoli.
Said, on the most unreliable authority, that Musgrove
chose “ Twelfth Night ” to follow “ Midsummer Night’s
Dream” because of the “local color" that prophetic
Shakspeare put into it. There’s a Sir Toby in it, for
instance. This Sir Toby is in the position of protector
of a fair lady, his benefactress ; but, too fond of late hours
and good company, he weakly assists a bounder to get
possession of her. “This quaffing and drinking will undo
you,” one Maria tells him finally. And another, Malvolio
—Australia knows Malvolio, anyhow—talks to him like a
father : “ Sir Toby, I must be round with you. My lady
bids me tell you that, though she harbors you as her
kinsman, she’s nothing allied to your disorders. If you
can separate yourself and your misdemeanors, you are
welcome to the house ; if not, an it would please you to
take leave of her, she is very willing to bid you farewell.”
Dan Barry told a ’Tiser interviewer in Adelaide that
his first strike was made in connection with the death of
Napoleon 111. He bought a big bundle of the twopenny
“ extras ” issued by the Melbourne papers, took a cab to
St. Kilda, and sold them easily at half-a-crown a copy.
“ We made so much money that we didn’t know' whether
to start a circus or endow a church.” But he went into
the theatrical business on the profits of his sixpenny
shocker, “The Craft, Crime, and Crowds of a Great
City.” “ Was it a true record?” “No ; that’s probably
w’hy it caught on with the masses. But that was a detail.
I sold thousands of copies, and made enough money to
start a small Company. That was in 1883, and I have
been in the theatrical line ever since.”
Sydney is much the same as Melbourne —a great deal of
what its public takes for theatrical criticism is merely so
much puff-paragraphing. Just before a stock co. put on
a piece, a w'eek or two back, the cast was radically altered
—after the programmes had gone out. But the bill was
all the daily-paper “critic” had to go by; and so on
Monday morning Jorkins (who wasn’t “on” at all) read
that he had done very effective work, as usual, and
Smithson was told that he was well-placed as something or
other that he didn’t play. Of course nothing unfriendly,
or even frank, is said in such notices ; it’s grease all the
time.
“J.A.C.”: Apropos the conjuror at Adelaide Tivoli
yarn (B. 27/8/’O3). I remember, at the time of the Mel-
bourne land boom and bank crashes, when everybody
was more or less on his or her “ uppers,” Will Whitburn
giving a burlesque conjuring sketch at the old Victoria
Hall. “ Will anybody kindly lend me a gold watch for
my next trick?” he asked. No reply. “Ah, yes; of
course—l understand. Just hand me up the ticket;
that’ll do as well.”
“ Melb.” : “The Resurrection” will be followed, at
Her Majesty’s, by Hall Caine’s “Eternal City.” Here
the Pope is introduced, the action of the drama being set
in a future time, and curiously enough, Hall Caine
imagined a Pope with the title of Pius X. The name will
doubtless be changed, more especially as Caine’s Pope is
a downright rascal. The scenery of the drama intro-
duces just those parts of Rome which the public is most
curious to know about, at the present juncture. I do
not remember a Pope on the stage in any former play,
but probably there has been such, on the Continental
stage if not the English. Of course cardinals (or at
least one, Cardinal Richelieu) are familiar enough on the
boards.

One of the most scientific electricians practising
in Australia is Mr. W. G-. ENGLISH, M.E.,
CERTIFICATED ELECTRICIAN, formerly
mechanical electrician to the Government Rail-
ways, Eveleigh, and the Government Electrical
Tramway Department. Mr. English’s knowledge
is not confined to the practical side of the Science
as applied to national industries. He has studied
deeply the Science of Electro-Therapeutics or the
application of Electricity to the human body for
the cure of such diseases as St. Vitus’ Dance,
Epilepsy, Rheumatism, or any class of Nerve
Diseases afflicting the human species. He is the
holder of a high class certificate granted at the
Sydney Technical College for competency in these
studies, which he received with honors.
Mr. English was recently interviewed by our
special reporter at his extensive laboratory,
Sydney. Replying to questions relative to Elec-
tric Belts generally he said
I think I have at my private residence over
30 different kinds of Electric Belts sold in different
parts of the world. I have specially studied this
science, and when any new Electric Belt or an
improvement is announced I receive it by first
mail from my European agents.”
Have you found any of them superior to the
Freeman and Wallace ‘ Invigorator * Belt, which
they sell for One Pound Only, and which you are
under contract to manufacture for them ? ”
No ! I have not. I have here a Belt adver-
tised largely and for which as muoh as ten
and fifty pounds is said to be paid, but it is
not equal to the Belt I manufacture here. I use
the finest material combined with the latest
improvements. The chief feature of my Belt is
the vulcanite insulation, for which the Institute’s
Specialists hold Australian patent rights. From
a medical point it is most important, for without
this no Belt is worth twopence medicinally, as the
current leaks away instead of being concentrated
on the human body affected.”
And this is most important in cases of serious
nerve diseases, I suppose ? ”
Very important. With the vulcanite insula-
tion the current is directed upon the electric discs
attached to the Belt-sheath and directly associated
with the body. Its benefit is thus directly felt,
no escapement is possible, and the invigoration of
the affected parts is rendered immediately com-
plete. It is a most scientific and effective
method.”

Cate was a big bull-faced man who should
have been a pork-butcher but wasn’t. He was a
parson. Cate was leaving his work at the church
one afternoon to attend the sale of a certain
brindled cow he had set his heart on, when Drout,
a farmer, happened along, with his wife and six
large children, and demanded a wholesale
christening. Somehow, in their babyhood, every
one of the half dozen had omitted to call and be
christened. Cate, with only a few minutes to get
to the cow-sale, said a verse of a church hymn
under his breath, ranged the little Drouts round
the font, got the father to write down the proposed
names, and commenced to christen for all he was
worth. But Papa Drout, in writing the names
down, hadn’t taken into consideration that the
parson would want the youngsters in order, and
before he could intervene. Cate, in his haste, had
named Rupert Archibald as Hedley Beaumont,
and Sophia Fredericka had been christened
William John, whilst poor Bill was named Jim
Charlotte. Finishing the ceremony to his own
satisfaction Cate hurried to the sale (arriving in
time to see the cow knocked down to someone
else) and left Mr. and Mrs. Drout bordering on a
state of insanity, and quite unable to say whether
their children were male or female. Moral: Get
christened John, which is a handy name over which
a parson can hardly blunder; and don’t get
christened by a parson who wants to buy a cow.

The Inky Way.
** The Viotorian Cyclopaedia,” a new publio&tion* gives
the dates of the establishment of the Melbourne news-
papers. Fawkner’s MSS. Advertiser appeared in 1838.
Another proprietary issued the first printed paper, the
Port Phillip Gazette, later, the same year. [But
Bonwick’s “Port Phillip,” published in 1856, says that
Fawkner was printing his paper for several months
before the Gazette appeared.] Fawkner’s printed paper,
the Port Phillip Patriot, appeared in 1839. The Herald
first came out in 1840. The Patriot changed to the
Daily News in 1845. The Argus first appeared in
1846, and the Age in 1854, the first number being printed
in the old Melbourne Exhibition, where the Mint is now.
The Argus and the Herald were the only daily papers in
1852. The Age was started by Cooke Brothers. Ebenezer
Syme, David Blair and T. L. Bright (not Charles) left the
Argus staff for the Age. The Cookes withdrew, and the
paper was carried on by a co-operative company, headed
by Blair, T. L. Bright and Shaw, the printer. Ebenezer
and David Syme bought the Age in 1856. The first Argus
proprietary had fallen into difficulties, and Edward
Wilson bought the paper, being then a small squatter, at
Dandenonsr. James Smith began on the Age. T. L.
Bright started “ My Note Book.”
* • • •
Some day, mayhap, the microbe of devilry will enter
some enterprising ink-slinger’s mind, and he will set about
to collect some, at least, of the weird legends that linger
round most newspaper offices—country ones, especially,
that have had their vicissitudes since the “roaring”
days. A scrap here about the remote editor—a man of
many parts. As effervescent to-day as a squib lighted at
both ends; to-morrow moralising that all the ways of life,
and ink, and drink, are Vanity—with a big V—and ending
his career either as Premier or other political potentate
of his State, or, what is just as probable, in maudlin
obscurity. Another scrap there about strange public
wires pulled, and strange public dodges dodged. A page
further on concerning the organ’s feuds against reptile
contemporaries, black, heavy, and unscrupulous as itself ;
as loopy in their ways and means to an end ; as shaky in
the syntax of their thunders. Then there are the papers’
acrobatic flip-flaps on matters of public policy ; its
unaccountable scuttlings of Ministerial ships ; its friend-
ships and its hatreds ; the axes it ground, the libel suits
it fought; its reasons for digging up the remains of this
buried scandal, and the reasons why it sat down hard on
the tomb of that one ; the men who were connected with
it, and their beginnings, doings, writings, drinkings, and
endings ; its bills, duns, ghosts, bailiffs, its collapses and
its resurrections. Every “rag” in the Commonwealth
has its history, its legends, and memories. Put to paper,
judiciously condensed, verified, and burnished, the
“Story of the Australian Press” still awaits the able
pen-driver for its consummation.

“Steff Cassander”: Was in Sydney recently. Called
early on bookstall and asked for morning paper.
Wretched man gave me a dismal stare, and pulled down
from shelf a big assortment of mourning paper—most
deadly-looking kind. I explained. He snorted and
hbtly replied, “ Why didn’t yer say ’Erald ? ” I repeated
“’Erald?” “ Yass.” Thought I ought to drop into The
Bulletin Office just to see how you propel. Young man
intently licking stamps behind counter received me as if
I was a sewing machine ambassador. Explained myself,
and he referred me to the editor’s secretary, a youth evi-
dently in the last stages of choleric agony, or composi-
tion, or something. Introduced me to the sub., as
the editor was away ill. The sub. didn’t seem
too well ; in fact, you all seemed ill. The
sub. was very nice, and talked to me about Maori-
land and referred to the M.L. “Handbook” (dated
1882). There was another gentleman in the room, who
looked as if he was dying. Whilst talking, a waxen-faced
man with stiff whiskers burst in and delivered some
excited news to the man who was dying, but no one took
any .notice of him. All this time the buzz of machinery
jigging was horrible—l think that buzz helps to shorten
your lives. I asked the sub. how he got on. He pointed
sadly to a pile of MSS. on his desk. Occasionally a leaf
seemed to drop slowly (of its own accord) into the waste-
paper crate. I understood, and felt relieved that he had
nothing of mine just at that time. [lt is always cheering
to know how you look to an outsider, but the remark
about the Maoriland “Handbook” dated 1882 is quite
uncalled for. Volume in question is dated 1902.—Ed. B.]
What an awful slump there has been in the Inky Way of
Victorian country newspaperdom in the onetime fat pick-
ings to be had off the bones of Municipal Printing Oon-
tracts. On every hand the prices are a mere shadow of
the stout corpses on which the early newspaper vulture*
fattened, and which seemed to be their sole source of
income. Instances are quoted where shire printing (con-
siderably less of it in the old days and much worse done
then than now) used to cost £2BO per annum ; but, with
the advent of a rival organ, it has come down to £24,
and the successful tenderer expresses satisfaction at the
price. A few years back a Victorian pressman, hearing
no other tenders were in, raised his last year’s estimate
from £72 to £lO5 and got the contract. Before the next
tender-time came round the paper had changed hands—
the aforesaid soft-snap being held out as a bait for the
new purchaser. When next year’s tenders were called for
a rumor got abroad that the old proprietor, who had
another newspaper up the line, intended competing for
the shire work. At the council meeting it was notified
that the local organ had secured the printing contract.
Then the pale-faced representative of the old proprietor
rose and asked that prices be made public, when it was
found that the successful tenderer’s price was £25 ; the
old original’s £4s—or thereabouts 1 If the cost of
municipal printing falls any lower half the country sup-
press will close up from anaemia I
• • * *
One outcome of the row between London Times and
dramatist Henry Arthur Jones over the exclusion of the
Times’ dramatic critic (Walkley) from the opening per-
formance of Jones’ “ Whitewashing of Julia,” on the
ground of Walkley’s alleged unfairness to Jones, is that
the Times has reverted to its old practice of rigidly pay-
ing for the seats it uses for purposes of criticism. Having
come to so dignified a decision, the Times had an oppor-
tunity to allow the whole undignified squabble to drop ;
but instead of going and buying its seats as an ordinary
theatre-goer, it sent to the collective theatre-manager a
pompous note announcing its decision and saying, “We
shall be obliged if you will forward two stall tickets for
each first night’s performance, and we will at once for-
ward payment.” Then a portion of the collective manager
also became pompously polite and wouldn’t hear of the
time-honored custom of the critic’s free seat being upset.
The result is that London was treated to the humor-
ous foolery of England’s inky institution coming out
without notices of certain theatres, because the inky in-
stitution’scritic wouldn’t occupy the free seat, and wouldn’t
simply buy his ticket in a quiet, anonymous way like a
mere human being, and the manager wouldn’t be a party
to any scheme of payment by forwarding tickets. When
the Times becomes heavily dignified it seems to fear that
the fact won’t be noticed quite so much as it ought to,
and it has to draw attention at the top of its voice to its
attitude of quiet and manly reserve.

Lately, a Queensland bank-owned paper advertised in
the Brisbane dailies for a manager-editor at £3 per week.
Imagine such extravagance ! And he would have nothing
whatever to do, while sweltering in a tropical, Chinese-
infested Northern township, except write leading articles
in the bank’s interests, do paragraphs, use the scissors and
Easte pot, secure ads., report, read proofs, keep the
ooks, make out accounts, keep the subscribers sweet,
etc., etc. Something must have happened to make that
bank so liberal.

“ Wurrym ” : The man mentioned in Bulletin (23/7/’O3)
Who reported the current price of “ seed pollard,” put in
a period of pen-and-inky servitude at Perth—at least, he
was reputed to be the same individual—and there became
responsible for the canard concerning a fight between
Piesse and George (now Commissioner of Railways) in the
Assembly. The guileless one heard that the roundsman
on the West Australian had full particulars, whereupon
he gathered up some sort of a yarn which the Herald
sub. had doubts about. Colebatch (formerly of Barrier
Miner), who was doing country wires, cross-examined his
junior, but the latter was confident. So Colebatch wired
the yarn away, and the goldfields papers published
it under “ Late Sporting.” That’s what made old Jarrah
Jack and his crowd squirm. The prosecution of the
Hockings of Kalgoorlie Miner failed ; so to get home on
someone a ukase was issued that Colebatch was to be
debarred the reporters’ gallery. He hadn't been In
the gallery for years, but that was enough to get
him there, and he promptly adorned a front seat. Pre-
sently a messenger came and desired him to withdraw.
You’ll have to put me out,” said Oolebatch as trucu-
lently as he knew how. As soon as the usher’s hand was
on his shoulder up he rose and “ wint quoitely, yer wor-
ship.” Then he issued a writ against the Speaker for
assault, the objectionable order was withdrawn, and
peace reigned again. But the reporter above referred to,
besides this and his “ seed pollard ” experiences, had one
concerning " stud wethers ” before he abandoned jour-
nalism and went back to his old home at the case.
* * * *
Veteran Melbourne pressman, Jas. Smith, was editor of
a newspaper in his native county of Devon, England,
at the age of 21. On arriving in Melbourne in ’53 he
thought of going into business as an auctioneer, but took
up the pen again instead. There were few practised
writers about in those days, and he soon established
himself. He wrote at) ome period or other for seven or
eight different Melbourne papers. In the prosperous
days of the country press he used to contribute articles
to provincial as well as metropolitan journals, and for a
long series of years turned out an average of 20 columns
per week.
< * * * *
“ Com ”: Practically the first newspaper man was the
Court Fool. Kings and wealthy men in the pre-news*
paper days so longed for an occasional comic paragraph
or satirical criticism on a friend, containing a biting
truth, that they engaged a salaried jester to fill the bill.
What a fine career George Reid missed though the
miserable accident of not being born at the time of the
Plantagenets !

English politicians now abjure precedent and submit
to the “ interview.” Mr. Chamberlain was interviewed
by several newspapers on his return from S’Africa. The
course that used to be adopted by a public man wishing
jJ’* ve ' rl formation to the press in England was to
address a public meeting or to write a letter to a friend,
who would promptly (as instructed) pass it on to the
press.

_ “ J.E.W.” : For its size Perth (W.A.) is the most paper-
ridden town in the Comm on wealth. It staggers along
under the weight of four dailies (one more than Mel-
bourne), half-a-dozen weeklies (one sixpenny, and the
rest threepennies), and a couple of sectarian prints, and,
as if these were not enough, some prize idiot is alleged to
be bringing out. a threepenny monthly. Said that at
least two are beginning to feel the shoe pinch, and no
wonder. [Perth may be the most paper-ridden town in
the Commonwealth, but Christchurch (M.L.) holds the
Australasian record, or very near it. Population about
67,000 ; four daily papers, seven weeklies, one fortnightly,
five monthlies, total 17. Ed. 8.l
* * * *
There was turmoil in an illustrated weekly office in an
Australian capital a while back. The M’Phairson of Loch
Fairgus, or something of the sort, had sent in his photo.
for publication on being elected, for the year, to the
position of Hereditary Grand Chief of the Phillibegs or
some kindred society. The M’Phairson was a big adver-
tiser in the daily, so the weekly felt bound to publish the
portrait of the proprietor of the Caledonian stores, as
Hereditary Grand Chief hereinbefore described, wearing
kilts and sporran and Skein Dhu and other weird articles
of clothing. The day of publication the office was filled
with furious M’Phairsons and M’Tavishes and M’Clay-
mores and other wild and hairy beings, for the title under
the honored chief’s portrait was “Mr. Macpherson in
fancy dress.”

Compositors who have been City of London apprentices
are entitled at all times to wear a sword within the City.
They don’t avail themselves of the privilege, but the
quaint old foolishness is regarded as an honorable tradi-
tion in the Inky Way.
Robert Hichen’s latest book, “Felix," describes a
supposititious School for Journalists." The founder of
this school, Mr. Samuel Collingbridge, known to his
young lions as “ Old Sam,” has a number of ambitious
P aj ' £1°0 a year, in return for
which he gives them the benefit of his fatherly advice,
the use of his office, and plenty of ink—“he has noticed
most young men prefer to choose their own paper ’’—and
he graciously allows them to do so. His opening instruc-
tions arefull of sound, but sordid, wisdom. To Felix he
. In .f^ der to be a successful journalist, you
must be either an observant person or an accom-
plished liar. I was both, and made £2000 a year.
The clever journalist must also be an alarmist, one well
able to play upon the fears of his country. I know a
“ adea fortune out of ‘the downfall of
England. The downfall of England is his speciality, but
1 don t recommend my pupils to specialise. You should
study every kind of type, from the palace to the butcher’s
PTj a ! 1 - ?°J t8 of sensations. If you were being
thrashed which Heaven forbid 1—study your sensations.
What is pain ? What is humiliation f What is cowardice ?
If you are being embraced by a Countess who wishes you
t° praise her salon , or by a prima donna who wishes you
to puff up her voice, ask yourself why does the meeting
of lips give pleasure ? You must analyse everyone and
everything. Analyse me. Analyse yourself. Analyse
the girl you are making real love to. Analyse the girl
you are merely fooling. Choose striking forms of expres-
sion. Never be tame. In journalism it is not acrime to be
vulgar. Far from it. But to be tame is a crime. Above all,
be sensational in everything. By sensationalism I don’t
mean blazing accounts of murders and arson, rape and
robbery. Many religious papers exist by being sensa-
tional about ritualism, atheism, bishops, Jesuits, and the
” i Jf ermen t.the public passions, then lull them to
rest. The public likes it. Then again the public likes
humbug. Give the public what it likes. Give it humbug,
lots of humbug. The humbug of England’s mission of
spreading light through the world’always takes well.
Study the art of humbug in the speeches of our great
political leaders. Study women closely ; they are hum-
bugs by nature, often very accomplished ones indeed 1 ”

. Toronto Globe publishes, from Burris Gahan, a very
interesting (though in places somewhat incorrect) sketch
of Australian newspapers from a Canadian point of view.
The morning papers strike him as excellent in their com-
ment columns, but poor in their news. “ Newspaper
reports here can usually be trusted, but they are often
very dry and dull. The Australian reporter, however, is
never so aggravating as when he starts fine writing. He
has not yet learned thoroughly the cardinal rule of sum-
marising the main points of his story in his opening sen-
tences. . . . All the press cables come direct from
London, even if they report happenings in China, South
Africa, or America. Twenty of the leading papers in the
Commonwealth have arranged a cable service of their
°wn. The only variation of the cables as they appear in
the two Sydney morning contemporaries is when one or
the other makes a glaring blunder in interpreting the
brief. The news received is very meagre, though judi-
cious padding makes it assume respectable proportions.
... If this powerful press of Australia has an adequate
representative it is Mr. l)avid Syme, the proprietor of the
Melbourne Age. He may be taken as a type of all that
is strongest, if not of all that is best, in Australian jour-
nalism. Locally he is known as ‘ David King.’ . . .
As a newspaper man he is remarkable for his organising
rather than for his literary ability. Although his name
appears on the title page of a volume in praise of Protec-
tion, no one has ever accused him of being its literal
author. ... It is remarkable that the evening papers
should be so poor. They cannot compare at all with the
evening papers of Toronto and Montreal. Their news is
scanty, their style is stiff and sombre, their editorial tone
is either dull or flippant, and their influence is slight.
. . . But the worthlessness of the evening press is
more than counterbalanced by the splendid char-
acter of the great illustrated weeklies. They are a
journalistic development peculiar to Australia
It would be well for Australia if the Australasian could
be called its national paper, but I fear that title must go
to The Bulletin. The Bulletin is one of the cleverest
papers published, but its cleverness is used unworthily.
It is the pride of Australasians, hut its popularity is not
as complimentary to the good taste as it is to the intelli-
gence of the people. Its tone is too often flippant, in-
delicate, cynical and irreligious. When it can be serious,
its independence and fearlessness are admirable. The
unpopular cause is always sure of an advocate here.”

“Polstan”: According to “ Ku-KIux ”
(B. 20/6/’03) booking clerks on the Victorian
railways have a lot to do. Don’t doubt it. They
have. But when “Ku-Klux” is at Spencer-
street he should go into the Enquiry Office and
look on for a minute or two at Bent’s overworked
official there. Last week I found the officer m
charge answering two telephones and hurling
answers to frenzied queries at the seething mob in
the office at the same time. He is said to be
marvellously correct and never to give a wrong or
an insufficient reply, which is to he wondered at
considering the manner in which he is besieged
and the silly-ox questions he is asked. There is
need for three men with the whole time-table in
their heads and with a large stock of concentrated
essence of Job in that office.
An official in uniform politely pointed out
that the front tram was a “through one.” The
bandy-legged passenger asked him if his grand-
mother was a monkey, and got on board. He was
clean shaven, and his face would have scared a
hangman. The tram started, and after we had
climbed the side of the hill we whizzed along at a
great rate. Suddenly the circular-legged passenger
pressed the little electric bell, but the tram
whizzed on heedlessly. “ Yer on a through tram,
boss ! ” said an obliging individual. “ Why don’t
yer tell me something I don’t know?” said the
Owner of the Countenance. “ Well, watcher
ringin’ ther bell for?” retorted the other. “He
won’t stop till he gets to his point!” “Oh, yes,
he will, mister; he’s goin’ ter stop here, right
now ! ” And the person with a hidden crime for
a face seized the rope and removed the overhead
arm from the wire. In a moment the tram was
plunged in darkness and stopped with a bump.
The ponductor^dropped off from .somewhere in
front and came running hack, but, by the time he
had overcome his indignation and was able to
comprehend what had happened, the person with
the space between his legs was a mere shadow
under a distant public house lamp.
They were inflicting the customary pre-
sentation on a telegraph-operator on the eve of
his departure from a M.L. township, and after
the leading lights had plastered him with much
butter, the message-boy rose and spoke at great
length, and incidentally remarked that “ he was
always well satisfied with the way the operator
had done his duty” !
“Morton”: Bulletin, 23rd June, says
re Henry Browne Hayes :
Hayes, nevertheless, opened the lodge on 16th May,
1803, and was deported to Van Diemen’s Land for his
rouble.
Can anybody tell us what became of Hayes after
1803? I’ve gone through files of old papers,
Gazettes and documents of all sorts. The Vande*
monian records of that time are—well, where are
they ? Besides, Vandemonia is hyper-sensitively
intolerant of the man who inquires after its grand-
father. Some time ago, I found this (Notes and
Queries, 3rd S. III., 284):
Towards the close o Hast century, Sir Henry Hayes
abducted a Quaker damsel; and after a day or two’s
detention, restored her to her parents, intact, and
absconded. So she outlawed him ; and a Government
reward was gazetted for his apprehension, which she, by
advertisement, doubled, adding an extract from Black-
stone, that “ it was lawful for any man to kill an outlaw.”
He was taken, tried, convicted, and sent to death. Her
vindictiveness had some share, I suppose, in his reprieve,
and he was sent to Botany Bay, whence, after several
years, he was permitted to return for the purpose of
taking his then grown-up family back with him to
Sydney. About 1816 I saw him in London, just before
his return thither, where he had established a brewery,
and after a while died, a wealthy man.
The vindictiveness of demure Mary Pike is not
inexplicable. Hayes stole her, and then handed
her hack in a cold, respectable way, as a disap-
pointing article. Anyhow, the Knight flashes
picturesquely across our history, and disappears
completely. Why did he disappear, and when ’
The Tageblatt (Berlin) recently asked the
opinions of its readers as to the ten most dis-
tinguished men living to-day. And the result is,
to say the least, interesting. Tolstoy headed the
list with 502; then came Mommsen, the historian
(whom the accurate Webster’s Die., 1894 edition,
says died 1892!), with only 10 behind ; and the
next eight in the following order : Marconi 445,
Ibsen 425, Edison 368, Nansen 270, Rontgen 264,
Nausel (the artist) 240, Koch 228, with—oh,
horror ! —Emperor Bill last with 203. Amongst
those who received between 100 and 200 votes
were Bjornson, Gorky, Chamberlain, Hauptman
and Max Klinger (painter and sculptor). Those
with less than 100 included painter Laubach and
Pierpont Morgan (!); those with less than 50,
Kruger, Pope Leo and Richard Strauss. Among
those who staggered under the weight of one
solitary vote was Mark Twain. A similar ballot
taken, say, by London Times would, it is safe
to say, turn out something like this : King Ned,
Chamberlain, Roberts, Campbell-Bannerman,
Kipling, teaman Lipton (if his yacht wins the
’Murkan Cup), Ranji, Dan Leno, Prince of Wales,
the owner of the next Derby winner (if the
favorite) or Irvine.
“ R.” : We were smoking in the parlor of
the Insane Emu publiebouse, when my friend, the
chemist, said, “You talk about worry ! Why, if
you were in my line you’d he grey in a week. An
instance: Took down the cocaine bottle once to
make up a prescription ; used it, and returned it
to shelf. Stood looking after the customer as he
left the shop, and, on turning round, there was
the strychnine bottle by my elbow. I was stunned,
but, shaking myself up a bit, sang out to my
assistant. ‘ Say, Bob! have you been using strych-
nine ? ’ ‘Yes, sir ; just used it; I’ll put it back in
a second.’ I breathed again ; hut felt as sick as if
I had taken an emetic. But very often there’s no
explanation like that, and then you just wonder
what you put in the prescription, There’s
nothing on earth so fiendish as the poison bottle.
It climbs down from the shelf on its own, and
stands beside you just to make you think you’ve
been using it by mistake, and then you stop
breathing for lialf-an-hour with the shock. But
the bottle’s got no sense. It’ll come down just
the same way if it’s empty.”

Dear Bulletin, — The present sea-serpent
is a decadent. Compared with former sea-
pents, he is a mild and inoffensive reptile that a
child might play with. Here is an item from the
United Service Journal of 1831:—
Extract from the log-boolc of the ship Douglas : “ Sailed
May 3rd, from Curacoa. May 6th, at 3 p.m., in lat. 35'
long. 68°40, made, as we supposed, a vessel bottom up’
about five or six miles distant; proceeded within 40ft. of
the object, which appeared in the form of a turtle ; its
height above water 10 or 12ft.; in length 25 or 30ft., and
in breadth 12ft., with oars or flappers, one on each side,
12 or 15ft. in length, one-third of the way from his taii
forward, and one on each side near his tail 5ft. long.
The tail, 20 to 25ft. long, had a large lion face with large
eyes. The shell or body looked like a clinker-built boat
of 25 or 30 tons, bottom up. There were some large
branches on him. This animal was standing south-east,
and in the course of Bermuda, and his velocity about two
knots per hour. A vessel running foul of this monster
might be much injured.”
Now, this is something like a sea-serpent. The
accurate measurements alone would enforce belief,
not to mention the tail with a large lion face.—
Ernest Favenc.


In four charges of robbing churches in and
about Sydney lately, the charge-sheet said
nothing about “ sacrilege.” And the court found
the thief guilty of “larceny.” A few years ago
the theft of a knee-pad out of a church was called
“ sacrilege,” and involved a ten-years sentence-
one year for the theft and nine years for desecrat-
ing Parson Howler’s edifice.
“ G.M.R.” : I enter the rich grandfather
of a baronet in the mean man competition. In
the early fifties a pal of mine was gaining experi-
ence on one of the many stations owned by said
millionaire. A likely buyer for the run having
turned up one day, grandfather and pal set out on
a tour of inspection with him. This was got
through in due time, and, as a final, an adjourn-
ment was made to the men’s hut, with a view of
making a valuation of it and its contents. These
consisted principally of a black, greasy-looking
frying-pan, which was knocked down at Is. Some
dripping, however, collected on one side of the
pan, caught the hawk eye of grandfather, who
firmly declared the sale off unless the buyer
sprung another Id., as he reckoned the dripping
was worth it
From Perth (W.A.) Sunday Times :
Proportion sum for Mr. Commissioner Roe, who in
Perth recently gave a half-starved man two years for
stealing a shilling, and immediately afterwards awarded
six months to a beast who had indecently assaulted a
five-year-old girl:—
A shilling equals two years’ gaol.
A little girl’s purity equals six months.
How many criminal assaults equal a tram fare ?
An occurrence like that in, say, N.S.W. wouldn’t
strike anyone as odd, however—N.S.W. Has,
before now, witnessed the barbarity-cum-bathos of
one of its judges first serving out three years’ gaol
to an old man for stealing a ninepenny tin of
baking powder, and then o0 ,n,encing to three
months’ imprisonment a Chow who had murdered
a white youngster.

PEARS
Sandy’s Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Parkers were friends of Sandy’s and Kate's,
and lived in town, where they were mostly out of
work. Sometimes Sandy sent them a bag of
potatoes; and once Parker sent Sandy a case of
fruit, which the railway people enjoyed.
Jimmy Parker came to Sandy’s to spend a
holiday. Jimmy was about 14, and had never
been to the bush. He had often longed to sed it,
though; and to become acquainted with cattle
and kangaroos was his ambition. Sandy met him
at the railway and took him to the selection in
the dray. Jimmy was delighted to see so much
country, and all the way along nursed a small
rifle he brought with him.
“How ’re y’ on kang’roos?” Sandy asked,
eyeing the weapon. Jimmy said he didn’t know.
“Never had a shot at any.”
Sandy told him there were thousands round his
place, and boasted of the size and ferocity of
them. Jimmy drew deep breaths, and his eyes
rolled excitedly.
“ How do you manage,” he asked, “ when an
old man tackles you ? ”
“ Them’s one of th’ things y’ ’ve got t’ look out
f r,” Sandy answered lightly, giving the old mare
the double of the reins, hard, on the rump, and
making her go a lot slower. “The las’ time one
tackled me,” he went on, “I just took ’im be th’
throat with me left hand till I got out me knife ;
then I stuck it inter ’im an’ ripped ’im right up
t’ th’ bloomin’ mouth, an’ let a heap o’ green grass
outer ’im.”
Jimmy shuddered, and suddenly discovered he
had left his pocket-knife at home.
“ Well, while y’r up ’ere,” Sandy said advisedly,
“you had better borrow mine whenever y’ go
out.”
Silence for about a mile.
Going down the Gap, near Talty’s, a great noise
suddenly broke out. There was wild commotion
in the gully.
“Hello!” Sandy said, stopping the dray;
‘‘ they’re after somethin’.” And he stared about.
“There it comes!” he cried; “look out! . . .
Get y’r rifle.”
A tired, miserable marsupial floundered towards
them pursued by three of the Talty girls and an
old blind dog.
“Sool ’im, sool’im!” the girls were shouting,
and the blind dog sooled in moderation.
Sandy jumped off the dray and heaved a waddy
vainly at the passing ’roo, then joined in the
chase. But Jimmy sat still and grinned and
wondered. A ’roo hunt was new to Jimmy.
When the dray arrived at the selection Kate
met it at the rails and nearly shook Jimmy’s hand
off. Kate always got excited when visitors came.
She commented on his altered appearance, asked
him how his mother was and how his father was
keeping, and if the baby was walking yet, and
why Julia, his sister, didn’t come, and finished
by inquiring how he liked Sleepy Creek. Then
she foraged in the dray for things Sandy had
brought from the store.
“ What about tea? Didn’t y’ bring teal ” she
cried.
Sandy threw down a new rake he had purchased
and stared sheepishly.
“ Clean f’got it ! ” he gasped.
“Well, y’ re a thing” Kate snapped, “ why it
was what we wanted most, an’ I told y’ not t’
f’get it! ” Kate nearly cried.
“Ain’t y’ got noneat all?” Sandy murmured.
Jimmy felt he was in the way, and strolled
over to the garden.
“Got none?” Kate snorted. “Of course I
ain’t.”
Sandy looked very dejected.
“Y’ didn’t f’get y’r old rotten t’bacca, I
know ?”
Sandy made no remark.
“Oh, yer mic/hter thought of it!” reproach-
fully. Then she went inside and slipped out the
other door, and went off to Mrs. Hogan’s as fast
as she could run. Kate was a great one for tea.
Jimmy removed his hat at the door, and
smoothed his hair with his hand. Sandy kept
his on.
. “Y’ know me Uncle?” Sandy said, introduc-
ing Jimmy.
Uncle grinned and leaned round to shake hands
without getting up, and the pumpkin he was
sitting on at the fire rolled, and he slipped off.
Sandy laughed. Uncle recovered himself and sat
staring into Jimmy’s face, as if he was a kanga-
roo, and made him uneasy.
“Pull y’r boxes up,” Sandy said when tea was
ready, and dumped his own down at the head of
the table.
They all sat round. Sandy piled enough corned
beef and. potatoes and pumpkin on Jimmy’s plate
to last him a week, and worried him fill through"
the meal to take more. And when he refused a
third cup of tea Kate became alarmed about his
health. She was sure he was ill, and suggested
he take a dose of oil or something and go to bed
early. But Jimmy shook his head and smiled.
After tea Sandy proposed a “bit o’ ’possum
shootin’, ’’ and he and Jimmy got ready and went
out. They mooned about, falling over logs and
fouling brambles and limbs that nearly poked
their eyes out, until the moon went down, but
didn’t see a solitary ’possum—saw nothing to
blaze at at all.
.“D funny!” Sandy mused. “Any other
night they’d be thick.”
They returned to the house.
“There’s a bed made for Jimmy at the fire,”
Kate called out from beneath the blankets ; “ an’
there’s some bags at th’ foot for him to throw on
hisself in th’ night if he feels cold.”
Jimmy assured Sandy he would be comfortable,
and turned in.
When lie woke in the morning the fire was
made, and Kate was skipping in and out getting
breakfast ready. Jimmy wished to rise and be
about. But Kate was in the road. He watched
her movements closely, and when she went out
the door to yell to Sandy, who was milking at the
yard, that, breakfast was ready, he jumped out,
grabbed his trousers and faoed the fire. Jimmy
never in his life before was in such haste so early
in the morning. He got one leg in and was
dancing about on it, balancing himself and kick-
ing and fumbling with the other when Kate
returned. Jimmy wasn’t a precocious youth—he
was sensitive and shy, and Kate regarded him
more as a son than anything else. She took
no notice of him, but went straight to the fire to
make the tea. Jimmy faced the other way then,
and tangled himself up and fell on the bed. Then
he snatched the blanket and covered himself
again ; he turned red, too, and was in distress.
Kate made the tea, and turned to the table and
commenced to hum a tune. After a while Jimmy
sat up and fumbled with the b’anket; and when
at last he stood on his feet his trousers were on.
After breakfast Jimmy helped Sandy to yoke
the horses; then took the rifle and went in search
of kangaroos. Sandy directed him to a spot
where he would find them in millions.
Jimmy tramped round and round the paddock,
and hunted about till the rifle felt heavy as lead
and the skin came off his heels, but saw nothing
to fire at, save two little brown birds and a jew-
lizard. He returned, and directed his steps to
where Sandy was running about behind two
horses and a plough, and swearing. Sandy was
surprised to hear there were no kangaroos about;
he couldn’t understand it. For a second or two
he stood, scanning the tree-tops waving over the
wire fenc< § '
“ Gen’a.iy a bear or two about here, he said,
scraping the dust off himself. “Don’t seem to
be any ter day, though.” • ■
Jimmy said it didn’t matter, and he looked as
if he spoke the truth. Jimmy was getting tired,
of kangaroo shooting—tired of the bush alto-
gether,
“Have a go at drivin’?” Sandy suggested,
cheerfully; “leave y’r rifle here, and take the
reins for a while, and see how y’ get on.”
Jimmy said he didn’t mind trying—but he
didn’t say it with any enthusiasm ; and in a
weary sort of way stood up and took the reins.
Sandy yelled at the horses ; they woke up and
made a rush at different intervals. The plough
jumped, stopped, jumped again, then scratched
along after then. Jimmy tried to keep up with
it—he limped, stumbled, jumped over some
clumps of burr, and tripped against dry corn-
stalks that hadn’t been cut. Sandy continued to
yell at the horses, struggling all the time to keep
his footing. A withered pumpkin vine that
seemed to be growing all over the paddock,, with
numerous small pumpkins dangling to it, got
twisted round one of Jimmy’s legs. Jimmy
kicked frantically to free himself of it, and fell
down.
The horses dragged him along till he got up
again. Sandy didn’t notice him ; he was battling
hard to keep the plough in.
“To y’!— to y’! ” Sandy called when the black
mare left the furrow. Jimmy pulled the wrong
rein. “Damn it!” Sandy shouted, losing his
temper—“ to y’.” Jimmy pulled the wrong rein
some more ; then Sandy threw the handles down
savagely, and cried : “Blarst it, what’s th’ good
o’ that ? ” and snatched the reins from Jimmy and
nearly dragged the heads off the horses.
A lump came into Jimmy’s throat, and while
the spade was falling heavily on the rumps of the
horses he stole away, feeling mistaken and disap-
pointed in Sandy, the friend of his father. Near
the house he met Kate coming along with a can
of tea and some scones and butter for Sandy’s
“eleven o’clock.” Kate was red with the heat,
and the haste she was making, but was cheerful as
a book agent. She pressed Jimmy to return to
the plough and have some tea. But Jimmy wasn’t
hungry, and said he would sooner “mooch about
a bit.” Kate showed him where he would find
Uncle. “He’s always there,” she added.
“Nothing’ll shift him 1 ”
Jimmy went to the house and put the rifle
away; then sat in the shade of the verandah and
felt lost and lonely. Not a soul about; scarcely
a sound audible ! Jimmy wondered how any one
could live in such a place, and began to think of
home and the town. Some fowls and a few
turkeys came round, stretched their necks and
took side-glances at him, then moved away again.
Jimmy rose and went in search of Uncle. He
found him snoring in the “ retreat?’—an old tent
patched with rag and bagging, standing some
distance from the house. Sandy sometimes used
it as a store-room. At the sound of Jimmy’s
voice Uncle woke up with a start, but when he
saw it wasn’t a policeman or a bushranger he drew
his legs up to give him room to sit on the corner of
the bunk, and settling himself went calmly off to
sleep again. Jimmy stared hard at Uncle, then at
a cat slumbering on top of a bag of flour near his
head, which was all the tent contained. Large
flies buzzed about like bees, and Jimmy felt lonelier
than before.
“How far is it from here to the train?” he
asked.
No answer. Uncle was fast asleep.
Jimmy touched him on the bare foot. “ Hey,”
he said, “ how far ”
Whip-cracks and men’s voices shouting sounded
close to the tent. Jimmy rushed out. Two
horsemen were in the grass-paddock wheeling
a wild-looking, black bull toward Sandy’s yard.
Foam was flowing from the bull’s nostrils and
mouth, and his blood was fairly up.
- “Look out there,” the men shouted when
Jimmy showed himself. “Look out \. . Keep
inside.”
Jimmy sheltered himself behind a large tree,
and got excited. The bull saw the tent, and
instead of taking fright went straight at it with
his head down. Jimmy clung to the tree and
yelled a warning to Uncle. But Uncle was a
hard man to shift. The bull went right through
the tent and levelled it to the ground. The cat
got a great start. It raced in three different
directions before remembering where the house
lay; then to see it light out! But there was
no word of Uncle. Jimmy nearly fainted. The
horsemen shouted hilariously, but they had no
idea the tent was inhabited. The bag of flour
attracted the bull’s attention ; he took it for an
enemy, and dug his horns into it, rooted it
about, and bellowed and breathed into it.
The flour clung to his moist nose and
hide, and went into dough on him, and
turned him white. Meanwhile, Uncle had been
lying low, but the bull tramped on him, and he
thought it time then to shift. On hands and
knees he emerged from the debris, and crawled
some yards, then, like a rat forsaking a log, he
made off as fast as terror and tender feet would
allow in the direction of the house. The bull
caught sight of Uncle, and, despite all attempts
of the horsemen to frustrate him, went headlong
in pursuit. Uncle had 50 yards to cover to reach
the house; the bull had 100 yards to go. Talk
about excitement! Neither Jimmy nor the horse-
men could speak. They stared and trembled.
The bull rattled over the ground like a racehorse
in a straight. You couldn’t hear Uncle moving,
but he got a lot out of his arms and head.
“ He’ll have, him ! ” one of the men murmured,
apprehensively; '‘he will . . . he .”
“ Done it! ” Jimmy burst out, excitedly, just
as Uncle threw himself in the back door, which,
happily, was wide open. Then, almost instantly,
there was a loud crash, and the bull and the slabs
and the roof of Sandy’s new skillion were wildly
mixed up, and the animal was plunging madly
about inside with groceries on his horns.
For a fortnight after, Jimmy was in high spirits,
and never thought of home once.

RHEUMATISM
Is a form of Blood Poisoning. You will never have Rheu«
matism if your Kidneys are well. For Healthy Kidneys
filter from the Blood all the Rheumatic and other
Poisons that cause Disease. Keep the Kidneys well,
■ and they will keep you well.
Rheumatism is not a
disease in itself; it is but
the symptom of a disease.
It is caused by an acid in
the blood, called uric acid,
of which there is enough
formed every twenty-four
hours to cause certain death
if not removed.
Nature has provided the
kidneys to take this acid
out of the system, and they
are the only means by which
it can be removed. As the
blood passes through the
kidneys it is filtered, and
the acids and poisons are
passed out of the system
along with the surplus
water.
But when the kidneys
get sick they do not do
this work properly, and
some of this acid stops in
the system, causing endless
pain and suffering. It will
crystallise somewhere, and
when it crystallises in the
muscles, the little, sharp,
jagged crystals cut and tear
the flesh, causing great pain.

That is muscular rheumatism.
They may crystallise in the joints, and that is the cause of gout; or in
the kidneys or bladder, causing stone in bladder or kidney, gravel, sedi-
ment, &c.
The acid will penetrate every nerve and fibre of the body, causing
nervousness, headache, sleeplessness, and interfering with the valves of the
heart; it will affect the eyes, and will cause a general weakness and fagged
feeling, bloating of limbs, &c.
The whole health of your body therefore depends on keeping your kidneys
well. There can be no rheumatism with healthy kidneys, no nervousness, no
headaches, no bad blood. Keep the kidneys well, so they may filter al
impurities from the system, and they will keep you well.

kidneys require to properly filter the blood. They heal the kidneys’ delicate
membranes, and, best of all, they are for th.e kidneys alone.
They do not act on the bowels, as so many medicines do.
SYDNEY PROOF.
Mr. James Fotheringham, Watson’s Bay, late 6th Imperial Bushmen,
writes: “ Whilst in South Africa with the 6th Imperial Bushmen, through
constant exposure to all sorts of weather, sleeping in wet clothes, etc., I got
a very severe attack of lumbago. I was so bad that I could not get up off the
ground—let alone mount my horse. Two military doctors had ago at me.
One blistered my back until all the skin was off; and the other gave me
medicine—but neither of them did me any good. Fortunately a comrade
came along with a box of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, and before I had
finished the box I was as right as ever I was in my life. Since then I always
keep a box of the pills handy, in case of the lumbago returning; but, so far, I
am glad to say, I have had no necessity to take them. You may make what-
ever use you like of this, as I consider Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills a boon
to humanity.”

by Foster-McClellan Co., 76 Pitt-street, Sydney.

that he has remained free of the piles and
itchiness of the skin ever since. It is simply
wonderful the way the ointment acted. We
have had letters from different parts of the
State asking if it is correct, and we have
replied to each letter advising the use of Doan’s
Ointment.”
It cannot be repeated too often that Doan’s
Ointment will cure itching piles. It will cure
them absolutely; but do not take the manu-
facturer’s word for this. Ask Mr. Gannon.
He knows, for it cured him ; and he lives here
in Sydney. What proof could be better or
stronger ?
Doan’s Ointment is sold by all chemists and
storekeepers at 2s. 9d. per pot, or will be
posted on receipt of price by Foster-McClellan
Co., 76 Pitt-street, Sydney.
But be sure it is DOAN’S. —Advt.
“Aux Pauvres Diables!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If over you happen to pay a
Short visit, when down in the Isles,
To the polychrome town of Noumea
Whereßeauty—bright, black and brown—smiles,
And you feel a desire for some brandy,
Or absinthe, or whisky, or gin
In a street, to the market close handy,
You will notice the Poor Devils’ Inn.
It is not a structure as stately
As some that in Sydney you know,
And if about style you are greatly
Concerned, it is not comme il faut;
Its doors are dirt-brown; its facade is
Of liver-red stucco, and tin ;
Yet the liquor you get not so bad is,
In the same little Poor Devils’ Inn,
The haughty imported officials,
The gendarme with pointed moustache,
Have not on its slate their initials
, (Its motto is French for “Spot Cash”).
But ever the humble and lowly
May fill themselves up to the chin
Very cheaply, and find themselves wholly
At their ease in the Poor Devils’ Inn.
The place had for me a strange glamor;
Its windows did wickedly wink ;
And, though I was weak in French grammar,
I knew how to ask for a drink.
’Twas vain to put airs on or graces ;
Its genial experienced grin
■Said, plainer than words—“ Here your place is,
Bon gars, in the Poor Devils’ Inn ! ”
Behold me, then, sitting, and drinking
Green absinthe and syrup of gum,
And feeling quite Gallic, yet thinking
I would have much rather had rum.
But, ventrebleu ! one must in foreign
Lands di ink their drinks, credit to win—
I spent very nearly a florin
Ere I quitted the Poor Devils’ Inn.
And I saw, though it seems like a fable,
A gentleman shabby, yet fine,
Who hammered his heels on the table
Demanding a bottle of wine.
His face had grown harder and thinner—
Who was he that raised such a din ?
Frangois Villon, as I am a sinner,
At home in the Poor Devils’ Inn !
What brought back this mad rogue from Hades,
Whence seldom a ghost comes at call ?
He said he was tired of dead ladies—
Of Lais, and Thais, and all—
So being of that sort whose star is
A guide that leads surely to Sin,
He returned to pick purses in Paris,
And brought up at the Poor Devils’ Inn.
He showed me a ballad he’d written
About a bright-eyed popinee,
By the charms of a gendarme death-smitten—
He’s sold it to Monsieur Puget,
Who printed it in his smart paper,
And Frangois the coin made to spin,
And cut up the devil’s own caper,
With his friends, in the Poor Devils’ Inn.
I thought it was kindly and witty
To give to the cafi this name,
Suggestive of jovial pity,
And one more last chance at the game.
And I hope, when a country still stranger
I go to, new life to begin,
By the grace of the Gracious Arranger,
I shall find out a Poor Devils’ Inn.
N.o.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Murphy’s Barmaid.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ll never forget the first bit of skirt Murphy got
out at the Water Hole. She was a pearl. She’d
take a man’s eye out an’ tell ’im he looked better
without it.
There was only one pub. there then. That was
Murphy’s, and he did all right too, because he
had no expense at all, only just a bit of tucker for
a drunken old bloke who did the slushying. We
had to take our gold into Lawlers in those days.
There was no bank on the Hole then. One time
a few of the boys were in with their bit of metal,
and came back with a yarn about all the pubs,
getting barmaids up, and said the place was going
ahead like wildfire. Well, we’d been out-back
for ages, and had never seen a white woman in
these parts, so this made us hanker after a sight
of one, and we had a bit of a meeting and reckoned
Murphy would have to get one up.
Bill Jones said there was a slashing little girl
at the Royal in Lawlers who would come out,
and he wanted to go in there and then and fetch
her. Murphy was willing too. He was a mean
sort of bloke, and thought to save her fare up;
you see, Bill had a buckboard and pair. But we
told them both that we weren’t taking any second-
hand girls from Lawlers, and Murphy would have
to get something bang up-to-date from below.
After a lot of arguing he sent down to one of
those registry offices for a girl, and one day he
got a letter to say that Miss Devereux was start-
ing the following day, and would be out about a
week after the letter.
You never saw such a week as that was on the
Hole. Saturday was mail day then. The coach
was only a buckboard, with room for one passen-
ger. Well, this Saturday night we got the news;
all hands got drunk, and that spree lasted until
Wednesday. Then Thompson, who ran the store,
said it was up to all hands to get sober for the
lady’s arrival.
Thompson also gave it out that all girls liked
white clothes on men; and, therefore, all hands
made a bee-line for the store to get on early in
the clothing department, and bought white moles
as fast as Thompson could hand them out, to say
nothing of shirts, hats, and boots galore.
We all sobered up before Saturday, and all
hands were at the pub. a couple of hours before
the coach arrived, putting in the time swapping
lies and playing two-up. It was a hot, dusty day,
and there’s always six inches of red dust in front
of Murphy’s, except after rain, when it’s red mud.
We were getting just about full of waiting, when
someone spotted the dust rising a mile down the
road, and a few minutes later the coach rolled up,
and we gave three cheers when we saw the girl
was there all right.
It was like a football match, the rush to help
her off the coach, and some of the moles were
pretty well soiled when it was over. She wasn’t
half bad-looking—a little woman with fair hair ;
but, of course, what with the long journey and
dusty road she wasn’t looking at her top just then.
After she had a wash and a change she came
out, and Murphy introduced us all round, and
we had several rounds of drinks, but she stuck to
soft stuff. Then all of a sudden she said she’d
lost her diamond-ring, and several volunteers
turned her room upside down looking for it, but
it wasn’t there. She suggested it might have
slipped off in the crowd as she got off the coach,
and straightaway went all hands trying to “speck”
it in the red dust, but it was no go. At last
Bill Jones went and got a shovel and sieve and we
started putting the whole roadway through, but
after we had been at it until sundown and were
all the color of the road from head to foot, we
gave it best. She started to cry then and said,
after thanking us for our trouble, that she must
have lost it on the road up. We couldn’t stand
her crying, so Jack Skinner passed the hat round
to buy her another, and got £23. We were all
doing well at the time. She was awfully pleased,
and said we were a dear lot of boys.
About a week after she asked me to get her a
bit of local gold. She would buy some diamonds,
and wanted to have them set in some of our gold.
I thought it was rather a nice idea, and showed
her a few pieces of alluvial I had; the alluvial
was done then, but we all had a few fancy bits.
She picked out a piece and said another one
would make a nice brooch—and, like a flat, I gave
her the two.
After she left the field we compared notes, and
I found she’d done the same thing to 44 other
fellows.
I got on to her once quietly about flirting with
other fellows, but she said she had to treat us all
alike when she was behind the bar or she’d lose
her place, and she couldn’t afford that; she had a
mother and sisters to keep. I wanted to marry
her then, and she blushed and half promised. I
suppose she told the 44 other idiots the same thing.
Then one Saturday a man came into town with
bluey up, looking for work. He was dead stiff, he
said in the bar while he was having a drink with
some of us, and when she heard it she took a note
out of her purse and gave it to him, so nice that
we thought she was an angel, and half envied him
for being broke.
There was no work there for wages-men, for
we were all working our own shows. There was
a ten-head mill going there then ;in fact, it had
only just started. A fellow named Langton put
it up for public crushing—an old, second-hand
rattletrap putting through about 70 tons a week.
Langton was the dottiest of the lot on the girl, so
when she asked him to give the poor fellow a job
he said “Certainly,” and put him on feeding,
and things went all right for a month or two.
Two other fellows were going extra strong there,
Jack Skinner, of the “Mississippi,” and Bill
Wilde, of the “ Oriental ” —the two be3t shows on
the field. They used to be the best of friends,
but after the Mississippi crushing, which was ex-
pected to go 30z., only panned out 30dwt., Jack
got the nark on Bill over the girl, and it ended in
a fight.
It ivas a fight, too. They were like a pair of
old women at the game. In the second round
Jack pushed his nose against Bill’s fist and it bled
like a pig, so the referee gave Jack twenty minutes
to stop the bleeding. Bill, who used to stutter
terribly, wanted to know what sort of a fool the
referee was, and said he only should have given
Jack 10 seconds, and, as the referee was only a
little bloke, he apologised and shouted for all
hands, assuring Bill that it wouldn’t occur again.
Jack came up smiling after the 20 minutes was
up, but Bill was too much of a general for him.
Every time he was getting the worst of it he
started arguing with the referee, and as it took
him about ten minutes to say a dozen words, he
used to get his wind every time. Finally he
knocked Jack out.
This on top of the disappointing crushing broke
Jack up, and he scratched for the matrimonial
stakes. In fact there were very few triers
now, for those moles were too much trouble to
wash. Bill and Langton had the lady a good bit
to themselves. Bill’s crushing was just coming
on ; his crowd had about 300 tons at gras3 and
was looking for IOOOoz. clean-up, and he went
down to see the stone through after the mill
had been running about three weeks on it.
Tommy Bates—that was the feeder who came
there stiff —got a letter about this time and said
he had to go below because his old man was very
ill. So he drew his time, and left by the first
coach.
When they cleaned-up it was a repetition of
the Mississippi—went about an ounce less than
they expected. Of course, there was a row ; there
always is in those cases. The battery gets the
blame, even if there’s no gold in the stone to save.
Bill put the question to her on his return from
Lawlers, but she told him she’d heard he was a
professional pug, and she wasn’t going to marry a
Erize-fighter. Langton was informed that he and
is battery had better clear their names of sus-
picion in connection with certain crushings before
he dared speak to her.
Then she picked a row with Murphy and
hopped aboard the first down coach. After she
was gone a key was found in her room, and when
Langton discovered that it opened the safe down
at the battery—the one he kept the amalgam in—
his language could be heard all over the field.
I saw her, the other day, in Kalgoorlie. I was
standing in front of the court when she came out
and shook hands with me, and wanted a tenner to
pay Tommy’s fine, or he’d get into trouble for
belting a John.
I asked who Tommy was, and she smiled like
an angel and told me he was her husband —also
that he was the same Tommy who came up to
Murphy’s dead broke and got the job at Lang-
ton’s battery.
I didn’t lend her the £lO. And all I said
was “ Damn Tommy ! ”
B. A. LEIX.
About the only point in favor of Pope
Booth of the Salrobbery Army is that when the
brethren and sistern of his alleged religion do
quarrel in public they don’t use very dreadful
language. At Summer Hill (Sydney) two of the
sistern got to blows lately, and the worst lan-
guage used only described tbe addressee as “a
dirty old chaff-bag, tied in the middle.” After a
few other complimentary phrases, in which one
sister described the other as an “old thing,”
which is how the young Australienne occasionally
speaks of her mother, one sister smote her mate
with a hymn book.

Beerbohm Tree has been “injuncted” against exhib-
iting on the walls of his Haymarket (London) theatre
the legend, surrounded by electric lights, “ His Majesty’s
Theatre.” Permission had been granted him to cut the
letters on a marble slab in a recess in the wall, but,
instead, he had fixed gilt wooden letters on the slab.
Ihe “head and front of his offending,” however, lay in
his not applying for a license and submitting a design.
In a manner of speaking, he approached the Throne on his
knees when he should have done it on his stummick.
u “ ■ J.A.C.” : Charles Santley, for a very long period
King of English baritones,” completed, a few months
ago, his 69th year. Before entering the vocal pro-
fession he was, like our own beauteous George V.,
a violinist. He studied singing in Milan, Italy, and
while there got so down on his luck that visions of
suicide flitted through his mind. His first operatic
engagement was at a small Milanese theatre, the Santa
Radegonda, where he appeared in the ultra-ungrateful
role oi the Doctor in “ Traviata,” Years afterwards he
SUT H? in “ H Trovatore,” at La Scala, in the same city,
and his rendering of “II Balen ” roused the most critical
audience in the world to enthusiasm. He has been for 4®
years a vocalist, and, besides, has composed several
masses and other sacred pieces and many popular songs.
Touching the little matter of the big theatre hat.
When the local amateurs were frivolling through “Pina-
fore ” at Oamaru (M.L.), a little woman, crowned with an
enormous top-piece, slid in and sat two seats in front of
an angry milliner, obscuring latter’s view. After vainly
endeavoring to induce the wearer to unload, the dress
architect borrowed a stick and tilted the hat to one side.
The performance was repeated as often as the gear was
straightened, and convulsed the house. Upshot was that
the woman with the hat sued her tormentor for assault
and mind anguish, or something, and the beak actually
fined the defendant half-a-sov., with £2 16s. costs ; which
a sympathetic public at once subscribed.
Dion Boucicault’s version of “The Cricket on tha
Hearth,” in which poor old J. L. Toole used to “ star” 30
years ago, is about to be revived and enlarged to meet
the craze for Dickens plays. The dramatist who tries
to improve on Boucicault has a tough contract.
Talking of Jane Hading’s performance in “ Sapho," at
the London Coronet Theatre, a critic says : “ When she
flings her lover’s linen into his trunk, in her hurry to
pack him off, the actress carried the house by storm.
But it only shows how curiously insensible some people
are, that the scene, which was really painful to a degree,
excited a great deal of laughter. Why ? Simply because
the people who laughed at the sight of Jean’s shirts saw
only the shirts and did not see the woman’s bitter passion.
Any article of underclothing is as good as a joke to the
untutored mind. You have only to say ‘ stockings,’ I
believe, to make some people smile.” ’This is true of
English-speaking audiences. The baneful influence of
Mrs. Grundy has given undergarments an “indecent”
character, and jt is the thought of indecency that the
average fool laughs at.
_ “ C.R.” : Re whistling: Years ago a blind girl travelled
Tasmania accompanied by her mother, an old lady who
looked upon her nose when it was red. The girl was an
accomplished mimic, bird-music chiefly claiming her
attention ; and no one, after hearing her clear and correct
rendering of the magpie, Tasmanian jackass, English lin-
net, skylark and canary, could easily forget it. She once
tried her luck in Melbourne. She whistled and the old
lady gathered in the shekels. How much the blind
whistler got for herself I don’t know.

“ F.": Apropos Vincent Wallace and “ Maritana.'
Thirty years ago two houses in Sydney were pointed out
to me as being the identical buildings wherein Wallace
composed “Maritana.” One was on the coiner of Castle*
reagh-street and Brougham-place—now Rowe-street, the
Hotel Australia being on the site. The other was in
Brougham-place. My authority was dear old Johnny
Welsh, mummer and white man (R.1.P.), Sydney native
(born in Brougham-place in the thirties), with a well-
stored, clear memory to the last. Said that Wallace
commenced the score in the latter house and finished it
in the former. There are two of the old Brougham-place
houses still intact. The house at the corner of Castle-
reagh-street and Brougham-place was in the fifties occu-
pied by Dunsmure and Stafford, solicitors, and next door
* Bill ” Nash lived and moved and had his being.
Frances Gill shrieks plaintively from afar off that it is
the duty of The Bulletin, in the causeof art, to summon
Dr. Ludwig Wullner to Australia. “He is the great
German singer, now in his first London season. His voice
is magnificent, and he is such an artist that he makes the
whole matter of his song alive before your eyes.” Well,
the German artist can consider himself summoned.
“ £>.” : The Mutoscope has come to Adelaide, and, after
the bright accounts of the machine in Tiie Bulletin, the
disappointment is so keen that one votes it a mockery, a
delusion and a snare, likewise a sham. We have about
30 machines. You put a penny in the slot, turn a stiff
crank, and presently a mild picture offends the view.
Being of a religious turn of mind, I spent one of my few
pennies on “ The Temptation of St. Anthony.” When the
machine shewed a feeble old ‘‘Sign of the Cross” Christian
groping before the vision of a nude woman, I groaned.
Bad enough to look at when the penny does all the work,
but to turn a crank ! However, a better religious picture
may come along, when it will be a pleasure to whirl the
machine.
When Marie Lloyd was appearing at Adelaide Tivoli,
she and a few other members of the co. one day jour-
neyed to Parkside Lunatic Asylum and gave a perfor-
mance. She sang several songs, and just before she
concluded her turn the applause was deafening. Every
madman shouted something different, and Marie
“ thought they had all gone mad.” At length silence
was restored, and a venerable and respectable lunatic
stood up and acted as spokesman. “ What my friends
wish to intimate,” said he, “is that the young lady
repeats the song in which she wears football costume.”
It was one of Marie’s coster items —the one with the
hilarious striped stockings in it—they wished to encore.
